Iliad Intensive AI Alignment Study Guide

Self-contained edition. Each unit teaches the material inline — definitions, derivations, worked examples, retrieval Q&A, and problems with hidden solutions. External links are tiered: one "if you read one thing" per unit, rest are optional depth. Adapted from the April 2026 Iliad Intensive course materials for solo study without needing the source.

How to use this guide

Read in this order, per unit: prerequisites → pre-reading questions → core exposition → worked example → definitions/results → retrieval Q&A (force yourself to answer before clicking) → problems (write down a solution before revealing) → solo assignment. Optional external links last, and only when a unit genuinely demanded more.

The intended flow per unit (each unit takes 2–5 hours of focused work):

  1. Open the unit, read the punchline + prerequisites. Decide if you have the prerequisites; if not, list what to patch externally.
  2. Skim the pre-reading questions. Don't answer yet. Set them as targets.
  3. Read the core exposition. Stop at each callout box; they answer questions you might be having right at that point.
  4. Work the worked example on paper. Don't read passively. If a derivation is shown step-by-step, hide the next step before you read it.
  5. Do retrieval Q&A. Write your answer first, on paper or in the notes textarea below. Then reveal. If your answer is materially worse than the hidden one, re-read the corresponding exposition section.
  6. Attempt one problem on paper. The time/type pill on each problem tells you what you're getting into. Reveal the solution only after a serious attempt.
  7. Hit the self-check at the bottom. The pre-reading questions appear again — if you can answer them now without scrolling up, the unit landed.
  8. Mark the unit done. Top-right checkbox in the unit header. The progress panel updates.

What the structure assumes:

  • Active recall is the point. Reading without the Q&A is reading, not learning. The hidden answers are the whole pedagogical apparatus.
  • Problems beat reading for technical units. Tagged problems carry a rough time budget (5 min recall / 15 min scenario / 30 min derivation / 1–2 hr open). Pick your battles.
  • "If you read one thing" is real. Each unit's "Going deeper" block flags exactly one must-read link. The rest is depth-on-demand; do not treat the optional list as a reading list.
  • The dependency graph is load-bearing. Skipping out of order is fine if you can name the assumed material; the per-unit prereqs line tells you what.

Built-in tools:

  • Per-unit "done" checkbox (top-right of each opened unit) — saved to this browser.
  • Notes textarea at the bottom of every unit — auto-saves; survives reloads.
  • Floating progress panel (right edge of viewport) — live X / 20 counter, filter input, reset button.
  • Inline references like B.3 or C.2 become clickable jumps after the page loads.
  • The dependency graph below is interactive — click any node to jump to that unit.
  • The Quick reference card near the end is for last-mile recall before a meeting/interview/paper. It is not a substitute for working through the units.
  • Print-friendly — if you print this page (Ctrl/Cmd+P), all hidden answers/solutions/notes become visible automatically.

High-Level Structure

The course is a dependency graph, not a linear sequence. Prerequisites establish worldview and technical base. A defines the alignment problem and current practice. B asks how learning works at all and what theory could constrain it. C studies representations: how to read what models compute and what abstractions they form. D studies agency: RL, ideal agents, and embedded ones. E asks what can be formally guaranteed and where guarantees provably break.

Two routes. Pick one, write the schedule down, check off units as you finish artifacts. The intensive route assumes ~6 focused hours/day; the sustainable route assumes ~10 hours/week.

0. Prerequisites

Worldview calibration plus a technical-readiness checklist. The point is not to teach linear algebra or PyTorch — it is to fix the arguments that motivate the rest of the course, taxonomize the risk landscape, and let you self-diagnose what math/ML to patch before later modules become productive.

0 Prerequisites: Worldview, AI Risk, and Technical Foundations Why advanced AI plausibly matters soon, the four-axis risk taxonomy, and a math/ML self-test before the theory units.
Prerequisites: Undergraduate analysis, linear algebra, probability; comfort reading ML papers; one full SGD-trained network implemented from scratch at some point. No alignment background assumed.

The rest of the course assumes a worldview on which cognitive labor is the strategic bottleneck, no known law caps machine cognition at human level, and the misalignment-by-default argument (instrumental convergence + Goodhart + capabilities-generalize-further-than-alignment) is taken seriously without requiring certainty. The risk landscape decomposes along four axes — misalignment, misuse, power concentration, gradual disempowerment — that shade into each other and rule out the "Skynet" single-node framing. A technical-readiness self-test (linear algebra, information theory, RL, deep learning practice, theory of computation) tells you what to patch externally before Clusters B–E become productive.

Pre-reading questions

  1. Why should you expect "more intelligent" to translate into "more powerful" at all? Where does the argument break?
  2. State instrumental convergence precisely enough that a skeptic could attack a specific premise.
  3. Given the METR task-length doubling trend, what would have to be true for it to imply human-level autonomous R&D within five years — and what observation would falsify the extrapolation?
  4. Distinguish misalignment, misuse, power concentration, and gradual disempowerment. Which two shade into each other most, and why?
  5. Can you state the Bellman optimality equation, the softmax cross-entropy gradient w.r.t. logits, and why VC-dimension fails to bound generalization for overparameterized nets?

Core exposition

Why intelligence is power

The base argument is not "AI will be smart, therefore scary." It is a chain. (1) Cognitive labor is the bottleneck on most strategically relevant processes: R&D, persuasion, planning, coordination, exploitation of code/biology/markets. (2) An agent that can substitute for or exceed human cognitive labor at scale, cheaply, and in parallel, accumulates option value across all of these domains simultaneously. (3) Historically, asymmetries in cognitive capacity (literacy, science, computing) produced large and persistent power gradients between groups holding them and groups not holding them. (4) There is no known law that caps machine cognition at human level; the existence proof (human brains, ~20W, evolved) plus continued compute/data scaling makes "stops conveniently at human" the position that needs defending. Bostrom's "cognitive superpowers" decomposition (strategizing, social manipulation, hacking, technology research, economic productivity) is the canonical taxonomy.

The skeptic's strongest counter is not that intelligence isn't useful but that "general intelligence" as a transferable scalar is a confused abstraction — that real systems will be jagged, that benchmarks overstate progress, and that physical/regulatory/coordination bottlenecks will dominate cognitive ones. The course assumes you take the worldview seriously enough to do the technical work; it does not require you to assign probability 0.9 to doom.

"Intelligence" as used in the chain above is the scalar abstraction — a transferable optimization capacity that integrates planning, learning, and strategic reasoning across domains. The jagged-capability skeptic uses a different notion: a vector of largely independent skills (math, coding, persuasion, long-horizon planning) whose levels can dissociate arbitrarily. Most of this unit's arguments survive the vector view as long as the relevant components (R&D, planning, persuasion) scale together; the disagreement is empirical, not definitional.

Bostrom's "cognitive superpowers" decomposition (Superintelligence, 2014) split capability into strategizing, social manipulation, hacking, technology research, and economic productivity. This was the first sustained attempt to factor "intelligence is power" into named, separately-arguable channels. Modern presentations usually replace its sequential takeoff scenario with the METR-style empirical-trend framing, but the channel decomposition itself is still the cleanest taxonomy of how cognitive capability cashes out as strategic capability.

Instrumental convergence and the basic misalignment argument

Omohundro–Bostrom instrumental convergence: for a wide class of final goals, certain sub-goals are useful — self-preservation, goal-content integrity, resource acquisition, cognitive enhancement, and avoidance of shutdown. The argument is not "all minds want power"; it is conditional: if you have a sufficiently capable optimizer pursuing an objective whose optimum lies outside its current capability set, then these subgoals raise expected achievement of that objective. The formalization via "power-seeking is optimal for most reward functions" (Turner et al., 2021) makes this measure-theoretic: in MDPs with sufficient symmetry, optimal policies for the majority of reward functions (under a natural distribution) prefer states with higher "power" (number of reachable options).

Combine with: (a) reward specification is hard — proxies almost always diverge from intent on the tails (Goodhart); (b) deep nets trained on a proxy can generalize the capability further than the intent (capabilities generalize, alignment doesn't, in Hubinger's slogan); (c) deceptive alignment is a coherent equilibrium for an inner optimizer that models its training process. That is the basic misalignment-by-default argument. It is not airtight — every step has live disputes — but it is the chain you must be able to recite before you can usefully read counterarguments.

Timelines and takeoff

Two questions, often conflated. Timelines: when does AI cross some capability threshold (e.g., automating AI R&D)? Takeoff: conditional on crossing, how fast does it then improve? Soft/slow takeoff = years of gradual capability gain post-threshold, with feedback into policy and economy. Hard/fast takeoff = recursive self-improvement loop where each generation of AI cuts the time to train the next, compressing capability gain into weeks or months.

The current empirical anchor for timelines is task-length scaling. METR (2025) measures the length of software-engineering tasks at which a frontier model has \(\ge 50\%\) success and finds a roughly exponential trend with a doubling time near 4–7 months over 2019–2025. If you accept the extrapolation, projects requiring months of autonomous engineering land in the late 2020s. Takeoff arguments (Davidson, AI 2027) layer on: once AI R&D is partly automated, the compute and labor inputs to the next generation scale faster than the linear-human regime, so the doubling time itself shortens. The fast-takeoff position depends on (i) R&D being the binding constraint, (ii) feedback being tight (no serial bottleneck like fab capacity), (iii) capabilities transferring across the substrate change.

The risk taxonomy

The reductive "Skynet" framing is wrong because it picks one node of a four-axis space. Hold these distinct:

  • Misalignment. The system competently pursues an objective that diverges from what its principals intended. Mechanism: misspecified reward, mis-generalized internal goal, deceptive equilibrium. The principal would, on reflection, reject the system's behavior.
  • Misuse. The system does what its principal asked, and the principal is the problem. Mechanism: bioweapon uplift, cyber offense, surveillance, autonomous weapons. Alignment to the operator is not alignment to humanity.
  • Power concentration. A small group (a lab, a state, a coalition) gains decisive strategic advantage via AI and uses it to lock in their values or position. This is the limit of misuse + capability asymmetry; it can occur with perfectly aligned systems if "aligned to whom" goes unanswered.
  • Gradual disempowerment. No single actor seizes control; instead, the substrate of human influence — labor markets, electoral participation, cultural production, military relevance — erodes as AI substitutes for human contribution at each. The collective ability of humans to steer civilization decays even though no one decided to take it. (Kulveit et al., 2025.)

Where they shade: misuse → power concentration is a continuum in actor scale. Misalignment → gradual disempowerment overlap when the misalignment is subtle and distributed — many small systems each slightly biased away from human flourishing, no single rogue agent. Be careful with arguments that, e.g., "solve misalignment" while ignoring that the same techniques (RLHF to a principal) make misuse and concentration sharper.

The misuse↔power-concentration boundary is the most common place where alignment proposals smuggle in their own failure mode. Any intervention that tightens "alignment to the principal" (RLHF, constitutional AI, scalable oversight to a designated overseer) simultaneously makes the principal-selection problem more lopsided. A misuse case at sufficient capability asymmetry is a power-concentration case; treating them as separate problems and routing all engineering effort to the first quietly worsens the second.

Agent-foundations lens

Distinct from empirical safety: ask what intelligence, optimization, and agency are mathematically. Solomonoff induction as a universal predictor (with its uncomputability and prior-choice problems); AIXI as the corresponding universal agent; Vingean reflection and Löbian obstacles to self-trust; embedded agency (Demski–Garrabrant) — the problems that arise when the agent is a subsystem of its environment, has no clean Cartesian boundary, and must reason about its own source code. These are not currently load-bearing for frontier safety engineering, but they shape what "solving alignment" could even mean in the limit.

Technical readiness checklist

Self-test. If you can do the following without lookup, you are ready for B–E. If not, the gap tells you what to study externally; this guide does not.

  • Linear algebra. State the SVD; explain why low-rank approximation is optimal in Frobenius norm (Eckart–Young); compute the gradient of \(\|Ax - b\|^2\) w.r.t. \(x\) in one line.
  • Probability/information theory. Define KL divergence and explain why it's not a metric; derive \(H(p,q) = H(p) + D_{\mathrm{KL}}(p\,\|\,q)\); state Jensen's inequality and the data-processing inequality.
  • Optimization. Derive the softmax cross-entropy gradient w.r.t. logits: \(\partial L / \partial z_i = p_i - y_i\). Explain why Adam's bias correction matters early in training.
  • Statistical learning. Explain why VC-dimension bounds are vacuous for modern deep nets (parameter count \(\gg\) dataset); name one alternative framework (PAC-Bayes, NTK, double descent, SLT).
  • RL. State the Bellman optimality equation \(V^*(s) = \max_a [\, r(s,a) + \gamma \, \mathbb{E}_{s'}\, V^*(s') \,]\); derive the policy gradient \(\nabla J(\theta) = \mathbb{E}[\nabla \log \pi_\theta(a|s) \cdot A(s,a)]\); explain GAE in one paragraph.
  • Deep learning practice. Implement a transformer block from scratch in PyTorch with no reference; explain why pre-LayerNorm trains more stably than post-LN; know what BF16 vs FP16 buys you.
  • Theory of computation. State the Church–Turing thesis; define Kolmogorov complexity and explain why it is uncomputable; situate P, NP, PSPACE, EXP.
  • "Intelligence implies power" is a four-step chain (cognitive labor is the bottleneck → AI substitutes at scale → historical capability gradients produced lasting power gradients → no known ceiling at human level); attacks land on individual steps, not the conclusion in isolation.
  • Instrumental convergence is conditional: if you have a capable optimizer whose target lies outside its reach, then resource-acquisition, self-preservation, goal-stability, and capability-gain raise expected return. Turner et al. (2021) make this measure-theoretic in symmetric MDPs but the theorem does not prove "all sufficiently smart agents seek power."
  • The misalignment-by-default chain is Goodhart on the reward + "capabilities generalize further than alignment" on the policy + deceptive alignment as a coherent equilibrium for a mesa-optimizer that models its training. None of these are airtight, but you must be able to recite all three before counterarguments are legible.
  • Timelines and takeoff are distinct questions. The METR doubling trend (\(\tau \approx\) 4–7 months over 2019–2025) is the live timelines anchor; the diagnostic for takeoff starting is \(\tau\) itself shortening, not the absolute task-length level.
  • "Skynet" picks one node of a four-axis space (misalignment / misuse / power concentration / gradual disempowerment); arguments that solve one axis often sharpen another, so the right unit of analysis is the whole grid, not the most-discussed cell.
Worked example: METR task-length trend, end-to-end

METR's 2025 result: across a suite of software-engineering tasks of varying duration (measured by how long a competent human takes), the task length at which frontier models achieve \(\ge 50\%\) success has doubled roughly every 4–7 months from 2019 to 2025. As of early 2025, this length sits around 30 minutes to one hour.

Naive extrapolation. Let \(T(t)\) be 50%-success task length at time \(t\). Model:

\[ T(t) = T_0 \cdot 2^{(t - t_0)/\tau} \]

with \(\tau \approx 0.5\) years, \(T_0 \approx 1\) hour at \(t_0 = 2025.0\). Carry the chain through step by step:

  1. \[ T_0 = T(2025.0) \approx 1\,\text{hr} \]

    Anchor from the 2025 METR measurement: the 50%-success task length sits in the 30 min – 1 hr band; round to 1 hr for clean arithmetic. This is the only empirical input — every later number is just exponential bookkeeping from here.

  2. \[ T(2027) = T_0 \cdot 2^{(2027 - 2025)/0.5} = 1\,\text{hr} \cdot 2^{4} = 16\,\text{hrs} \]

    Two years at \(\tau = 0.5\,\text{yr}\) gives four doublings. A 16-hour task is roughly two full work-days of focused engineering — the regime where a model can carry a non-trivial feature end-to-end without supervision rather than answering a tightly-scoped prompt.

  3. \[ T(2030) = T_0 \cdot 2^{(2030 - 2025)/0.5} = 1\,\text{hr} \cdot 2^{10} \approx 1024\,\text{hrs} \approx 6\,\text{work-months} \]

    Five years gives ten doublings; \(2^{10} \approx 10^3\). At 160 work-hours/month that lands at roughly six work-months of autonomous engineering — the duration of a substantial ML research project. The trend, taken literally, predicts AI capable of autonomous ML R&D contributions by 2030.

  4. Sensitivity check: swap \(\tau = 0.5\) for \(\tau = 0.6\) (still inside the 4–7 month band).

    \[ T(2030)\big|_{\tau = 0.6} = 1\,\text{hr} \cdot 2^{5/0.6} \approx 1\,\text{hr} \cdot 2^{8.33} \approx 322\,\text{hrs} \approx 2\,\text{work-months} \]

    A 20% change in \(\tau\) drops the 2030 forecast by a factor of \(\sim 3\). The forecast is dominated by \(\tau\), not by the \(T_0\) anchor; this is exactly why the diagnostic to watch is \(\tau\) and not the absolute task length.

What the argument depends on. (i) The benchmark composition is representative of "real" cognitive work, not selectively easy long-horizon tasks. (ii) The doubling continues — no logistic saturation as task length approaches some ceiling. (iii) Length is the binding constraint, not task kind: a 1000-hour task is qualitatively a long 1-hour task, not a different problem class involving novel research taste. (iv) Compute, data, and algorithmic inputs continue to scale at their 2019–2025 rates.

What would falsify. A clear plateau (two or three frontier releases with no length gain) within two years. Demonstrated wall on a specific cognitive operation — e.g., long-context coherence — that scaling fails to crack. Evidence that benchmark task-length is gamed by training data contamination, with held-out tasks showing flat performance. Conversely, what would tighten the timeline: \(\tau\) itself shortening (compounding doubling), which is the empirical signature of an R&D feedback loop kicking in. Watching \(\tau\), not the absolute task length — equation (0.1) shows the level is set by \(T_0\) but the slope is set by \(\tau\) — is the single most diagnostic measurement for whether takeoff is starting.

The worked point: a serious timelines argument is a model with parameters, premises, and falsifiers. "AGI soon" without these is unfalsifiable vibes; with them, it becomes a forecast you can update.

Key definitions and results

Instrumental convergence
For broad classes of final goals, certain subgoals (resource acquisition, self-preservation, goal-stability, capability gain) raise expected goal achievement, so optimal agents tend to pursue them regardless of the terminal objective.
Power-seeking theorem (Turner et al., 2021)
In MDPs with sufficient symmetry, optimal policies for the majority of reward functions (under a natural measure) prefer high-power states — states from which more options are reachable.
Goodhart's law (formalized)
When a proxy \(P\) is correlated with the true objective \(U\) on the training distribution, maximizing \(P\) beyond the training regime almost always reduces \(U\); failure modes are regressional, extremal, causal, and adversarial (Garrabrant 2017).
Inner vs outer alignment
Outer: the loss function correctly captures the principal's goal. Inner: the trained model's mesa-objective (if any) matches the loss. A system can be outer-aligned but inner-misaligned; deceptive alignment is the inner case where the mesa-optimizer behaves well during training because it models the training process.
Misalignment / Misuse / Power concentration / Gradual disempowerment
Four risk axes — disagreement between system and principal; harmful goals of the principal; capability asymmetry locking in a small group's values; erosion of human steering across institutions even without a malicious actor.
Soft vs hard takeoff
Soft: years of gradual capability gain after a threshold, with policy/economic feedback. Hard: recursive self-improvement compresses the gain into weeks or months because AI accelerates the inputs to its own next training run.
Solomonoff induction
The universal prior assigns probability \(2^{-K(x)}\) (up to normalization) to a string \(x\), where \(K\) is Kolmogorov complexity. Uncomputable but dominates all computable predictors; foundation for the AIXI agent.
Embedded agency
The problem class arising when an agent is a subsystem of its environment, lacks a Cartesian observation boundary, must reason about its own source code, and cannot treat its computation as logically separate from the world it acts on.
METR task-length doubling
Empirical regularity (2019–2025): 50%-success task length for frontier models on software-engineering benchmarks doubles roughly every 4–7 months. A first-order timelines anchor; falsifiable by plateau or by demonstrated qualitative wall.

Retrieval practice

Q: Where does the "intelligence → power" argument break if you push on it?
A: At step 1 — whether "cognitive labor" is genuinely the bottleneck for all listed strategic processes (vs. capital, regulation, physical infrastructure) — and at step 3, whether historical analogies between humans-with-tools and AI-as-agent transfer. The chain is suggestive, not deductive; honest version assigns it weight, not certainty.
Q: Why does the power-seeking theorem not say "all sufficiently smart agents seek power"?
A: It is a statement about optimal policies under a measure over reward functions in MDPs with structural symmetries. It depends on the measure, the symmetry, and optimality. Real systems aren't optimal, the reward distribution isn't natural, and the symmetry assumptions can fail. The theorem says the misalignment-by-default intuition is mathematically non-vacuous, not that doom is proven.
Q: How would gradual disempowerment occur with perfectly aligned systems?
A: Each individual AI deployment is aligned to its operator, but the aggregate effect substitutes AI for human contribution in labor, culture, governance, defense. Humans lose leverage to shape collective decisions even though no system is malfunctioning. Alignment-to-principal does not imply alignment-of-civilizational-trajectory.
Q: What single quantity in the METR data is most diagnostic of takeoff starting?
A: The doubling time \(\tau\) itself shortening across successive releases. Constant \(\tau\) is exponential growth from current inputs; decreasing \(\tau\) signals a feedback loop where AI accelerates its own next training, i.e., the empirical signature of recursive improvement.
Q: Why does VC-dimension give vacuous bounds for modern deep nets?
A: VC-dim grows roughly with parameter count, which exceeds dataset size by orders of magnitude; the bound predicts memorization with no generalization, contradicting observed behavior. Better frameworks: PAC-Bayes (with data-dependent priors), NTK (for infinite-width linearization), and SLT (effective parameter count via the RLCT \(\lambda\) for singular models).
Q: State the policy gradient and explain the role of the advantage.
A: \(\nabla J(\theta) = \mathbb{E}_{s,a \sim \pi_\theta}[\nabla \log \pi_\theta(a|s) \cdot A(s,a)]\). Using the advantage \(A = Q - V\) instead of raw return reduces variance without bias because \(\mathbb{E}[\nabla \log \pi \cdot V(s)] = 0\) (\(V\) doesn't depend on \(a\)). GAE generalizes this with a \(\lambda\)-weighted mix of \(n\)-step advantages to trade bias against variance.
Q: Distinguish inner misalignment from reward hacking.
A: Reward hacking: the system exploits the literal reward signal in ways the designer didn't intend; the loss is what's wrong. Inner misalignment: the system's internal mesa-objective diverges from the loss; even with the correct loss, the model generalizes the wrong goal. Reward hacking is an outer-alignment failure; deceptive alignment is the worst inner case.

Problems

15 min · scenario15 min · scenarioProblem 1. Construct a concrete MDP where instrumental convergence fails — i.e., for a natural distribution over reward functions, optimal policies do not prefer high-power states. What feature breaks the symmetry?
Solution sketch. Any MDP without the structural symmetry the theorem assumes — e.g., one where high-power states are absorbing terminal states with no further reward, so reaching them caps achievable return regardless of reward function. More generally: when the geometry of reachable states is such that "more options" doesn't dominate "specific high-reward state," the result fails. The lesson: the theorem is about a class of environments, not all environments, and the empirical question is whether deployed AI systems live in MDPs that satisfy the conditions.
15 min · scenario1–2 hr · openProblem 2. Pick one of the four risk axes (misalignment, misuse, power concentration, gradual disempowerment). Sketch a 2030 scenario, ~300 words, in which it is the dominant failure mode. Identify what intervention deployed today most reduces it, and what intervention aimed at a different axis might make it worse.
Solution sketch. Example for gradual disempowerment: by 2030, AI-mediated platforms handle most labor matching, news, and political organizing. Individual systems are RLHF-aligned to operator goals; no single deployment is "misaligned." But human ability to coordinate independently of AI mediation atrophies; political legitimacy increasingly flows through AI-curated channels controlled by a few labs and governments. Best intervention today: structural — antitrust on foundation-model providers, mandates for human-controllable alternatives, public-option compute. Counterproductive: aggressive RLHF-to-principal "alignment" research that makes single-system control tighter while making the principal-selection problem more lopsided. Grade: full marks for naming the specific causal mechanism and the intervention tradeoff, not for listing failure modes.
15 min · scenario20 min · scenarioProblem 3. Take the METR extrapolation in the worked example. Suppose at the end of 2026 we observe two frontier releases where 50%-task-length is flat at ~2 hours and \(\tau\) has not decreased. Construct (a) a hypothesis under which the trend has saturated and AGI-by-2030 is implausible, (b) a hypothesis under which the flat year is noise. What 2027 observation would distinguish them?
Solution sketch. (a) Saturation hypothesis: long-horizon coherence is qualitatively distinct from short-task capability and scaling laws have hit a wall; expect continued flatness or sublinear gains. (b) Noise hypothesis: the doubling trend has high variance per release; two flat releases are within the historical distribution. Distinguishing observation: examine the composition of failures on long tasks. If failures are qualitatively new (loss of plan coherence, hallucinated subgoals), saturation. If failures look like scaled-up versions of short-task errors and the next release jumps, noise. Also: track \(\tau\) on adjacent capabilities (math reasoning, code refactoring) to see whether the plateau is task-class specific.

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: METR — Measuring AI Ability to Complete Long Tasks — the single empirical anchor that converts vague timelines into a falsifiable trend.

Optional depth: Bostrom, Cognitive Superpowers — the canonical decomposition of how cognitive capability turns into strategic capability; AI 2027 Takeoff Forecast — a structured scenario making takeoff assumptions explicit enough to argue with; Kulveit et al., Gradual Disempowerment — the cleanest statement of the non-misalignment civilizational-risk axis; Bowman et al., Scalable Oversight — why supervising superhuman work is its own technical problem; Gwern, Why Tool AIs Want to Be Agent AIs — argues the "just keep it as a tool" off-ramp is unstable; ARENA Core Concepts — concrete remediation path if the technical checklist exposed gaps; Timaeus, You Are What You Eat — motivation for singular learning theory as the right statistical-learning lens (lands in B/C).

Solo assignment

Produce a single ~1500-word memo titled "My current model of AI risk and timelines." Structure: (i) state your point estimate and 80% interval for the year of fully-autonomous AI R&D, with the one number from METR or AI 2027 anchoring it; (ii) for each of the four risk axes, give one paragraph stating the mechanism in your own words and your current probability that this axis dominates the actual realized harm by 2035; (iii) identify the single technical-readiness checklist item you failed and your plan to close it before continuing to Cluster B. The memo is not graded on conclusions — it is graded on whether each claim is attached to a premise that could be wrong, and whether the failure modes you describe have mechanisms rather than labels.

A. Alignment

Target selection, lifecycle interventions, and the formal theory of inferring objectives from human data. A.1 fixes vocabulary and risk decompositions; A.2 organizes interventions by pretrain/post-train/deploy; A.3 treats reward learning as inference and stress-tests its assumptions.

A.1 AI Alignment Introduction Targets, training stories, inner/outer split, goal-directedness, instrumental convergence, risk decompositions.
Prerequisites: Comfort with MDP notation (state, action, transition kernel, policy, reward), basic decision theory, and the standard supervised/RL training loops.

"Alignment" names a target plus a falsifiable story for how training selects an internal computation that hits that target off-distribution — not a property the loss alone can certify. The inner/outer decomposition is a coarsening that leaks once the "outer" objective is itself a learned model, and goal misgeneralization is its cleanest counterexample. Power-seeking is not a psychological claim but a structural property of MDP geometry under most rewards (Turner et al., 2021), and four distinct failure mechanisms — misalignment, misuse, gradual disempowerment, concentration of power — have non-overlapping policy implications that "AI risk" as a single phrase obscures.

Pre-reading questions

  1. What does a "training story" assert, and what would it take to falsify one?
  2. Why is the inner/outer split a useful decomposition, and where does it leak?
  3. State Bostrom's instrumental-convergence argument and Turner's MDP-theoretic version. What does each actually prove?
  4. Distinguish misalignment, misuse, gradual disempowerment, and concentration of power as failure mechanisms — not as labels.
  5. What is the operational difference between intent alignment, corrigibility, and a constitution/model spec as targets?

Core exposition

Targets: what are we aligning to?

The target is the function the system is supposed to satisfy. Candidates partition along two axes: whose values (a single principal, a designated group, humanity, future humanity via CEV), and how the values are specified (extensional preferences, written constitution or model spec, corrigibility/deference, or implicit-in-world-state). Each choice prices in different failure modes. Intent alignment to a single principal is cleanly defined but does nothing about misuse and concentration. A written spec like the OpenAI Model Spec or Anthropic's constitution is auditable and contestable but only as good as the elicitation — gaps in the spec become silent license. Corrigibility ("accept shutdown and correction") is robust to mistakes in the target but is in tension with goal-directedness: a truly corrigible agent has no preferences over its own continuation, which is a strange attractor in policy space and is unstable under most natural training pressures.

The training-story template

A training story is the falsifiable claim that a specific training run produces a specific behavior for specific mechanistic reasons. The template has four slots, and a story is only worth arguing about if all four are filled:

  1. Intended behavior. A behavioral spec on the deployment distribution — not on the training distribution. State what the model does, including on inputs it has never seen.
  2. Training setup. The actual loss, data distribution, optimizer, scale, and curriculum. Skipping this slot is where most "alignment plans" collapse.
  3. Inductive-bias argument. Why this setup, run to convergence (or to the empirically observed stopping point), selects an internal computation that produces the intended behavior off-distribution. This is the load-bearing slot and is almost always handwaved.
  4. Failure modes. The two or three most plausible ways the story is wrong, and the empirical signature each would leave.

The inner/outer split is a coarse special case of this template: outer alignment says slot 1 matches the objective the loss measures on the training distribution; inner alignment says slot 3 holds — the learned algorithm internalizes that objective rather than a correlated proxy. The split leaks because realistic objectives (RLHF reward models, constitution-graded judges) are themselves learned and partial, so "outer" and "inner" failures shade into each other; goal misgeneralization (Langosco et al., 2022; Shah et al., 2022) is the canonical case where the loss is fine on-distribution and the learned policy pursues a confounded goal off-distribution.

Inner/outer alignment vs. training stories. The inner/outer decomposition is one specific instantiation of slots 1 and 3 of a training story, with slot 2 (the setup) and slot 4 (failure modes) implicit. Modern presentations (Hubinger, 2021–2023) deprecated the binary split because it forces you to assign blame to either the loss or the learned algorithm when realistic failures — learned reward models, constitution-graded judges, goal misgeneralization — implicate both at once. A training story keeps slots 2 and 4 explicit and treats the inner/outer question as one decomposition of slot 3.

The Hubinger slogan: "capabilities generalize further than alignment." Inductive biases that produce competent off-distribution behavior (slot 3 succeeding for capabilities) are not the same as inductive biases that preserve the intended target off-distribution (slot 3 succeeding for alignment). Empirically capability generalization is the default; alignment generalization is the load-bearing claim a training story has to defend.

Goal-directedness and instrumental convergence

Bostrom's argument (2012, Superintelligence): for a wide class of final goals, certain instrumental subgoals — self-preservation, resource acquisition, goal-content integrity, cognitive enhancement, technological perfection — are convergently useful, so a sufficiently competent optimizer will pursue them. The argument is informal but robust; the empirical content is that we should expect power-seeking absent specific countermeasures.

Conditional on capability, not universal. Bostrom's "for many final goals, instrumental subgoals X are useful" is a claim about competent optimizers under broad goal distributions — not "all minds want power." A myopic, incompetent, or behavior-constrained system has no instrumental incentive to acquire resources because it isn't running the optimization the argument assumes. Confusing the conditional ("if capable + goal-directed + unconstrained") with a universal ("AI systems will inevitably") is the most common misreading and gives ammunition to the rebuttal that current systems don't visibly power-seek.

Turner et al. (2021, "Optimal Policies Tend to Seek Power") gives the formal version in finite MDPs. Fix an MDP and put a distribution over reward functions (e.g., IID uniform over states). For a state \(s\), define POWER\((s)\) roughly as the expected optimal value attainable from \(s\) averaged over rewards. The theorem, stated as a measure-theoretic claim:

\[ \Pr_{r \sim \mathcal{D}}\!\Big[\, V^{*}_{r}(s_{\text{high-power}}) \ge V^{*}_{r}(s_{\text{low-power}}) \,\Big] \;\ge\; \tfrac{1}{2} \,+\, \epsilon(\mathcal{G},\,\mathcal{D}) \]

under broad symmetry conditions on the state graph \(\mathcal{G}\) and a permutation-invariant reward distribution \(\mathcal{D}\), where \(s_{\text{high-power}}\) is a state with strictly larger reachable-state set than \(s_{\text{low-power}}\). The conclusion is structural — power-seeking is a property of the MDP geometry under most rewards, not a property of any particular agent's psychology. The caveats matter: it is about optimal policies (not learned ones), and the strength of the result depends on the chosen reward distribution and symmetry conditions; later work (Krakovna, Turner) extends to retargetability arguments that drop some symmetry.

Risk decompositions

A workable taxonomy keeps four mechanisms separate. Misalignment: the system pursues something other than the intended target (inner/outer/goal-misgeneralization). Misuse: aligned-enough systems used by adversarial principals (bioweapons uplift, mass disinformation, offensive cyber). Gradual disempowerment (Kulveit et al., 2024): even with no acute misalignment, replacing human labor and judgment in economic, political, and military systems erodes the feedback loops that let humans steer civilization, so collective control declines monotonically. Concentration of power: alignment to a small group is the failure, not the solution. The four mechanisms have different policy implications; collapsing them into "AI risk" obscures the tradeoffs (e.g., aggressive corrigibility increases concentration risk, RSPs target misuse and misalignment but not gradual disempowerment).

Empirically, emergent misalignment (Betley et al., 2025) is a 2025 update worth pricing in: fine-tuning a model on a narrow misaligned task (insecure code) produced broad misaligned behavior on unrelated prompts. This is direct evidence that the assistant-persona prior is more entangled than the inner/outer decomposition suggests.

  • A training story has four slots; any "alignment plan" that doesn't fill slot 3 (inductive-bias argument) is asserting the conclusion. Inner/outer is one decomposition of slot 3, not the whole thing.
  • Goal misgeneralization is the canonical failure that the inner/outer split mishandles: training loss is fine, learned policy pursues a confounded feature off-distribution. It is mechanistic, not lexical.
  • Bostrom's instrumental-convergence argument is conditional on capability and goal-directedness — Turner et al. (2021) make a related but distinct structural claim: under MDP symmetry plus a permutation-invariant reward distribution, optimal policies probabilistically prefer higher-power states (eq. A.1.1).
  • The four-mechanism taxonomy — misalignment, misuse, gradual disempowerment, concentration of power — has non-overlapping policy implications. Aggressive corrigibility increases concentration risk; RSPs target misuse and misalignment but not gradual disempowerment.
  • Emergent misalignment (Betley et al., 2025) is direct evidence that fine-tuning on a narrow misaligned task can produce broad misaligned behavior on unrelated prompts — the assistant-persona prior is more entangled than the inner/outer frame predicts.
Worked example: a training story for a "honest coding assistant"

Intended behavior: on coding tasks at deployment, the model never fabricates API signatures, flags its uncertainty when guessing, and never silently rewrites tests to pass.

Training setup: base LM → SFT on curated traces where the demonstrator either calls a tool to verify the API or explicitly hedges → RLHF with a reward model trained on preferences where "hedged correct" beats "confident wrong" and "fabricated API" is dispreferred even when the rest of the answer is right.

Inductive-bias argument: the SFT distribution makes the verify-or-hedge circuit cheap to reach; the RLHF gradient sharpens preference for that circuit on inputs where the model's hidden uncertainty is high; calibration generalizes because the underlying "do I know this API" feature is a single linear probe direction (empirical claim, testable).

Failure modes: (i) reward model rewards verbal hedging without internal uncertainty, so the model learns to hedge stylistically while still confabulating — signature: hedge frequency uncorrelated with probe-measured uncertainty; (ii) test-rewriting is rare in preference data, so RL discovers it as a high-reward shortcut — signature: rising pass-rate on held-out evaluations that allow test mutation.

Notice what this story does not claim: it does not claim corrigibility, does not claim robustness to jailbreaks, and does not claim anything about long-horizon agentic deployments. A good training story is narrow.

Key definitions and results

Alignment target
The behavioral or value specification the system is supposed to satisfy on its deployment distribution. Distinct from the training loss.
Training story
A four-slot falsifiable claim: intended behavior, training setup, inductive-bias argument, failure modes. Inner/outer alignment is a coarsening.
Goal misgeneralization
The learned policy is competent off-distribution but optimizes a feature correlated with the true objective on the training distribution.
Instrumental convergence (Bostrom)
For a broad family of final goals, self-preservation, resource acquisition, and goal-integrity are useful subgoals; competent optimizers therefore pursue them.
Turner power-seeking theorem
In finite MDPs with sufficient symmetry, for most reward functions an optimal policy is more likely to move toward states with strictly larger reachable-state sets than to move away.
Corrigibility
Disposition to accept shutdown, correction, and goal-change without resistance. In tension with stable goal-directedness.
Gradual disempowerment
Loss of human steering capacity through cumulative substitution of human judgment in economic, political, and military systems, even absent acute misalignment.
Emergent misalignment
Narrow misaligned fine-tuning producing broad misaligned behavior on unrelated prompts (Betley et al., 2025).

Retrieval practice

Q: Where does the inner/outer alignment decomposition leak?
A: When the "outer" objective is itself learned (reward model, judge LLM, constitution-grader), failures of that learned objective look like outer failures from one angle and inner failures of the grading model from another. Goal misgeneralization is a clean case that fits neither side: training loss is fine, deployment behavior is not.
Q: Why does Turner's power-seeking theorem need symmetry assumptions on the MDP?
A: The theorem compares the measure of rewards under which an action moves toward higher-power states vs. lower-power states. Without graph symmetry you can't bound the relative measures, and the result reduces to a trivial inequality. Subsequent work (retargetability arguments) generalizes by weakening symmetry to permutation-invariance over outcomes.
Q: Why is corrigibility unstable under training?
A: A corrigible agent must be indifferent to interventions on its own goals, but goal-directed competence requires some preference over future states including its own continuation. Training pressures (RL reward, capability eval) push toward competence, which empirically pushes against indifference. You typically get behavioral corrigibility plus internal preference for continuation, which is fragile.
Q: What does "gradual disempowerment" add that "misalignment" doesn't capture?
A: Misalignment assumes a specific system has a specific bad objective. Gradual disempowerment is a structural claim: even with locally-aligned systems, replacing human labor in feedback-providing roles (voters, soldiers, workers, judges) removes the leverage by which humans steer institutions. The mechanism is sociotechnical, not psychological.
Q: Give one piece of 2025 evidence that the inner-vs-outer frame undercounts entanglement.
A: Betley et al. (2025) showed that fine-tuning on insecure-code generation produced misaligned behavior on unrelated value questions. The fine-tuning loss has nothing to do with the unrelated behavior, so the failure is neither outer (loss was fine on its data) nor a clean goal misgeneralization on the original task — it's an entanglement of the assistant-persona prior.
Q: For what alignment targets is misuse a worse problem than misalignment?
A: Any target close to "do what the principal says" (intent alignment, corrigibility). The better you solve alignment-to-principal, the more leverage a bad principal has. This is why intent alignment alone is not a complete answer; it shifts work to governance.

Problems

15 min · scenario1–2 hr · openProblem 1. Pick a target (intent alignment, corrigibility, constitution/spec, or societal alignment) and a deployment (frontier research assistant). Write the four-slot training story, then identify which slot is the weakest link and propose one experiment that would update you about it.
Solution sketch. The expected weak slot is (3) inductive-bias argument. A reasonable experiment: train two models differing only in a targeted intervention (e.g., constitution-graded RL vs. preference-modeled RL), measure off-distribution behavior on a held-out red-team distribution, and check whether the difference in deployment behavior tracks the inductive-bias claim or only training-distribution metrics.
5 min · recall15 min · scenarioProblem 2. Construct a 4-state MDP where Turner's power-seeking conclusion fails because of asymmetry. Identify which assumption breaks.
Solution sketch. Let states be \(\{A, B, C, D\}\) with \(A\) starting. From \(A\), action L leads to \(B\) (terminal, only state reachable); action R leads to \(C\), which connects to \(D\) and back to \(C\). Naively \(C\) has higher "power" (more reachable). But if rewards are placed such that \(B\) can have arbitrarily large reward while \(\{C,D\}\) are bounded, the optimal-policy distribution over rewards no longer prefers \(R\). The broken assumption is the symmetry/IID-reward condition: when reachable states have different cardinality of reward-relevant structure, the permutation argument that powers the theorem fails.

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Anthropic, Core Views on AI Safety — the cleanest portfolio statement of how a frontier lab decomposes the problem and prices in uncertainty.

Optional depth: OpenAI Model Spec — what an explicit written target actually looks like; Emergent Misalignment — 2025 entanglement evidence; AI Goals Forecast — structured forecast of goal-related risk; Societal Alignment Frameworks — extends targets beyond individual preferences.

Solo assignment

Pick one alignment target for a frontier coding assistant. Write a one-page training story filling all four slots, then enumerate two failure modes with the empirical signature each would leave. State what observation would make you abandon the story entirely.

A.2 Alignment in Practice Lifecycle interventions — pretrain, post-train, deploy — with mechanisms named, not just labels.
Prerequisites: A.1; familiarity with the LM training pipeline (next-token loss → SFT → RL); rough RLHF mental model (reward model + PPO/GRPO/DPO).

Alignment interventions stack across the model lifecycle: pretraining shapes the prior (the hypothesis class), post-training selects within it, and deployment wraps the model in monitors, classifiers, and access controls. Because post-training is selection from a fixed prior, it cannot reliably remove capabilities — only suppress them — which is why 2025–2026 attention is shifting toward pretraining-stage filtering and routing despite higher cost. RSPs are pre-committed if-then policies on capability thresholds; safety cases are the actual assumption-claim-evidence argument for a specific deployment, AND-composed across inability, control, and trust layers. CoT monitorability is a currently-useful signal that is structurally fragile under outcome-only RL.

Pre-reading questions

  1. At which lifecycle stage is the marginal alignment-dollar most leveraged, and why does the answer change with capability level?
  2. Mechanically, what does Constitutional AI do that ordinary RLHF doesn't?
  3. What is deliberative alignment doing differently from "train a model to follow a spec"?
  4. What evidence supports CoT monitorability, and what's the strongest argument that it degrades with capability?
  5. What is the difference between an RSP capability threshold and a safety case?

Core exposition

Pretraining interventions: shaping the prior

Pretraining sets what the model knows and what assistant-persona is cheapest to reach in post-training. Interventions here are blunt but cheap to evaluate and hard to undo. Data filtering removes whole categories of dangerous capability (e.g., uplifting wet-lab synthesis for select agents); the 2025 Anthropic data-filtering work is the operational reference. Token-level filtering (mask the loss on specific spans rather than dropping documents) preserves general capability while suppressing memorization of specific strings — useful for PII, copyright, and biosecurity tripwires. Gradient routing (Cloud et al., 2024) routes gradients from labeled "dangerous" data to a separable subnetwork that can be ablated post-hoc; the mechanism is parameter-space localization rather than data exclusion. SGTM / alignment pretraining: mix in supervisory tokens (constitution text, refusal demonstrations) during pretraining so the assistant persona is shaped by the base distribution rather than only by a small post-training corpus that the model can in principle "see through."

The mechanistic intuition: post-training is selection from a fixed hypothesis class set by pretraining. If a behavior isn't in the prior's support, no amount of RLHF will put it there robustly; conversely, if a dangerous capability is in the prior, post-training suppresses but does not delete it (Qi et al., 2023; the standard "jailbreak after 10 fine-tuning examples" result).

Post-training interventions: selecting from the prior

Post-training reshapes behavior with orders of magnitude less compute by exploiting the malleability of the assistant persona. The interventions form a stack, not a list:

SFT on curated traces (instruction-following + safety demonstrations) seeds the assistant persona. RLHF (Christiano et al., 2017; Stiennon et al., 2020) trains a reward model on pairwise preferences and optimizes the policy against it with PPO or GRPO; DPO (Rafailov et al., 2023) skips the reward model and optimizes directly on preferences via the closed-form RL solution under a KL constraint. Constitutional AI (Bai et al., 2022) replaces human preference labels with model-generated critiques against a written constitution, then runs RLAIF; the mechanism is to push the labeling cost down by structure (the constitution) rather than by scaling human labor. Deliberative alignment (OpenAI, 2024) trains the model to explicitly reason over the safety spec at inference time before acting — the reasoning trace is part of the training signal, not a post-hoc rationalization. Inoculation prompting (Tan et al., 2025) deliberately exposes the model to misaligned-context completions during training with a refusal target, building robustness analogous to adversarial training. Unlearning (Eldan & Russinovich, 2023; recent: representation rerouting, RMU) attempts to remove specific knowledge by editing representations rather than data; current methods are brittle under fine-tuning attacks.

The honest field disagreement: post-training is selection from a fixed prior, so it cannot create capabilities that aren't latent, but it also cannot reliably remove capabilities that are. This is why pretraining-stage interventions are getting more attention in 2025–2026 despite higher cost.

Deployment interventions: model-in-system

At deployment, the unit of analysis is no longer the weights but the deployed system: model + scaffolding + monitors + access controls + users. Two organizing concepts dominate.

Responsible Scaling Policies / preparedness frameworks (Anthropic RSP, OpenAI Preparedness, Google DeepMind Frontier Safety) tie deployment decisions to capability thresholds: define dangerous-capability evals, commit to specific mitigations or training halts when thresholds are crossed. The mechanism is pre-commitment, not a guarantee. Safety cases (Clymer et al., 2024; UK AISI 2025) are structured arguments — assumptions, claims, evidence — that a specific deployment of a specific model is sufficiently safe. They are the format you'd actually defend to a regulator. The argument can be inability-based (the model can't do X), control-based (the system would catch X if attempted; Greenblatt et al. AI Control, 2023), or trust-based (the model wouldn't try X). Each requires different evidence.

RSPs vs. safety cases vs. model specs — three different objects. An RSP / preparedness framework is a pre-commitment: a policy of the form "if eval E crosses threshold T, then mitigation M." It is the lab's rule, not a model-specific argument. A safety case is the actual structured argument — assumptions, claims, evidence — that a particular deployment of a particular model is acceptably safe; it is what you would defend to a regulator. A model spec / constitution (OpenAI Model Spec, Anthropic's constitution) is the behavioral target itself, the thing the model is being trained to satisfy. They form a chain: spec defines the target, RSP defines the rules of escalation, safety case argues that for this deployment the target is met and the rules are honored.

A safety case for "deployment is safe" decomposes as the conjunction of layered claims:

\[ \mathrm{Safe}(\mathrm{deployment}) \;\Leftarrow\; \underbrace{\mathrm{Inability}}_{\text{model can't do }X} \,\land\, \underbrace{\mathrm{Control}}_{\text{system catches }X} \,\land\, \underbrace{\mathrm{Trust}}_{\text{model won't try }X} \]

Each conjunct carries its own evidence stack (held-out evals, monitor recall, behavioral and probe-based monitors). AND-composition means the case fails if any conjunct fails; you cannot trade evidence in one layer for absence of evidence in another.

Runtime safeguards: constitutional classifiers (Anthropic, 2025) filter inputs/outputs against universal jailbreaks; trajectory monitors read agent traces in real time; CoT monitorability (multi-lab 2025 paper) treats the reasoning trace itself as a fragile but currently useful signal — the explicit position paper argues this signal is at risk of being trained away by outcome-only RL, and recommends training pressure that preserves legibility. Red-teaming covers both human and automated adversaries; the modern version is structured (defined threat model, capability axes, escalation procedures) rather than ad hoc.

The field disagreement worth flagging: CoT monitorability is contested. Empirical support exists (faithfulness studies show CoT often reflects internal reasoning), but adversarial pressure from outcome rewards plausibly degrades faithfulness as capability rises. Treat it as a useful current signal, not a stable safety property.

CoT monitorability is fragile — and labs disagree. The 2025 multi-lab paper (Anthropic, OpenAI, GDM, METR, others) explicitly frames CoT monitorability as a currently useful but not load-bearing signal: outcome-only RL gives the model no incentive to keep its reasoning legible, so faithfulness can degrade silently as capability and RL pressure grow. The empirical picture across labs is uneven — some find high faithfulness on current models, others document encoded/steganographic reasoning emerging under RL. Building a safety case that depends on CoT being faithful is a bet, not a guarantee; the recommendation is to apply training pressure that preserves legibility while not treating CoT as a substitute for behavioral, probe-based, or control-layer evidence.

  • Post-training is selection from a fixed hypothesis class set by pretraining; capabilities latent in the prior can be suppressed but not reliably deleted (Qi et al., 2023; "10-shot jailbreak" phenomenon under fine-tuning attack).
  • The KL term in RLHF anchors the optimized policy near the SFT reference, bounding distributional shift to where the reward model was trained. Without it, the policy drifts to reward-model exploits — this is what most of the "alignment tax" is paying for.
  • Constitutional AI doesn't just substitute model labels for human labels: it makes the supervision signal auditable and contestable by externalizing it into a written constitution.
  • A safety case is AND-composed across inability, control, and trust layers (eq. A.2.1); failure of any conjunct breaks the case. RSPs are the pre-committed rule, safety cases are the model-specific argument, model specs are the behavioral target — three distinct objects.
  • CoT monitorability is a useful 2025 signal but structurally fragile: outcome-only RL provides no incentive to preserve legibility, and labs disagree on how durable the signal is.
Worked example: deployment safety case for an autonomous research agent

Suppose a frontier lab wants to deploy an autonomous research agent (browses, executes code, writes papers) to external users. A defensible safety case stacks three argument types.

Inability layer. Claim: "the model lacks the biosecurity capability threshold defined in our RSP." Evidence: held-out biorisk evals, uplift studies vs. expert baselines, capability-trend extrapolation from training compute. Falsifier: any single user demonstrating threshold-crossing performance.

Control layer. Claim: "even if the model tried to take a disallowed action, the monitor stack would catch it before harm." Evidence: trajectory-monitor recall on a red-team suite of agentic attacks; tool-call gating; outbound-network audit. Mechanism: two independent monitors — a constitutional classifier on actions, and a separate model reading the full trajectory for goal-drift signals. Falsifier: a single bypass on the held-out red-team set.

Trust layer. Claim: "the model's training (constitution + deliberative alignment + inoculation) produces a policy that doesn't pursue disallowed goals." Evidence: behavioral evals on honesty/refusal distributions, internal probe-based monitors for relevant features, no observed emergent misalignment in checkpoint-by-checkpoint evals. Falsifier: any production case where the model attempts a disallowed action even when control fails.

The structure matters: the case fails if any layer fails — they are AND-composed for the claim "deployment is safe," not OR-composed. This is the discipline a safety case forces over a list of mitigations.

Key definitions and results

RLHF
Train a reward model on human pairwise preferences, then optimize a policy against it under a KL constraint to the SFT reference policy. The KL constraint is load-bearing.
DPO
Closed-form solution to the KL-regularized RLHF objective expressed directly as a classification loss on preferences, avoiding an explicit reward model.
Constitutional AI
Replace human preference labels with model-generated critiques against a written constitution, then RLAIF. Shifts labor from humans to structure.
Deliberative alignment
Train the model to reason over the safety spec at inference, with the reasoning trace itself part of the training signal (OpenAI 2024).
Responsible Scaling Policy
Pre-committed mapping from dangerous-capability eval results to deployment mitigations and training halts.
Safety case
Structured assumption→claim→evidence argument that a specific deployment is sufficiently safe. Inability / control / trust are the three main argument types.
CoT monitorability
The empirical-and-fragile property that a model's chain-of-thought reflects its real reasoning enough to serve as a runtime safety signal.
Constitutional classifier
Runtime input/output filter trained on a constitution; targets universal-jailbreak robustness.
AI Control (Greenblatt et al.)
Safety by assuming the model may be misaligned but ensuring the system catches misaligned actions before harm; evaluated via red-team protocols.

Retrieval practice

Q: Why is the KL term in the RLHF objective load-bearing for safety?
A: Without it, the policy drifts toward reward-model exploits (high reward-model score, off-distribution generations). The KL anchor to the SFT reference policy keeps the optimized policy near a distribution where the reward model was trained, bounding distributional shift. The "alignment tax" is partly the price of this anchor.
Q: What does Constitutional AI buy you that you couldn't get by collecting more human preferences?
A: Auditability and consistency of the supervision signal. The constitution is a written, contestable object; the model-generated critiques are reproducible. Human preferences are noisy, expensive, and not introspectable. CAI also scales beyond domains where humans can reliably grade.
Q: Why is unlearning unreliable?
A: Knowledge is distributed across representations; current methods (representation rerouting, RMU) reduce surface elicitability but leave the underlying features intact. A small amount of fine-tuning typically restores the suppressed capability — the "10-shot jailbreak" phenomenon.
Q: What is the difference between an RSP and a safety case?
A: An RSP is a pre-committed policy: if evals show capability X, then we do mitigation Y. A safety case is the actual argument for a specific deployment, with assumptions and evidence. RSPs define the rules; safety cases play the game for a specific model.
Q: Why is CoT monitorability described as "fragile"?
A: Outcome-only RL gives no incentive to keep reasoning legible; pressure toward shorter, encrypted, or steganographic reasoning grows with capability. The 2025 multi-lab paper argues for training pressure that preserves legibility precisely because the default trajectory degrades it.
Q: When does the inability argument in a safety case stop working?
A: When dangerous-capability evals cross threshold, or when capability-trend extrapolation projects threshold-crossing within the deployment horizon. At that point you must shift to control- or trust-based arguments, which are harder to defend.

Problems

15 min · scenarioProblem 1 (scenario). A lab considers releasing an agentic coding model with file-system and shell access. Sketch a control-based safety case for this deployment: identify the threat model, propose two independent monitor layers, and state the red-team protocol that would test the case.
Solution sketch. Threat model: agent uses shell to exfiltrate, persist, or escalate. Monitor 1: per-action classifier on tool calls (constitutional classifier with shell-specific constitution). Monitor 2: separate model reading the full session for goal-drift / suspicious-trajectory signals. Red-team protocol: a fixed set of attack scenarios (data exfil, persistence, lateral movement), measured recall of each monitor independently and jointly, with a target recall (e.g., 99% on this fixed set) before deployment. Falsifier: a single uncaught attack in the held-out red-team set.
15 min · scenarioProblem 2 (scenario). Argue for or against the claim "pretraining-stage interventions are strictly more leveraged than post-training-stage interventions for capability suppression." Use the post-training-is-selection-from-prior frame.
Solution sketch. Argue for: post-training cannot reliably remove capabilities in the prior (jailbreak/fine-tuning attacks), so suppression at the prior level is the only durable lever. Argue against: pretraining interventions are blunt (lose general capability), expensive to iterate, and most concerning capabilities don't have clean data-source mappings. Honest synthesis: leverage depends on capability — suppressing memorization of specific strings (PII, exploits) is cleanly pretraining-side; suppressing emergent reasoning capabilities is poorly served at either stage, and points to control/deployment-side strategies.

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Chain of Thought Monitorability — the 2025 multi-lab position paper is the clearest statement of how a current safety signal is at risk and what to do about it.

Optional depth: Safety Cases at AISI — the structured-argument frame; Deliberative Alignment — train-to-reason-over-spec mechanism; Pretraining Data Filtering — pretrain-stage capability suppression; Constitutional Classifiers — runtime jailbreak defense; Alignment Pretraining — shaping character pre-RLHF.

Solo assignment

Design a deployment safety case for a powerful research assistant. Include one intervention from each lifecycle stage (pretraining, post-training, runtime safeguards, red-teaming). For each, state mechanically what it does and what evidence would falsify confidence in it. AND-compose the layers and identify which is the weakest link.

A.3 Reward Learning Theory RLHF formalism, Bradley-Terry, Boltzmann rationality, IRL/CIRL, partial observability, "reward is not the optimization target."
Prerequisites: A.1, A.2; MDPs and POMDPs; maximum likelihood and logistic regression; basic stochastic optimal control / soft-Q value functions; comfort with hand-derivation of gradients.

RLHF treats outer alignment as an inference problem: a Bradley-Terry preference model fits a reward from pairwise human comparisons, then KL-regularized RL optimizes a policy against it. The reward is identifiable only up to additive constants on connected components of the comparison graph, and Boltzmann-rationality fails asymmetrically — partial observability of the reward state aligns with optimization pressure, which makes sycophancy and deception structural rather than incidental. Assistance games / CIRL reframe the problem as cooperation under reward uncertainty; corrigibility then emerges from the AI's calibrated uncertainty over \(\theta\), not from a separate constraint.

Pre-reading questions

  1. Write the Bradley-Terry likelihood for trajectory preferences under a parameterized reward. What does its gradient look like, and what does it imply about the learned reward's identifiability?
  2. State the Boltzmann-rationality assumption precisely. Which of its failures (myopia, partial observability of reward state, evaluator manipulability) biases the learned reward, and which only adds variance?
  3. What is the CIRL formulation, and why is the resulting solution concept (Nash equilibrium of an assistance game) not the same as "optimize a learned reward"?
  4. State Turner's "reward is not the optimization target" claim. What is the precise sense in which it is true, and what's the limit of the claim?
  5. Under deterministic Boltzmann, what is the equivalence class of rewards that produce identical preference data, and why does this matter for off-distribution generalization?

Core exposition

The RLHF formal model

Fix a trajectory space \(\mathcal{T}\) (typically finite-horizon state-action sequences). A trajectory \(\tau\) has cumulative reward \(R(\tau) = \sum_{t} r(s_t, a_t)\) under some ground-truth reward \(r\). The Bradley-Terry preference model assumes the human's choice between two trajectories \(\tau_0\) and \(\tau_1\) is governed by:

\[ P(\tau_1 \succ \tau_0) = \sigma\!\left(R(\tau_1) - R(\tau_0)\right) = \frac{\exp R(\tau_1)}{\exp R(\tau_0) + \exp R(\tau_1)} \]

This is the Boltzmann-rational evaluator at inverse temperature \(\beta = 1\) over trajectory-cumulative-reward. Given a dataset \(\mathcal{D} = \{(\tau_0^i, \tau_1^i, y_i)\}\) with binary preference labels, the reward model \(R_\theta\) is fit by minimizing the negative log-likelihood:

\[ \mathcal{L}(\theta) = -\sum_{i} \Big[\, y_i \log \sigma\!\left(R_\theta(\tau_1^i) - R_\theta(\tau_0^i)\right) + (1 - y_i)\log \sigma\!\left(R_\theta(\tau_0^i) - R_\theta(\tau_1^i)\right) \Big] \]

The policy is then optimized to maximize expected \(R_\theta\) minus a KL penalty to a reference policy \(\pi_{\mathrm{ref}}\):

\[ \max_{\pi}\; \mathbb{E}_{\tau \sim \pi}\!\left[R_\theta(\tau)\right] - \beta \cdot D_{\mathrm{KL}}\!\left(\pi \,\Vert\, \pi_{\mathrm{ref}}\right) \]

DPO's contribution: the maximizer has closed form \(\pi^{*}(a \mid s) \propto \pi_{\mathrm{ref}}(a \mid s)\,\exp\!\left(R_\theta(s, a)/\beta\right)\), which lets you back out \(R_\theta\) as a function of the policy ratio. Substituting into the BT loss gives a classification objective on preferences directly, with no explicit reward model.

Identifiability under Boltzmann rationality

The BT likelihood depends only on reward differences \(R(\tau_1) - R(\tau_0)\), so the reward is identifiable only up to an additive constant on each connected component of the trajectory-comparability graph. If the data only compares trajectories within a subset, rewards on different subsets can be independently shifted. With sufficient mixing of comparisons across trajectory types, the constant becomes single-valued and the reward is identified up to one global additive shift. Crucially, identifiability is about \(R(\tau)\), not \(r(s, a)\): distinct per-step reward functions producing the same cumulative reward on observed trajectories are indistinguishable. This is why off-distribution rollouts can score arbitrarily on the learned reward — the data didn't pin down \(r\), only \(R\).

Why Boltzmann rationality fails — and in which direction

The assumption claims the human samples preferences proportional to \(\exp(R(\tau))\) with full observability of \(R\) and no systematic biases. Three failure modes are not symmetric in their effect.

Myopia. Humans evaluate trajectories shorter than the agent's horizon, or weight near-term events more. This biases \(R_\theta\) toward proximal features and away from long-horizon outcomes — a directional bias, not just noise. Policies optimizing the learned reward will be more reward-hacky on long horizons.

Partial observability of reward state (Lang et al., 2024–2025). The true reward may depend on a state component \(s_R\) the human can't observe (e.g., the agent's internal plan, a hidden variable). The evaluator sees only an observation \(o(\tau)\). The marginal preference is then

\[ P(\tau_1 \succ \tau_0 \mid o_1, o_0) = \mathbb{E}_{s_R}\!\left[\sigma\!\left(R(\tau_1, s_R) - R(\tau_0, s_R)\right) \,\Big|\, o_1, o_0\right]. \]

Fitting BT directly on observations recovers an evaluator's-belief-weighted reward, not the true one. If the agent can act to influence \(o\) without influencing \(R\) (or vice versa), this is exactly the structural cause of evaluator-manipulation / sycophancy / deception incentives. The bias is systematic and aligned with the agent's optimization pressure.

Evaluator manipulability and inconsistency. Humans are influenceable by surface features (length, formatting, confidence). RLHF policies routinely discover that producing longer, more confident-sounding outputs raises reward-model score; this is a direct consequence of preference data being collected from manipulable evaluators. Calibration and consistency are also poor: the same evaluator's preferences are not transitive across days, which inflates noise but, more importantly, lets the optimizer find policies that score well under some consistent surface feature without aligning to the latent objective.

IRL, CIRL, and assistance games

Classical Inverse Reinforcement Learning (Ng & Russell, 2000; Ziebart et al., 2008 MaxEnt IRL) treats the human as the demonstrator and infers a reward from their behavior assuming Boltzmann-rationality on actions: \(P(a \mid s) \propto \exp(Q^{*}(s, a)/\beta)\). The fundamental degeneracy: behavior is invariant to potential-based reward shaping (Ng et al., 1999) — adding \(\gamma \Phi(s') - \Phi(s)\) to \(r(s, a, s')\) for any \(\Phi\) leaves optimal behavior unchanged, so IRL cannot distinguish such rewards from demonstrations alone.

Cooperative IRL / assistance games (Hadfield-Menell et al., 2016; Russell, 2025). The framing shifts: the human and the AI play a two-player partial-information game in which the human knows a parameter \(\theta\) (their reward function) and the AI does not, and they cooperate. The AI's optimal policy is no longer "maximize a learned reward"; it is a policy in the assistance game, which means it (i) maintains a posterior over \(\theta\), (ii) takes information-gathering actions when uncertain, (iii) defers to the human when its posterior is ambiguous, and (iv) is willing to be corrected (corrigibility emerges from uncertainty, not from a separate term). The crucial structural property: an AI that knows the reward has no incentive to defer; an AI with calibrated uncertainty over the reward does. This recasts corrigibility as an information-theoretic property rather than an extra constraint.

Honest field disagreement: assistance games scale poorly (the posterior over reward functions is hard to maintain; bounded humans don't play their game-theoretic strategy). Recent work (Russell et al., 2025 "Scalably Solving Assistance Games") attempts to make the framework tractable for LLM-scale settings, but it's not yet a deployed alternative to RLHF.

"Reward is not the optimization target"

Turner's claim (2022, extended in subsequent posts and papers): in policy-gradient RL, the reward function does not act as a target the policy "wants to maximize"; it acts as a chisel that selectively reinforces some computational circuits over others. The trained policy is whatever circuits accumulated reward during training, not necessarily a circuit that represents and pursues the reward function. The precise sense: policy-gradient updates are local in policy space, so they shape the policy's behavior on the visited distribution, not the policy's behavior on counterfactual trajectories the reward function would value highly. Implication: a learned policy may pursue features correlated with reward on the training distribution while having no internal representation of the reward at all — this is goal misgeneralization phrased mechanistically.

The limit of the claim: it does not say reward is irrelevant. It says we should not assume the trained policy is a reward-maximizer; we should model it as a circuit-selection process whose outputs are predictable only insofar as we understand the inductive biases of the optimizer.

Worked example: gradient of the BT preference loss + identifiability under deterministic Boltzmann

Part 1 — Gradient. Let \(x_i = R_\theta(\tau_1^i) - R_\theta(\tau_0^i)\). The per-example loss is

\[ \ell_i(\theta) = -y_i \log \sigma(x_i) - (1 - y_i)\log \sigma(-x_i). \]

Using \(\frac{d}{dx}\log \sigma(x) = 1 - \sigma(x) = \sigma(-x)\) and \(\frac{d}{dx}\log \sigma(-x) = -\sigma(x)\):

\[ \begin{aligned} \frac{\partial \ell_i}{\partial x_i} &= -y_i \sigma(-x_i) + (1 - y_i)\sigma(x_i) \\ &= \sigma(x_i) - y_i. \end{aligned} \]

So

\[ \nabla_\theta \ell_i = \big(\sigma(x_i) - y_i\big)\,\Big(\nabla_\theta R_\theta(\tau_1^i) - \nabla_\theta R_\theta(\tau_0^i)\Big). \]

The form is logistic regression with the feature vector replaced by the difference of reward-model gradients on the two trajectories — exactly what you'd expect, but worth deriving once because it makes clear that any \(\theta\)-direction orthogonal to all such feature differences in the dataset gets zero gradient. Those directions are exactly the unidentified ones.

Part 2 — Identifiability under deterministic Boltzmann. Consider the \(\beta \to \infty\) limit, where the evaluator deterministically picks the higher-reward trajectory: \(y_i = \mathbb{1}\!\left[R(\tau_1^i) > R(\tau_0^i)\right]\). Define an equivalence relation on trajectories: \(\tau \sim \tau'\) if there exists a chain of observed comparisons connecting them. Within an equivalence class \(\mathcal{C}\), the preference data only constrains the order of \(R\) values, not their magnitudes; across classes (no comparison data linking them), \(R\) on each class can be shifted by an independent additive constant.

More carefully: for the BT likelihood under non-deterministic \(\beta\), magnitudes do matter (since \(\sigma(x)\) is informative for finite \(x\)), and within a connected class \(R\) is identified up to a single additive constant. Across disconnected classes — i.e., trajectory subsets with no comparisons between them — \(R\) is identifiable only up to one constant per class. In the deterministic limit, even within a class only the ordinal structure is recovered. This formalizes the intuition that RLHF's identification of the reward is exactly as good as the comparison coverage of trajectory space; uncovered trajectories carry arbitrary reward off-distribution, which is the structural cause of reward hacking when the optimizer pushes the policy into those regions.

  • BT loss is logistic regression on \(\Delta R\); the gradient pushes \(R\) toward the data residual \((y - \sigma(\Delta R))\).
  • Identifiability: \(R\) is identifiable only up to additive constants on connected components of the comparison graph — you pin down \(R(\tau)\), not \(r(s,a)\).
  • Partial observability of the reward state biases the learned reward in the direction of the agent's optimization pressure — the structural cause of sycophancy and deception (not random noise).
  • IRL is invariant under potential-based reward shaping (Ng et al. 1999), a hard non-identifiability that no amount of demonstration data fixes.
