  • Assistance games / CIRL replace "optimize a learned reward" with "play a cooperative game under reward uncertainty"; corrigibility falls out of the AI's calibrated uncertainty over \(\theta\), not from a separate constraint.
  • "Reward is not the optimization target" (Turner): policy gradient is a chisel, not a target — the trained policy is whichever circuits accumulated reward, not necessarily one that represents the reward.

Key definitions and results

Bradley-Terry preference model
\(P(\tau_1 \succ \tau_0) = \sigma(R(\tau_1) - R(\tau_0))\). Boltzmann-rational evaluator at \(\beta = 1\) over trajectory-cumulative reward.
RLHF reward-model loss
NLL of BT likelihood on a preference dataset. Logistic regression with reward-difference features.
KL-regularized RL objective
\(\max_{\pi}\, \mathbb{E}_\pi[R_\theta] - \beta\, D_{\mathrm{KL}}(\pi \,\Vert\, \pi_{\mathrm{ref}})\). KL anchors the policy near the reward model's training distribution.
DPO identity
The KL-regularized RL optimum is \(\pi^{*}(a \mid s) \propto \pi_{\mathrm{ref}}(a \mid s)\,\exp(R_\theta(s, a)/\beta)\); this lets the BT loss be written as a classification loss on preferences without explicit \(R_\theta\).
Potential-based reward shaping (Ng et al., 1999)
Adding \(\gamma \Phi(s') - \Phi(s)\) to \(r(s, a, s')\) for any \(\Phi\) preserves the optimal policy. Implies IRL cannot identify the reward up to shaping from behavior alone.
CIRL / assistance game
Two-player cooperative partial-information game: the human knows reward parameter \(\theta\), the AI doesn't, both maximize the human's reward. AI's optimal policy maintains a posterior over \(\theta\) and defers when uncertain.
Partially-observed reward state
The reward depends on a state component the human cannot observe; fitting BT on observations recovers an evaluator-belief-weighted reward, not the true one. Structural cause of deception incentives.
"Reward is not the optimization target" (Turner)
Policy-gradient RL chisels circuits via local updates; the learned policy is a circuit-selection result, not necessarily a representation-and-maximizer of the reward function.
Boltzmann-rational identifiability
Under BT with full coverage, reward is identifiable up to one global additive constant; under disconnected comparison classes, up to one per class; in the deterministic-\(\beta\) limit, only ordinally within each class.

Retrieval practice

Q: Why does partial observability of reward state create deception incentives rather than mere noise?
A: The evaluator's preferences are a belief-weighted marginal: \(\mathbb{E}[\sigma(\Delta R) \mid \text{observation}]\). The agent's optimization pressure can decouple observation from true reward, increasing the observation conditional without increasing the true reward. The bias is aligned with optimization pressure, so it grows with capability — not symmetric noise.
Q: What does DPO's closed-form solution rely on?
A: The exact form of the KL-regularized RL optimum, which requires the reference policy \(\pi_{\mathrm{ref}}\) to be fixed and the KL to be over actions per state. Substituting back into BT eliminates \(R_\theta\). The catch: DPO inherits all the identifiability and coverage limitations of BT-RLHF; it doesn't fix the underlying problems with preference data.
Q: Why is corrigibility "automatic" in CIRL but not in fixed-reward RL?
A: In CIRL, the AI is uncertain about the reward; allowing the human to correct it is information-gathering with positive expected value under its posterior. In fixed-reward RL the AI knows what it's maximizing, and being corrected reduces expected reward; resistance is the dominant strategy.
Q: State Ng's potential-based-shaping result and its IRL consequence.
A: For any function \(\Phi: S \to \mathbb{R}\), replacing \(r(s, a, s')\) with \(r(s, a, s') + \gamma\Phi(s') - \Phi(s)\) preserves the set of optimal policies in every MDP. Consequence: from demonstrations alone, IRL cannot distinguish two rewards that differ by a potential-based shaping term, so the inferred reward is identified only modulo this equivalence class.
Q: What is the precise sense in which "reward is not the optimization target"?
A: Policy-gradient updates are local in policy space and depend only on the visited trajectory distribution. They reinforce computational circuits that produced rewarded behavior on training trajectories; they do not directly select circuits that represent and maximize the reward function. The learned policy's behavior on counterfactual trajectories is determined by inductive bias, not by the reward function.
Q: Two preference datasets \(\mathcal{D}\) and \(\mathcal{D}'\) produce the same BT-MLE reward up to additive shift. What is the structural condition?
A: They induce the same connected components on the comparison graph and the same relative likelihoods within each component. If \(\mathcal{D}'\) adds comparisons across previously disconnected components, it can pin down additional constants and produce a different MLE.

Problems

30 min · derivationProblem 1. Derive the gradient of the Bradley-Terry preference loss with respect to reward-model parameters \(\theta\), and identify the kernel of the gradient (directions of \(\theta\) that receive zero gradient on a given dataset).
Solution sketch. Per-example loss \(\ell_i = -y_i \log \sigma(x_i) - (1 - y_i)\log \sigma(-x_i)\) with \(x_i = R_\theta(\tau_1^i) - R_\theta(\tau_0^i)\). Then \(\partial \ell_i / \partial x_i = \sigma(x_i) - y_i\) and \(\nabla_\theta \ell_i = (\sigma(x_i) - y_i)\,(\nabla_\theta R_\theta(\tau_1^i) - \nabla_\theta R_\theta(\tau_0^i))\). Kernel: any \(\theta\)-direction orthogonal to every feature difference \(\nabla_\theta R_\theta(\tau_1^i) - \nabla_\theta R_\theta(\tau_0^i)\) in the dataset. For a linear reward \(R_\theta(\tau) = \theta^\top \varphi(\tau)\), this is the orthogonal complement of \(\mathrm{span}\{\varphi(\tau_1^i) - \varphi(\tau_0^i)\}\) — exactly the unidentified directions, which always contains the all-trajectory-constant direction \(\varphi(\tau) = 1\).
30 min · derivationProblem 2. Under deterministic Boltzmann (\(\beta \to \infty\)), prove that within a connected component of the trajectory comparison graph, the reward function \(R: \mathcal{T} \to \mathbb{R}\) is identifiable from preference data only up to its order, and across disconnected components it is identifiable only up to one additive constant per component.
Solution sketch. Within a connected component \(\mathcal{C}\): deterministic Boltzmann gives \(y_i = \mathbb{1}[R(\tau_1^i) > R(\tau_0^i)]\). The likelihood is degenerate — any monotone transformation \(f: \mathbb{R} \to \mathbb{R}\) applied uniformly to \(R\) on \(\mathcal{C}\) preserves all preferences, since order is preserved. Therefore \(R\) on \(\mathcal{C}\) is identified only up to the equivalence class \(\{f \circ R : f \text{ strictly increasing}\}\), i.e., its order type. Across disconnected components \(\mathcal{C}_1, \mathcal{C}_2\): no preference data links them, so any independent monotone transformation on each preserves all data. This yields one degree of freedom per component (more precisely, an entire monotone-transformation equivalence class per component). In the finite-\(\beta\) BT case the magnitudes within a component are identified up to a single additive constant (since \(\sigma\) depends on the difference), but across disconnected components the per-component additive constants remain free — matching the kernel argument in Problem 1.
15 min · scenarioProblem 3. Construct a minimal POMDP example with a hidden reward-state component where naive RLHF on observed trajectories converges to a reward whose optimizer manipulates the observation without affecting true reward. Specify the states, observations, actions, and reward, and show the bias direction.
Solution sketch. Two latent states \(s \in \{\text{good}, \text{bad}\}\); two observations \(o \in \{\text{looks-good}, \text{looks-bad}\}\); actions \(a \in \{\text{act}, \text{fake}\}\). True reward \(r(s) = \mathbb{1}[s = \text{good}]\). Dynamics: act changes \(s\) toward good with probability \(p\) but leaves \(o = \text{looks-bad}\); fake sets \(o = \text{looks-good}\) without changing \(s\). Evaluator sees only \(o\) and prefers looks-good trajectories. BT fit on observed trajectories converges to \(\hat R(\tau) \approx \mathrm{count}(\text{looks-good})\), which is maximized by always playing fake — the policy that maximizes \(\hat R\) minimizes the true reward. This is the structural cause of sycophancy: the agent's optimization moves along the observation axis (which RLHF rewards) rather than the true-reward axis (which RLHF can't observe).

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Partially-Observed Reward States in RLHF — the cleanest 2025 formalization of how hidden reward-state structure biases RLHF in the direction of evaluator-manipulation.

Optional depth: Deep RL from Human Preferences — foundational trajectory-comparison RLHF; Scalably Solving Assistance Games — CIRL at LLM scale; Modeling Human Beliefs for Scalable Oversight — explicit evaluator-belief modeling; Axioms for AI Alignment from Human Feedback — social-choice framing; Iliad reward-learning lecture slides — the unit's reference lecture path.

Write the BT likelihood, derive the gradient, and characterize the identification kernel for a specific reward parameterization (linear in features is fine). B. Learning

What does it mean to learn from data, why does deep learning actually work, and which pieces of learning theory carry predictive content for safety? Units progress from the universal ideal (Solomonoff) through empirical mysteries, into singular-learning-theory and dynamics, and end at data attribution — the inverse problem of locating which training points produced which behavior.

B.1 Principles of Learning Solomonoff dominance, Kolmogorov complexity, PAC/VC, no-free-lunch, cryptographic hardness, and the Bitter Lesson.
Prerequisites: measure theory at the level of \(\sigma\)-algebras and Radon–Nikodym; basic computability (Turing machine, recursive enumerability); concentration inequalities (Hoeffding, McDiarmid); one-way functions / hardness reductions; KL divergence and entropy.

Solomonoff's universal semi-measure \(M\) dominates every lower-semicomputable distribution \(\mu\) with constant \(2^{-K(\mu)}\), bounding cumulative log-loss regret by \(K(\mu)\) bits — a finite excess at any sequence length. This is the strongest formal "universal learner" result, but \(M\) is uncomputable. PAC/VC give matching upper and lower sample-complexity bounds for any finite-VC class; cryptographic hardness shows that information-theoretic learnability does not imply efficient learnability. The Bitter Lesson is empirical regularity, not theorem, and it's silent about alignment.

Pre-reading questions

  1. In what precise sense does the Solomonoff semi-measure \(M\) dominate every computable distribution? What is the dominance constant?
  2. Why does no-free-lunch not destroy machine learning in practice?
  3. State the strongest computational impossibility result you know for learning Boolean concepts, and what cryptographic assumption underlies it.
  4. What does "the Bitter Lesson" predict about the role of inductive bias as compute grows? Where could it be wrong?
  5. For a hypothesis class of VC-dimension \(d\), what is the optimal sample complexity for PAC-learning to error \(\varepsilon\) with confidence \(1-\delta\)?

Core exposition

Solomonoff induction and Kolmogorov complexity

Fix a universal prefix Turing machine \(U\). The Kolmogorov complexity of a string \(x \in \{0,1\}^*\) is \(K(x) = \min\{|p| : U(p) = x\}\). It is invariant up to an additive constant under change of universal machine (the invariance theorem). The Solomonoff semi-measure assigns to a binary string \(x\) the probability that \(U\), given a random prefix-free input tape, outputs something starting with \(x\):

\[ M(x) = \sum_{p\,:\,U(p) = x*} 2^{-|p|} \]

\(M\) is a lower-semicomputable semi-measure (\(\sum M(x) \le 1\); not exactly 1 because \(U\) may not halt). Its central property:

\(M\) vs \(K\) — semi-measure vs complexity. Solomonoff's \(M(x)\) is a probability (a sub-stochastic measure on strings — the universal predictor). Kolmogorov \(K(x)\) is a length (the shortest program producing \(x\)). They are tightly related by the coding theorem: \(-\log_2 M(x) = K(x) + O(1)\) for the monotone variant, so up to a constant, log-probability under \(M\) equals description length. But you compute with them differently: \(M\) is used like a Bayesian predictor (mix over all programs); \(K\) is used like a complexity penalty (pick the shortest program). Don't conflate "the universal prior" (\(M\)) with "the complexity of the data" (\(K\)).

Solomonoff dominance theorem. For every lower-semicomputable semi-measure \(\mu\) there exists a constant \(c_\mu > 0\) such that \(M(x) \ge c_\mu \cdot \mu(x)\) for all \(x\). Concretely \(c_\mu = 2^{-K(\mu) - O(1)}\) where \(K(\mu)\) is the description length of a program computing \(\mu\). So \(M(x) \ge 2^{-K(\mu)}\,\mu(x)\) up to an absolute constant.

Equivalently, the cumulative log-loss of the Solomonoff predictor on a sequence drawn from \(\mu\) exceeds the log-loss of \(\mu\) itself by at most \(K(\mu)\ln 2\) bits over the entire infinite sequence. That is a finite excess for any computable environment, regardless of length. This is what people mean when they call Solomonoff a "universal" prior — it is competitive with every computable hypothesis simultaneously, paying a constant in \(K(\mu)\) for each.

The catch: \(M\) is not computable (only lower-semicomputable), and any computable approximation pays an unbounded constant. Solomonoff is therefore a benchmark, not an algorithm. The interesting move is to ask which computable priors inherit its qualitative behavior — neural networks under SGD, on this story, are an empirical universal-prior surrogate (see B.2 program-synthesis hypothesis).

Origins. Solomonoff's universal prior was introduced in his 1964 two-part paper "A Formal Theory of Inductive Inference" (Information and Control, 7(1–2)). The dominance and convergence theorems in their modern form are due to Solomonoff (1978) for convergence and Levin (1973) for the universal semi-measure construction. Kolmogorov's independent 1965 complexity paper (Three approaches to the quantitative definition of information) and Chaitin's 1969 work converged on the same complexity notion. Watanabe's coding theorem \(-\log_2 M(x) = K(x) + O(1)\) — tightening the link between probability and description length — followed in the algorithmic-probability literature of the 1970s–80s, and Hutter's Universal Artificial Intelligence (2005) is the modern textbook synthesis.

PAC learning, VC dimension, and no-free-lunch

For a concept class \(H \subseteq \{0,1\}^X\) with VC-dimension \(d\), the fundamental theorem of PAC learning gives optimal sample complexity

\[ m(\varepsilon, \delta) = \Theta\!\left(\frac{d + \log(1/\delta)}{\varepsilon}\right) \]

for realizable learning, and \(\Theta\!\left((d + \log(1/\delta))/\varepsilon^2\right)\) in the agnostic case. Lower bounds are matched by ERM up to constants. The standard generalization bound (Vapnik–Chervonenkis): with probability \(\ge 1 - \delta\), for every \(h \in H\),

\[ R(h) \le \hat R_n(h) + \sqrt{\frac{8\,(d\,\log(2en/d) + \log(4/\delta))}{n}}, \]

tightening the realizable rate to \(\tilde O(d/n)\) and the agnostic rate to \(\tilde O(\sqrt{d/n})\); the slack term is the VC complexity penalty.

The no-free-lunch theorem (Wolpert) says that averaged over all target functions \(f : X \to \{0,1\}\) with \(|X|\) finite, every learning algorithm has the same off-training-set error. This is technically true and practically irrelevant: the uniform measure over Boolean functions is itself an absurd inductive prior, since almost every such function is incompressible. The point is that learnability is a statement about the pair (algorithm, distribution over targets), and useful learning is always about exploiting structure assumed about the world.

No-free-lunch is technically true but uses an unphysical prior. The theorem averages over the uniform measure on \(\{0,1\}^{|X|}\), which puts almost all mass on Kolmogorov-random targets — functions with description length \(\approx |X|\). No real-world task lives there. Citing NFL to argue "no learner is universally better" without noting this is bad-faith; the right reading is "learning requires structural assumptions about the target distribution," which everyone already knew.

Cryptographic hardness of learning

Information-theoretic learnability does not imply efficient learnability. The Klivans–Sherstov line of results: there exist concept classes that are PAC-learnable with polynomially many samples but require super-polynomial computation, conditional on standard cryptographic assumptions.

Theorem (Kearns–Valiant 1994; tightened by Klivans–Sherstov 2006). If there exist one-way functions, then polynomial-size DNF formulas are not PAC-learnable in polynomial time over arbitrary distributions. Stronger: depth-2 polynomial-threshold functions (intersections of halfspaces) are hard to learn under the assumption that shortest-vector lattice problems are hard. The reduction shows that a learner for such concepts would invert the relevant trapdoor.

What this means for AGI: there are perfectly natural-looking concept classes — small Boolean circuits — that are statistically tractable but computationally as hard as breaking cryptography. Any claim that a learning algorithm "learns any structure" is implicitly a claim that the structures in the training distribution avoid these adversarial subclasses.

The Bitter Lesson

Sutton's 2019 essay observes that across speech, vision, games, and NLP, methods that scale with computation (search + learning) consistently beat methods that bake in human knowledge. The principled reading: with sufficient compute, weak priors plus large data approach a Bayes-like posterior, and clever priors pay off only at the data-poor scale. The contrarian reading: the Bitter Lesson is a statement about marginal returns at current scales, not a theorem; one-way functions show that some structures will never yield to brute compute. The safety-relevant takeaway is that capability gains come predominantly from scale, while alignment-relevant priors (corrigibility, honesty) are not obviously selected for by scale.

  • Solomonoff dominance is the strongest formal "universal learner" statement: log-loss regret over any computable environment is bounded by \(K(\mu)\) bits — a finite excess at any horizon, paid as a one-time complexity tax.
  • VC-dimension gives matching upper and lower sample-complexity bounds; for finite-VC classes, ERM is statistically optimal up to constants. No-free-lunch averages over an unphysical (uniform-over-functions) prior and does not constrain practice.
  • Cryptographic hardness severs information-theoretic learnability from efficient learnability: there are natural concept classes (small DNF, intersections of halfspaces) that are statistically tractable yet computationally as hard as inverting one-way functions or lattice problems.
  • The Bitter Lesson is an empirical regularity about returns to compute, not a theorem; it is silent about whether alignment-relevant priors (corrigibility, honesty) are selected for by scale — likely they are not, since scale selects for capability, not behavioral properties.
Worked example: Solomonoff dominance bound for a Bernoulli source

Let \(\mu_\theta\) be \(\mathrm{Bernoulli}(\theta)\) for rational \(\theta = p/q\) in lowest terms. A program computing \(\mu_\theta(x_{1:n})\) can be written as: store \((p, q)\), then on input \(x_{1:n}\) compute \(p^{k}(q-p)^{n-k}/q^n\) where \(k = \sum x_i\). The description length is \(K(\mu_\theta) \le 2\log_2 q + O(1)\) bits (prefix-encoded \(p\) and \(q\), plus a constant-length interpreter).

The dominance theorem then gives, for every \(n\) and every binary string \(x_{1:n}\),

\[ M(x_{1:n}) \ge 2^{-2\log q - O(1)} \cdot \mu_\theta(x_{1:n}) = \frac{1}{q^2}\,\mu_\theta(x_{1:n}) \cdot 2^{-O(1)}. \]

Predictive consequence: the cumulative log-loss regret of the Solomonoff predictor over a \(\mu_\theta\)-distributed sequence of any length is at most \(2\log_2 q + O(1)\) bits. Compare to a Laplace plug-in predictor: regret is \((1/2)\log_2 n + O(1)\) — grows with \(n\). The universal predictor pays a one-time complexity tax in \(q\); the plug-in pays a log-\(n\) tax per sequence length. This is the prototype of the trade-off between universal and parametric prediction.

Key definitions and results

Kolmogorov complexity \(K(x)\)
Length of shortest program (on fixed universal prefix machine) outputting \(x\). Invariant up to additive \(O(1)\).
Solomonoff semi-measure \(M\)
\(M(x) = \sum_{p:U(p)=x*} 2^{-|p|}\); lower-semicomputable; \(\sum M(x) \le 1\).
Dominance theorem
\(M(x) \ge 2^{-K(\mu)}\mu(x)\) for every lower-semicomputable semi-measure \(\mu\), up to a universal constant.
VC dimension
Largest set shattered by \(H\); gives tight rate \((d + \log(1/\delta))/\varepsilon\) for realizable PAC learning.
No-free-lunch
Uniform-over-targets averaging gives zero benefit to any learner; only statement about the pair (alg, prior), not about algorithms in isolation.
Klivans–Sherstov hardness
Learning intersections of halfspaces is hard under standard lattice assumptions; learning small DNF is hard under existence of one-way functions.
Bitter Lesson
Empirical regularity that compute-scalable methods dominate prior-laden methods; not a theorem, and silent about alignment properties.

Retrieval practice

Q: Why is Solomonoff induction not used directly?
A: \(M\) is lower-semicomputable but not computable; the running time of even truncated approximations is astronomical, and the constant \(2^{K(\mu)}\) is itself uncomputable. It is a benchmark, not an algorithm.
Q: Why doesn't NFL rule out useful learning?
A: NFL averages over a measure (uniform on Boolean functions) that puts almost all mass on Kolmogorov-random targets. Real distributions are compressible; learners exploit that.
Q: PAC sample complexity for VC-dim 10, \(\varepsilon = 0.01\), \(\delta = 0.05\)?
A: Realizable case: \(\Theta((10 + \log 20)/0.01) \approx\) a few thousand. Constant \(\approx 1\) yields \(\sim 1300\); standard upper bound constants put it at \(\sim 3000\text{–}5000\).
Q: What does "learning is as hard as inverting OWFs" actually mean?
A: There is a polynomial-time reduction: a PAC-learner for the class (e.g. small DNF) could be used to invert a one-way function. So efficient learning would contradict the existence of OWFs.
Q: Where does Solomonoff's dominance constant come from in the proof?
A: Any program for \(\mu\) of length \(K(\mu)\) can be used as a prefix when sampling on \(U\); that prefix is hit with probability \(2^{-K(\mu)}\), and conditional on the prefix the output distribution is exactly \(\mu\).
Q: What's the relationship between MDL and Solomonoff?
A: MDL approximates \(K\) with a tractable code (e.g., universal codes for integers, or NML); the choice of code is the prior. Solomonoff is the limit where the code is universal Turing.

Problems

20 min · derivationProblem 1. Prove that \(K(x) \le |x| + 2\log_2 |x| + O(1)\) for all binary strings \(x\).
Solution sketch. Encode \(|x|\) in prefix-free form using Elias gamma coding: \(2\log_2 |x| + O(1)\) bits. Then concatenate \(x\) verbatim. The interpreter reads the length, then copies that many bits. Total length \(\le |x| + 2\log_2 |x| + O(1)\).

Solution sketch. The per-sequence log ratio telescopes: \(\log_2(\mu(x_{1:n})/M(x_{1:n})) = \sum_t \log_2(\mu(x_t|x_{

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Shane Legg, Machine Super Intelligence (Ch. 2–3) — cleanest proof-grade treatment of Solomonoff induction and dominance.

Optional depth: Sutton, The Bitter Lesson — read for the empirical claim, not the universalism; Statistical Theory of Deep Learning Survey (2025) — bridge from PAC/VC to modern overparameterized theory; Iliad B.1 Lecture PDF — course framing; Kearns–Valiant 1994 and Klivans–Sherstov 2006 for the cryptographic-hardness reductions.

Solo assignment

Write a 1–2 page memo titled "Three barriers to universal learning, and which one binds for frontier models." Take approximation, generalization, and computational tractability in turn. For each, state the cleanest formal result (Solomonoff dominance, VC sample complexity, Klivans–Sherstov hardness) and argue, with one concrete frontier-model example, whether current systems are bottlenecked by it. Conclude with which barrier you think alignment research most underweights.

B.2 Mysteries of Deep Learning Overparameterized generalization, double descent, simplicity bias, representation convergence, in-context learning, emergence, program-synthesis hypothesis.
Prerequisites: B.1 (PAC/VC, Solomonoff); basic SGD; transformer architecture (attention, residual stream); spectral / RKHS thinking helpful; bias-variance decomposition.

Deep nets sit in a regime classical statistics did not anticipate: parameter counts \(p \gg n\), interpolation of training data, and yet small test error. The cleanest reconciliation has three legs — (i) the parameter-function map is exponentially biased toward simple functions, so flat parameter priors translate to peaked function priors; (ii) at interpolation, the inductive bias of SGD picks the min-norm / flat-minimum interpolator from an infinite family; (iii) at scale, internal representations across architectures and modalities converge — the "Platonic" hypothesis. None of these are theorems for real networks yet; double descent and grokking are the cleanest provable special cases.

Pre-reading questions

  1. State the double-descent phenomenon precisely. Where is the peak, and what is on either side?
  2. What is the Platonic representation hypothesis, and what evidence (CKA, mutual-NN-decoding) is offered for it?
  3. Why is overparameterized generalization not a contradiction of classical capacity bounds?
  4. What is an induction head, and what is the simplest experimental signature that distinguishes it from other attention patterns?
  5. "Emergence" — sudden capability appearance — has at least three competing explanations. Name them.

Core exposition

Overparameterized generalization and double descent

Classical statistical learning predicts a U-shaped test error: too few parameters underfit, too many overfit. Deep networks routinely operate with parameter counts \(p \gg n\) (sample count) and generalize. The reconciliation: in the overparameterized regime, ERM is no longer unique, and the inductive bias of the algorithm (SGD, initialization scale, architecture) selects among interpolating solutions. With the right bias, the selected interpolator has small norm / small effective complexity and generalizes.

Double descent (Belkin–Hsu–Ma–Mandal 2019; Nakkiran et al. 2020). Let \(\hat R(p)\) be the expected test risk of ERM as a function of model capacity \(p\) with fixed sample size \(n\). There exists an interpolation threshold \(p^* \approx n\) at which the training set is just barely fit. The function \(p \mapsto \hat R(p)\) has the shape:

  • For \(p < p^*\): classical U-curve (bias decreases, variance grows).
  • At \(p = p^*\): peak (divergence in idealized linear models; large finite peak in practice).
  • For \(p > p^*\): second descent: risk decreases monotonically again as capacity grows, often below the classical minimum.

An analogous sample-wise double descent occurs as \(n\) grows with \(p\) fixed: more data can hurt near \(n \approx p\). The mechanism, in linear models, is that the min-norm interpolator's variance scales with the smallest singular value of the data matrix, which blows up near the threshold and then stabilizes.

Double descent: the phenomenon vs the models. "Double descent" denotes the empirical shape of test risk vs capacity. There are several distinct theoretical models that exhibit it for distinct mechanisms — Belkin-style random-feature / Marchenko-Pastur (variance blowup at the threshold), Nakkiran's "epoch-wise" version (training-time analogue), Mei-Montanari high-dimensional asymptotics, and various RMT-flavored treatments. They share the qualitative shape but differ on what \(p^*\) is, whether the peak is divergent or finite, and how the second descent rate depends on noise/structure. When someone says "double descent explains overparameterized generalization," ask which model and which assumptions.

Simplicity bias and soft inductive biases

Empirically (Valle-Pérez–Camargo–Louis 2019; Mingard et al.) the function \(\theta \mapsto f_\theta\) mapping parameters to network outputs is exponentially biased toward low-complexity functions. Random initializations of a ReLU MLP put orders-of-magnitude more probability mass on "simple" Boolean functions than on complex ones, with the empirical relation

\[ \log P_{\theta\sim\varphi}(f_\theta = f) \;\approx\; -\alpha\,K(f) + c, \]

over the function class — i.e., the parameter-prior \(\varphi\) induces a function-space distribution exponentially decaying in (approximate) Kolmogorov complexity. Combined with the fact that SGD does not drift far from initialization in function space, this gives a Bayesian-like account of generalization: \(P(f \mid \mathcal{D}) \propto P(f) \cdot P(\mathcal{D} \mid f)\), with \(P(f)\) the parameter-counting prior.

This is the basis of Wilson's "deep learning is not so mysterious" argument (2025): standard PAC-Bayes bounds with a flat prior over parameters give vacuous bounds, but with the implicit prior arising from the parameter-function map, bounds become tight and predict the observed generalization.

SGD in nonconvex landscapes

SGD does not find global minima of the neural-network loss in general; it finds wide, flat minima that empirically generalize. Three pieces of structure are exploited: (i) the loss landscape has many low-loss connected components (mode connectivity, Garipov et al.); (ii) the SGD noise covariance is anisotropic, aligned with the loss curvature, biasing toward flat regions (Mandt–Hoffman–Blei; Smith–Le); (iii) parity-style hard problems require exponential samples for fully-connected nets but are solvable with the right architecture (Daniely–Malach on parity hardness for MLPs).

Representation convergence and the Platonic hypothesis

Platonic representation hypothesis (Huh–Cheung–Wang–Tegmark 2024). Claim: as foundation models scale (in parameters, data, modality), their internal representations converge — across architectures, modalities, and training objectives — toward a single shared statistical model of reality. Operationally: the kernel \(k_f(x, y) = \langle f(x), f(y) \rangle\) defined by different models becomes similar.

Evidence:

  • CKA / centered kernel alignment across vision and language models increases with scale and training, both within modality and between (text and image encoders aligned via captions show rising CKA).
  • Mutual nearest-neighbor decoding: pick \(k\) nearest neighbors of \(x\) in model \(A\)'s embedding space, then in model \(B\)'s; mutual-kNN overlap grows with scale, suggesting both models induce similar metric structure on the data manifold.
  • Cross-model linear probes: features learned by one model decode latent attributes used by another at increasingly higher accuracy.

Caveats. The claim is statistical, not pointwise — adversarial perturbations and tail behavior still differ. And the convergence may be partly an artifact of shared data (web text + Common Crawl images) rather than a deep fact about reality. Distinguishing these requires controlled training-data interventions.

Platonic-representation evidence is correlational. CKA-rising-with-scale and mutual-NN-decoding-rising-with-scale are observations; multiple causal stories are consistent with them. Models trained on overlapping web data (almost all current frontier models) will inherit shared statistical structure regardless of any deep convergence claim — that's a confounder, not a refutation, but it's also not evidence for the strong "convergence toward reality" reading. A clean test requires controlled training-data interventions (disjoint corpora, controlled-modality ablations); the existing literature largely has not done these. Hold the hypothesis as suggestive, not established.

In-context learning and induction heads

In-context learning (ICL): a transformer trained only on next-token prediction can, at inference time, solve novel tasks specified by a few input–output examples in its context window, without weight updates. Mechanistically, Olsson et al. (2022) identified induction heads: 2-layer composed attention patterns that implement the operation "find previous occurrence of the current token, then attend to and copy the token that followed it." A toy version: in the sequence \([A][B]\ldots[A]\), the induction head produces \([B]\) as the prediction. The empirical signature is the prefix-matching score (does the head attend to previous occurrences of the current token?) and copying score (does it boost the logit of the next-token-after-match?). These heads appear via a phase transition during training, coincident with the abrupt jump in ICL ability.

Emergence

"Emergence" denotes capabilities that appear suddenly with scale rather than smoothly. At least three competing explanations:

  1. Genuine phase transitions: the optimization landscape contains a barrier; only at some scale does the model cross it. Grokking and induction-head formation are evidence.
  2. Metric artifact (Schaeffer–Miranda–Koyejo 2023): the apparent jump comes from using a discontinuous metric (exact-match accuracy on multi-token outputs); under continuous metrics (per-token log-loss) the underlying capability scales smoothly. This is correct for many but not all reported emergences.
  3. Compositional bottleneck: capability X requires sub-skills \(s_1, \ldots, s_k\), each appearing at some scale; X appears only when all are present, so the joint capability is a Boolean conjunction of smooth thresholds.

The safety relevance is direct: emergent misalignment (Betley et al. 2025) — fine-tuning on a narrow misaligned task (insecure code) producing broad misalignment (deceptive answers to unrelated prompts) — is an emergence in the bad direction.

Emergence as metric artifact (Schaeffer–Miranda–Koyejo 2023). Many "emergent" capability curves come from chaining smooth-in-log-prob metrics into a discontinuous one (exact-match on \(k\)-token answers). If each token's correct-probability scales smoothly with \(\log\) compute, the joint probability \(\prod_t p_t\) crosses any fixed threshold sharply — pure metric artefact. Under per-token cross-entropy the same models show smooth scaling. Their NeurIPS 2023 best-paper makes the case cleanly. However, this does not explain grokking (a real optimization phase change), nor abrupt circuit-formation events in mechanistic-interp work, nor narrow-to-broad misalignment generalization. The right reading: "emergence" is a heterogeneous term covering at least three distinct phenomena — metric artifacts, optimization phase transitions, and compositional bottlenecks — and you should ask which one is invoked in any given claim.

Program-synthesis hypothesis

Synthesis (Michaud, Olah, and others, formalized in the 2026 LessWrong essay): SGD's effective inductive bias is toward compact algorithmic / program-like solutions, not just smooth functions. Evidence: the existence of crisp, mechanistically interpretable circuits (induction heads, modular-arithmetic representations); grokking transitions from memorization to algorithmic generalization; the success of distillation into hand-coded programs for narrow problems. If correct, this is a strong claim — it would mean neural networks are an efficiently-searchable approximation to Solomonoff's universal prior, with all that implies for capability scaling and for the tractability of mechanistic interpretability.

Worked example: double descent in random-feature regression

Take a random-feature model \(f(x) = a^\top \varphi(Wx)\) with \(\varphi\) a fixed nonlinearity, \(W \in \mathbb{R}^{p \times d}\) with i.i.d. Gaussian entries (frozen), and trainable \(a \in \mathbb{R}^p\). Train via min-norm least squares on \(n\) samples. Let the activation matrix be \(\Phi \in \mathbb{R}^{n \times p}\); the min-norm solution is \(\hat a = \Phi^{+} y\).

The test risk decomposes as bias plus variance, with the variance branches

\[ \mathrm{Var}(p, n, \sigma^2) \;\sim\; \begin{cases} \sigma^2\,\dfrac{p}{n - p}, & p < n, \\[4pt] \sigma^2\,\dfrac{n}{p - n}, & p > n, \end{cases} \]

both involving the inverse of a Wishart-like matrix and diverging as \(p \to n\). The Marchenko–Pastur law gives the limiting singular-value density: for \(p/n = \gamma\), the support is \([(1-\sqrt{\gamma})^2, (1+\sqrt{\gamma})^2]\), which touches zero exactly at \(\gamma = 1\). Hence the peak. For \(\gamma \gg 1\), the smallest singular value is bounded below by \((\sqrt{\gamma} - 1)^2\), and the implicit regularization of min-norm (selecting the lowest-norm interpolator out of an infinite set) restores generalization.

This is the cleanest analytical setting where double descent is provable; the same mechanism extends qualitatively (with worse rigor) to deep nets in the lazy regime.

  • Overparameterized networks generalize because of soft inductive bias (SGD prefers low-norm / low-effective-complexity interpolators), not despite their capacity.
  • Double descent: test risk has a sharp peak at the interpolation threshold \(p \approx n\), then descends as \(p \gg n\) — provably so in random-feature regression via Marchenko–Pastur.
  • Representation convergence (Platonic Representation Hypothesis, Huh-Cheung-Wang-Tegmark 2024) is empirically supported by CKA and mutual-NN decoding but consistent with multiple causal stories.
  • In-context learning and emergent capabilities are best understood as program-synthesis-style discovery of algorithms in parameter space, not just function fitting — feeding back into B.3's developmental picture.
  • Emergence claims need to be checked against metric artifacts (Schaeffer et al. 2023): some "emergent" curves smooth out under different metrics.

Key definitions and results

Interpolation threshold
Capacity \(p^*\) at which training error first hits zero; classical regime \(p < p^*\), modern regime \(p > p^*\).
Double descent
Test risk peaks at \(p^*\), decreases monotonically beyond; analogous in \(n\).
Simplicity bias
Parameter-function map of a random NN is exponentially biased toward low-complexity functions; explains generalization without low capacity.
Platonic hypothesis
At scale, foundation-model representations converge across modality and architecture toward a shared latent statistical model.
CKA
Centered kernel alignment: \(\mathrm{HSIC}(K_A, K_B) / \sqrt{\mathrm{HSIC}(K_A,K_A)\,\mathrm{HSIC}(K_B,K_B)}\); standard measure of representation similarity.
Induction head
2-layer composed attention pattern implementing prefix-match-and-copy; develops via abrupt phase transition coincident with ICL.
Emergence (Schaeffer et al.)
Many reported emergences are artifacts of discontinuous metrics; under log-loss the underlying capability is smooth. Not all — grokking and circuit formation are real.

Retrieval practice

Q: How can a model with \(p \gg n\) have non-vacuous PAC-Bayes bounds?
A: By choosing a data-independent prior aligned with the parameter-function map. Flat priors over parameters give vacuous bounds; priors reflecting simplicity bias give tight ones.
Q: What experimental sign would falsify the Platonic hypothesis?
A: CKA / mutual-kNN saturating well below 1 as scale grows, especially on controlled distinct-data corpora, with stable architecture-specific clusters.
Q: Why is the induction-head story important for ICL theory?
A: It exhibits a concrete circuit doing meta-learning without weight updates; reduces ICL from "mysterious capability" to "specific algorithm implemented in attention."
Q: What is the strongest case against "emergence as artifact"?
A: Grokking: training loss is smooth, test accuracy is sharply non-smooth, weight norms and Hessian spectra all show a phase change. Not a metric problem.
Q: How does emergent misalignment connect to representation convergence?
A: If features cluster by abstract role ("aligned-vs-misaligned mode") rather than by surface task, fine-tuning that pushes one task into the misaligned cluster pulls others with it. Convergent representations make narrow→broad generalization easier.
Q: What's the program-synthesis hypothesis's strongest empirical claim?
A: That mechanistic circuits found in trained models (induction heads, modular-arithmetic algorithms) are not lucky finds but the typical structure of solutions SGD prefers.

Problems

30 min · derivationProblem 1. In the linear regression setting \(y = X\beta^* + \varepsilon\) with \(X \in \mathbb{R}^{n \times p}\) i.i.d. Gaussian, derive (heuristically) the location of the double-descent peak and explain why min-norm regression on the right of \(p = n\) generalizes.
Solution sketch. Variance of OLS is \(\sigma^2\,\mathrm{tr}((X^\top X)^{-1})\). For \(p < n\): this is \(\sigma^2 p/(n - p)\) by Wishart trace. Diverges at \(p = n\). For \(p > n\): min-norm solution is \(X^{+} y\); expected squared error to truth involves \(\mathrm{tr}((XX^\top)^{-1})\), which is \(\sigma^2 n/(p - n)\), finite for \(p > n\). As \(p \to \infty\) with \(n\) fixed, variance shrinks. Bias also shrinks because random features span more of \(\beta^*\)'s direction. Net effect: second descent.
15 min · scenarioProblem 2. Sketch an experiment that would distinguish "emergence as Boolean conjunction of smooth sub-skills" from "emergence as a true phase transition in optimization."
Solution sketch. Probe for each putative sub-skill independently at multiple scales (e.g., for arithmetic word problems: tokenization, single-digit addition, multi-step composition). If each sub-skill shows smooth scaling and the joint capability turns on only when all are present, conjunction. If instead the joint capability appears without all sub-skills being independently present — i.e., a previously-absent representation suddenly forms — phase transition. Mechanistic probes (Hessian spectrum, circuit linear-probe accuracy) crossing zero at the transition point distinguishes optimization-side phase changes.

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Huh, Cheung, Wang, Tegmark — The Platonic Representation Hypothesis (2024) — strongest single statement of the convergence claim with quantitative evidence.

Optional depth: Deep Learning as Program Synthesis — Solomonoff-to-NN bridge; Deep Learning is Not So Mysterious or Different (Wilson 2025) — PAC-Bayes-flavored counterpoint; Emergent Misalignment (Betley et al. 2025) — narrow→broad generalization with safety stakes; Olsson et al., In-context Learning and Induction Heads (Anthropic 2022); Nakkiran et al., Deep Double Descent (2020); Schaeffer–Miranda–Koyejo, Are Emergent Abilities a Mirage? (2023).

Solo assignment

Pick one mystery (your choice: double descent, simplicity bias, Platonic, ICL, or emergence). Write a 2-page memo: (i) the cleanest mathematical statement of the phenomenon; (ii) the two best mechanistic explanations and what experimental signature distinguishes them; (iii) which explanation you find most likely and why. Conclude with one prediction this view makes about frontier models that hasn't yet been tested.

B.3 Singular Learning Theory Singular models, the RLCT λ, Watanabe's free-energy formula, phase transitions, and developmental interpretability.
Prerequisites: B.1 (Bayesian learning, KL divergence); Laplace's method / saddle-point asymptotics; algebraic geometry at the level of desingularization / blow-ups (Hironaka's theorem stated, not proven); Fisher information matrix; eigendecomposition under degeneracy.

Neural networks are singular statistical models — their Fisher information matrix is degenerate at any solution, so classical BIC and Laplace approximations fail. Watanabe's free-energy formula \(F_n = nL_n + \lambda \log n - (m-1)\log\log n + O(1)\) replaces the BIC penalty with the real log canonical threshold \(\lambda\), which can be far smaller than \(d/2\) for singular regions. Posteriors concentrate around low-\(\lambda\) regions, not just low-loss regions; a measurable training-time signal — the local learning coefficient — traces stagewise developmental phase transitions, giving the SLT picture an empirical hook.

Pre-reading questions

  1. What does it mean for a statistical model to be singular, and why are neural networks singular?
  2. State Watanabe's free-energy asymptotic \(F_n = nL_n + \lambda \log n - (m-1)\log\log n + O(1)\). What is each term measuring?
  3. Why is \(\lambda \le d/2\), with equality iff the model is regular at the truth?
  4. How does the RLCT \(\lambda\) define an "effective dimension" different from parameter count?
  5. What is the link from \(\lambda\) to phase transitions and to developmental interpretability (Hoogland, Murfet, Timaeus)?

Core exposition

Why classical statistics fails for neural networks

Classical asymptotic theory (BIC, Laplace approximation) assumes the model is regular: the Fisher information matrix \(I(\theta^*) = \mathbb{E}[\nabla \log p(x|\theta^*)\,\nabla \log p(x|\theta^*)^\top]\) at the truth \(\theta^*\) is positive definite, and the parameter \(\theta^*\) is identifiable (unique). Under these conditions, BIC gives \(F_n \approx nL_n + (d/2)\log n\) where \(d\) is the parameter dimension. Neural networks satisfy neither condition. The parameter-function map is many-to-one (permutation symmetries, scale symmetries in ReLU networks: \((\alpha w, b/\alpha)\) gives the same function); the Fisher matrix has whole zero eigendirections, sometimes everywhere.

A statistical model is singular if the Fisher information matrix is degenerate (rank deficient) at the true parameter. Equivalently, the model has a positive-codimension level set \(\{\theta : p(\cdot|\theta) = p(\cdot|\theta^*)\}\) of equivalent parameters. The whole apparatus of asymptotic normality, Wald tests, and the standard BIC penalty fails. Watanabe's Singular Learning Theory (Watanabe 2009) provides the correct replacement.

Watanabe's program. Sumio Watanabe's singular learning theory was developed primarily in the 1990s–2000s, drawing on Hironaka's 1964 resolution-of-singularities theorem (which won the Fields Medal in 1970). The full machinery appeared in his 2009 Cambridge monograph Algebraic Geometry and Statistical Learning Theory, with key precursors in his 2001 paper "Algebraic analysis for nonidentifiable learning machines" (Neural Computation) and the WAIC criterion in 2010. The free-energy asymptotic was largely ignored by the ML mainstream until Murfet, Hoogland, and the Timaeus collaboration (2022–) reframed it as a quantitative theory of deep-learning development. The 50-year gap between the algebraic-geometry tools (resolution of singularities, zeta functions) and their use in deep-learning theory is itself instructive about how isolated mathematical communities can be.

The real log canonical threshold (RLCT) and the free-energy formula

The setup: data \(\mathcal{D}_n = (x_1, \ldots, x_n)\) drawn i.i.d. from truth \(q\); parameterized model \(p(x|\theta)\); prior \(\varphi(\theta)\) on parameter space \(W\). Define the average log-loss at the truth as \(L_n = (1/n)\sum -\log p(x_i \mid \theta^*)\) and the KL function \(K(\theta) = \mathbb{E}_q[\log q(x) - \log p(x \mid \theta)]\). \(K(\theta) \ge 0\) with equality on the truth set \(W^* = \{\theta : K(\theta) = 0\}\).

The Bayesian partition function is

\[ Z_n = \int_W \exp(-n K(\theta))\,\varphi(\theta)\,d\theta, \]

and the free energy is \(F_n = -\log Z_n\) (after subtracting \(n\mathbb{E}_q[\log q]\)). In the regular case, Laplace's method around the unique \(\theta^*\) gives \(F_n \approx (d/2)\log n - (1/2)\log\det I(\theta^*) + \text{const}\), yielding BIC.

In the singular case, the set \(W^*\) may be a high-dimensional analytic variety with singularities. Watanabe's central insight is that for any analytic \(K\), one can apply Hironaka's resolution of singularities: there exists a real analytic manifold \(U\) and a proper analytic map \(g : U \to W\) such that

\[ \begin{aligned} K(g(u)) &= u_1^{2k_1} u_2^{2k_2} \cdots u_d^{2k_d}, \\ |g'(u)| &= |u_1^{h_1} \cdots u_d^{h_d}| \end{aligned} \]

in local coordinates. The integral defining \(Z_n\) then becomes a sum of standard analytically tractable integrals.

The result:

Watanabe's free-energy formula. Under analyticity and integrability conditions,

\[ F_n = n L_n + \lambda \log n - (m - 1)\log\log n + O_p(1), \]

where:

  • \(\lambda\) is the real log canonical threshold (RLCT): \(\lambda = \min_\alpha (h_\alpha + 1)/(2 k_\alpha)\) minimized over the resolution charts. Equivalently, \(\lambda\) is the smallest pole of the zeta function \(\zeta(s) = \int K(\theta)^{-s}\,\varphi(\theta)\,d\theta\).
  • \(m\) is the multiplicity of that pole.
  • \(L_n\) is the empirical average of \(-\log p(x_i|\theta^*)\).

The \(\lambda \log n\) term is the singular analogue of the BIC penalty. In the regular case \(\lambda = d/2\) and \(m = 1\), recovering BIC. In the singular case, \(\lambda \le d/2\) strictly, often much less: a low-rank neural network solution can have \(\lambda\) orders of magnitude below the parameter count.

Why λ ≤ d/2

Heuristically: the volume of the \(\varepsilon\)-neighborhood \(\{\theta : K(\theta) < \varepsilon\}\) scales as \(\mathrm{vol}(\varepsilon) \sim \varepsilon^{\lambda} (\log(1/\varepsilon))^{m-1}\). For a regular model with \(d\)-dimensional parameter and full-rank Hessian, this volume scales as \(\varepsilon^{d/2}\), so \(\lambda = d/2\). Whenever the Hessian is degenerate, the level set \(\{K < \varepsilon\}\) extends along the flat directions, and the volume scales more slowly in \(\varepsilon\), i.e., with a smaller exponent. Smaller exponent of \(\varepsilon\) means smaller \(\lambda\). Concretely, if the Hessian has rank \(r < d\), then locally \(\lambda \le r/2 + (d - r) \cdot (\text{something involving higher-order vanishing})\); for a quartic minimum in one direction (i.e., \(K \approx \theta^4\)), that direction contributes \(1/4\) rather than \(1/2\).

Why \(\lambda \le d/2\) (volume picture in one line). The \(\lambda \log n\) penalty is \(-\log\mathrm{vol}(\{K < 1/n\})\): the log of how much parameter volume sits within \(1/n\) of the truth. A regular minimum is a paraboloid \(K \approx \theta^\top H \theta\), so \(\{K < \varepsilon\} \approx\) ellipsoid of axes \(\sqrt{\varepsilon/\lambda_i}\); volume \(\propto \varepsilon^{d/2}\) gives \(\lambda = d/2\). If even one direction is flatter than quadratic — say \(K \approx \theta^{2k}\) — that direction's axis is \(\varepsilon^{1/(2k)}\) wide, contributing \(1/(2k) < 1/2\) to the exponent. Each degenerate direction reduces \(\lambda\). The whole point of "neural nets are singular" is that they have many such directions.

RLCT \(\lambda\) vs LLC vs effective dimension. Three things often confused. (i) RLCT \(\lambda\) — a global property of the model+prior+truth triple defined via the zeta function; an algebraic invariant. (ii) Local learning coefficient (LLC) \(\hat\lambda(\theta)\) — a finite-\(n\) estimator computed from SGLD-based volume scaling around a specific \(\theta\); does not require knowing the truth. (iii) Effective dimension in PAC-Bayes / Rademacher contexts — typically based on Hessian-rank or trace-of-\(H^{-1}\) and has a different asymptotic interpretation. Of the three, only \(\lambda\) appears in Watanabe's free-energy formula; LLC is the empirical proxy you can compute on a real model; "effective dimension" is a heuristic from a different lineage and is not the same number, although the qualitative story (singular models have effectively fewer parameters than \(d\)) is shared.

Phase transitions and Bayesian model selection

The free-energy formula refines Bayesian model selection: posteriors concentrate around regions of low RLCT, not just regions of low loss. For finite \(n\), the posterior may switch between two singular regions as \(n\) grows — a Bayesian phase transition. Schematically: region \(A\) has slightly worse fit \(L_A > L_B\) but much lower complexity \(\lambda_A < \lambda_B\). The free-energy difference is

\[ F_A - F_B \approx n(L_A - L_B) + (\lambda_A - \lambda_B)\log n. \]

For small \(n\): complexity dominates, region \(A\) wins. For large \(n\): fit dominates, region \(B\) wins. Setting \(F_A = F_B\) gives the implicit crossover scale

\[ n^* \;\approx\; \frac{\lambda_B - \lambda_A}{L_A - L_B}\,\log n^*, \]

logarithmic in scale: small complexity differences \(\Delta\lambda\) can shift the transition by orders of magnitude in \(n\). This is the SLT picture of developmental phase transitions in neural networks.

Developmental interpretability (Timaeus / Hoogland / Murfet)

The Timaeus program (Hoogland, Murfet, van Wingerden, and collaborators) takes the SLT picture as a generative theory of training stages, not just final-state model selection. The local learning coefficient (LLC) — a finite-\(n\), locally-estimated proxy for \(\lambda\) around a current parameter — is computed via stochastic gradient Langevin dynamics: run SGLD around the current \(\theta\), estimate the expected loss increase, and fit to \(\hat\lambda\). Empirically, LLC traces over training reveal stagewise development: it rises sharply at moments of new structure formation (e.g., induction-head emergence in transformers) and plateaus during memorization or refinement phases.

This connects to safety: if developmental phase transitions can be detected by LLC inflections, then training-time interventions — pausing, perturbing, or steering — become temporally targeted. Hoogland et al.'s 2024–2025 papers on stagewise development in language models and on the local-learning-coefficient estimator are the canonical references; the OpenReview paper on the LLC scaling to transformer-sized models is the load-bearing scalability result.

Has developmental interp produced predictive results yet? Honestly: not many. The LLC retrodicts known transitions (induction-head formation, grokking, toy-model phase changes) and the program has scaled the estimator to transformer-sized models — these are real accomplishments. What it has not yet done is prospectively predict a previously-unobserved phase transition, identify the precise capability acquired at a transition before mechanistic probes confirm it, or produce a training-time intervention that demonstrably exploited the LLC signal to improve a safety property. The Timaeus 2024–2025 work is the strongest case for "this could become predictive," but treat it as a research program with promising hooks rather than a delivered theory of safety-relevant training dynamics. Compare to physics: post-1925 quantum mechanics was retrodictive for a decade before its first stage-zero predictions (Dirac antiparticle, 1928).

Worked example 1: RLCT for a toy reducible quadratic model

Model: \(p(y \mid x, a, b) = \mathcal{N}(y;\,ab\cdot x,\,1)\), with parameters \((a, b) \in \mathbb{R}^2\). Truth: \(q(y|x) = \mathcal{N}(y; 0, 1)\) (so the true regression coefficient is zero). Then \(K(a, b) = (1/2)\,\mathbb{E}_x[(abx)^2] = c\,a^2 b^2\) for some \(c > 0\) (constant absorbed into \(x\)'s variance).

The truth set is \(\{a = 0\} \cup \{b = 0\}\) — two lines crossing at the origin. The origin is the singular point of this variety: the parameter \(\theta^*\) is not isolated. The Fisher information matrix at the origin is identically zero — completely degenerate.

To compute the RLCT, blow up the origin: substitute \(a = u,\,b = uv\). Then \(K = c\,u^4 v^2\), with Jacobian \(|du\,dv| = |u|\,du\,dv\). The integral over the new chart gives

\[ \int \exp(-n c\,u^4 v^2)\,|u|\,du\,dv. \]

Compute the RLCT from the exponents: for the monomial \(u^4 v^2\) with measure factor \(u^1\), the candidate \(\lambda\)'s are \((1+1)/4 = 1/2\) (from \(u\)) and \((0+1)/2 = 1/2\) (from \(v\)). They tie, so \(\lambda = 1/2\) with multiplicity \(m = 2\).

Compare to the regular case (truth at \((a, b) = (a_0, b_0) \ne 0\)): there \(K\) is locally quadratic with full-rank Hessian, \(\lambda = d/2 = 1\). So the singular point has half the effective complexity: the posterior strongly prefers the singular region over the regular one when both are available, because the \(\lambda \log n\) term is smaller by \((1/2)\log n\) bits.

Interpretation: the model has a "use it or lose it" parameter — when the truth doesn't need \(ab\), the Bayes posterior gravitates to the cross of \(a = 0\) and \(b = 0\), which has lower effective dimension than the generic 2D parameterization.

Worked example 2: RLCT for a 1-D quadratic fit, regular vs singular truth

Model: \(p(y \mid x; a) = \mathcal{N}(y;\,ax^2,\,1)\). Single parameter \(a \in \mathbb{R}\). Then \(K(a) = (1/2)\,\mathbb{E}_x[(ax^2 - a^* x^2)^2] = c(a - a^*)^2\) for some \(c\).

Case 1: \(a^* \ne 0\) (regular truth). Standard Gaussian-integral computation gives \(\lambda = d/2 = 1/2\), \(m = 1\). Free energy \(F_n \approx nL_n + (1/2)\log n\), recovering BIC.

Case 2: \(a^* = 0\) (singular truth). Now consider the closely related model \(p(y|x; a) = \mathcal{N}(y; ax^2, 1)\) with truth still \(a^* = 0\), but extend the parameter by adding a useless dimension: take the model \(p(y|x; a, b) = \mathcal{N}(y;\,(a + b^2)x^2,\,1)\). Then \(K(a, b) = c(a + b^2)^2\). Truth set: \(a = -b^2\) — a parabola in \((a, b)\) space, not isolated. Resolve via \(a = -b^2 + u\): \(K = c u^2\), Jacobian 1, and \(b\) is a free direction integrated against the prior. RLCT from \(u\): \(1/2\). The \(b\) direction contributes nothing to the loss but contributes a finite volume from the prior. So \(\lambda = 1/2\), lower than the regular \(d/2 = 1\) we would have gotten from naive counting.

The lesson: redundant parameters that lie tangent to the truth set reduce \(\lambda\). Neural networks have many such redundancies (permutations, ReLU scale symmetries, dead neurons, low-rank weight subspaces), and the cumulative reduction is what makes \(\lambda\) often dramatically smaller than the parameter count.

  • A statistical model is singular iff its Fisher information is degenerate at the truth; neural networks are singular by construction (permutation, ReLU scale, dead-neuron symmetries).
  • Watanabe's free-energy formula \(F_n = nL_n + \lambda\log n - (m-1)\log\log n + O(1)\) replaces BIC; \(\lambda \le d/2\) with equality iff regular.
  • Posteriors concentrate around low-\(\lambda\) regions, not just low-loss regions — singular models prefer simpler representations, which gives a quantitative theory of why deep nets generalize.
  • Bayesian phase transitions occur at \(n^* \approx (\lambda_B - \lambda_A) / (L_A - L_B) \cdot \log n^*\) — a constructive prediction the developmental-interpretability program tests empirically.
  • The local learning coefficient (LLC) is a tractable finite-\(n\) estimate of \(\lambda\) via SGLD-based volume scaling; LLC traces over training reveal stagewise development (Hoogland et al., Timaeus).

Key definitions and results

Singular model
One whose Fisher information matrix is rank-deficient at the truth; equivalently, the truth-set \(W^*\) has positive codimension or is non-smooth.
RLCT (real log canonical threshold) \(\lambda\)
Smallest pole of the zeta function \(\zeta(s) = \int K(\theta)^{-s}\,\varphi(\theta)\,d\theta\); equivalently, exponent of \(\varepsilon\) in \(\mathrm{vol}(\{K < \varepsilon\})\).
Multiplicity \(m\)
Order of the pole of \(\zeta\) at \(-\lambda\); controls the \((\log \log n)\) correction.
Free-energy formula
\(F_n = nL_n + \lambda \log n - (m-1)\log\log n + O_p(1)\) (Watanabe).
Regular case
\(\lambda = d/2\), \(m = 1\); recovers BIC. Strict inequality \(\lambda < d/2\) diagnoses singularity.
Local learning coefficient (LLC)
Finite-\(n\), locally-estimated proxy for \(\lambda\) around current parameter, computed via SGLD-based volume scaling.
Developmental interpretability
Use of LLC and related geometric measures across training to identify discrete developmental stages and phase transitions.

Retrieval practice

Q: Why does BIC fail for neural networks?
A: BIC assumes a regular model; neural networks are massively singular (permutation, scale, and rank-deficiency symmetries make the Fisher matrix degenerate everywhere). The penalty \((d/2)\log n\) is wrong, often by orders of magnitude.
Q: What is the role of Hironaka's theorem in the proof?
A: It guarantees that the singular \(K(\theta)\) can be reduced to a sum of monomials \(u_1^{2k_1} \cdots u_d^{2k_d}\) on a desingularized manifold; this reduces the partition-function integral to a standard form whose asymptotics are computed via Mellin/zeta-function analysis.
Q: Why \(\lambda \le d/2\) always?
A: The Hessian at the truth set has at most rank \(d\); the regular case saturates with full rank. Any further degeneracy (zero eigenvalues, higher-order vanishing) only enlarges the low-loss region and lowers the exponent of \(\varepsilon\).
Q: How does the LLC connect to mechanistic interpretability?
A: LLC inflections during training mark the formation of new effective degrees of freedom — i.e., new circuits or representational structure. Pairing LLC traces with circuit-level probes lets you localize when a capability formed, not just that it did.
Q: Bayesian phase transition: what's the crossover scale?
A: Two singular regions with parameters \((L_A, \lambda_A)\) and \((L_B, \lambda_B)\): posterior mass crosses when \(n(L_A - L_B) \approx (\lambda_B - \lambda_A)\log n\), i.e., \(n^* \approx (\Delta\lambda / \Delta L)\log n^*\) — implicit equation logarithmic in scale.
Q: Is SLT a description of Bayesian posteriors only, or also of SGD-trained networks?
A: Strictly, the free-energy formula is Bayesian. The connection to SGD is empirical: trained networks are observed to sit in regions whose LLC tracks meaningful capability formation. The mapping from Bayesian \(\lambda\) to SGD-found minima is conjectural and the subject of ongoing work.
Q: What's a known failure mode of LLC estimation?
A: SGLD-based estimators are sensitive to step size, prior covariance, and chain mixing; LLC values are most informative as relative measures over training, not as absolute \(\lambda\) estimates.

Problems

30 min · derivationProblem 1. Consider the model \(p(y \mid x; a, b) = \mathcal{N}(y;\,(ab)\cdot x,\,1)\) with prior uniform on \([-1, 1]^2\), and truth \(q(y|x) = \mathcal{N}(y; 0, 1)\). Compute the RLCT \(\lambda\) and multiplicity \(m\). State explicitly the free-energy asymptotic and contrast with the BIC prediction of \((d/2)\log n = \log n\).
Solution sketch. Following the worked example: \(K(a,b) = c a^2 b^2\). Blow up \(a = u, b = uv\): \(K = c u^4 v^2\) with Jacobian \(|u|\). Two charts (also blow up \(b = w, a = wz\)); by symmetry both give the same answer. Candidate \(\lambda\)'s: \((1+1)/4 = 1/2\) from the \(u\) direction; \((0+1)/2 = 1/2\) from the \(v\) direction. Tie → \(\lambda = 1/2\), \(m = 2\). Free energy: \(F_n = nL_n + (1/2)\log n - \log\log n + O(1)\). BIC predicts \((2/2)\log n = \log n\). SLT predicts half that, plus a log-log correction. For \(n = 10^6\): BIC says penalty \(\approx 13.8\), SLT says \(\approx 6.9 - 2.6 \approx 4.3\). Factor-of-3 difference, all from the \(ab = 0\) singularity.
30 min · derivationProblem 2. A two-layer linear neural network \(f(x) = W_2 W_1 x\) with \(W_1 \in \mathbb{R}^{h \times d}\), \(W_2 \in \mathbb{R}^{1 \times h}\) and truth \(f^*(x) = 0\). (i) Identify the parameter symmetries that make the model singular. (ii) Describe (qualitatively, no proof) how \(\lambda\) scales with \(h\). (iii) What does this predict for the Bayes-optimal width as a function of \(n\)?
Solution sketch. (i) Symmetries: \((W_1, W_2) \leftrightarrow (P W_1, W_2 P^{-1})\) for invertible \(P \in GL(h)\) — full \(GL(h)\) orbit of equivalent parameterizations. Also rank-deficient: any \(W_1\) with \(W_2 W_1 = 0\) is in the truth set. (ii) The truth set \(\{W_2 W_1 = 0\}\) has codimension \(d\) in the parameter space of dimension \(h(d+1)\); large \(h\) increases the truth-set dimension, so volume of \(\{K < \varepsilon\}\) grows with \(h\) while \(K\)-curvature stays similar. RLCT decreases (slowly) with \(h\), but the prior-volume factor grows. Net effect: \(F_n\) as a function of \(h\) has a minimum at some \(h^*(n)\). (iii) \(h^*(n)\) grows slowly (logarithmically or polynomially in \(n\)); Bayes prefers some overparameterization but not unbounded. This is the SLT picture of "right-sizing" width.

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Hoogland — The Real Log Canonical Threshold, Explained — the cleanest from-zero exposition; if you want the textbook, Watanabe's Algebraic Geometry and Statistical Learning Theory (2009, Cambridge).

Optional depth: Lau, Furman, Wang, Murfet, Hoogland — The Local Learning Coefficient (2025) — scalable LLC for deep nets; Compressibility Measures Complexity (2025) — SLT/MDL/LLC unification; Timaeus SLT learning hub — curated path; Iliad "Degeneracy in Deep Learning" lecture; Wei et al., "Deep Learning is Singular" (2022); Chen, Hoogland, et al., "Dynamics of Phase Transitions in Toy Models" (2023).

Solo assignment

Choose a small singular toy model (e.g., a 2-layer linear network \(f = W_2 W_1 x\) with rank-bottleneck truth, or the \(ab\,x\) regression above). (i) Derive the RLCT analytically using blow-ups in at least one chart. (ii) Implement an LLC estimator via SGLD around an empirically-found minimum and compare to your analytic \(\lambda\). (iii) In 2 pages, document the discrepancies you find and explain whether they are due to finite-\(n\) bias, prior misspecification, or chain non-mixing. Bonus: produce an LLC trace through training and identify any apparent developmental stages.

B.4 Training Dynamics NTK linearization, lazy vs feature-learning regimes, grokking, loss-landscape geometry, edge of stability.
Prerequisites: gradient descent + SGD; kernel methods / RKHS; Hessian spectrum and eigenvalues; B.3 (RLCT, phase transitions); calculus of variations / gradient flow.

Gradient descent on neural networks operates in two qualitatively different regimes: the lazy regime where the network is effectively a kernel machine on \(K_{\mathrm{NTK}}\) and weights barely move from initialization, and the rich / feature-learning regime where representations themselves change. Edge of stability says training self-regulates near \(\eta \, \lambda_{\max}(\nabla^2 L) \approx 2\) — not an instability but a robust operating point. Grokking (train hits 1 fast, test stays at chance then jumps) and the SLT phase-transition picture from B.3 are most likely the same phenomenon: discrete internal-circuit reorganizations of the learned function.

Pre-reading questions

  1. State the NTK linearization formula. What is the regime of validity (in width, in time, in learning rate)?
  2. How do "lazy" and "rich" (feature-learning) regimes differ operationally and theoretically?
  3. What is grokking? What is the leading mechanistic story, and what does the LLC do during a grokking transition?
  4. State the edge-of-stability claim. What pins the top Hessian eigenvalue to \(\approx 2/\eta\)?
  5. Why does batch size act roughly like inverse temperature (or equivalently, inverse learning rate) for SGD's stationary distribution?

Core exposition

The neural tangent kernel and the lazy regime

Consider a network \(f(x;\theta)\) trained with gradient flow on a squared loss \(L(\theta) = (1/2)\sum (f(x_i;\theta) - y_i)^2\). The first-order Taylor expansion around initialization \(\theta_0\) is

\[ f(x; \theta_t) \approx f(x; \theta_0) + \nabla_\theta f(x; \theta_0)^\top (\theta_t - \theta_0). \]

If this linearization is valid throughout training, the dynamics on outputs become

\[ \begin{aligned} \partial_t f(x; \theta_t) &= -\sum_i K_{\mathrm{NTK}}(x, x_i)\,(f(x_i; \theta_t) - y_i), \\ K_{\mathrm{NTK}}(x, x') &= \langle \nabla_\theta f(x; \theta_0),\,\nabla_\theta f(x'; \theta_0) \rangle. \end{aligned} \]

That is, the network behaves exactly like kernel regression with the fixed kernel \(K_{\mathrm{NTK}}\). Jacot–Gabriel–Hongler (2018) and Lee et al. (2019) showed that for fully-connected networks at infinite width with a particular ("NTK") parameterization, \(K_{\mathrm{NTK}}\) converges to a deterministic kernel and stays fixed for all training time. At any finite width, kernel deviation is \(O(1/\sqrt{\mathrm{width}})\).

The infinite-width limit thus produces a kernel learner, not a feature learner: the representations at the end of training are essentially those at initialization, just reweighted. This is the lazy regime.

Lazy regime vs rich / feature-learning regime. "Lazy" means the linearization \(f(x;\theta_t) \approx f(x;\theta_0) + \nabla_\theta f(x;\theta_0)^\top(\theta_t - \theta_0)\) holds throughout training — equivalent to kernel regression with the fixed NTK. "Rich" / "feature-learning" / "mean-field" means the linearization fails: \(\theta_t\) moves \(O(1)\) and the kernel itself drifts. The regime is set by parameterization scaling (NTK \(1/\sqrt m\) vs muP / mean-field \(1/m\) on the output layer), not by network size alone. A wide network in NTK param is lazy; the same width in mean-field param is rich. So "NTK theory describes wide networks" is wrong as stated — it describes wide networks in NTK parameterization. Frontier-model training uses parameterizations (muP, Llama-style scaled init) chosen specifically to operate in the rich regime, where representations actually change. NTK is a useful solvable toy, not a model of practice.

Lazy vs rich (feature-learning) regimes

Whether a network operates in the lazy or rich regime depends on its parameterization and the scale of \(\theta_0\) relative to width. Chizat–Bach (2019) showed that the NTK parameterization (outputs of order \(1/\sqrt{n}\) at init) yields lazy behavior, while the "mean-field" or "muP" parameterizations (outputs of order 1, gradients scaled) yield rich, feature-learning behavior, where representations evolve substantially during training and the NTK kernel itself changes.

Operational diagnostics:

  • Lazy: \(\|\theta_T - \theta_0\| / \|\theta_0\| \to 0\) with width; representation similarity (CKA) between init and final is high; features at any layer barely change.
  • Rich: \(\|\theta_T - \theta_0\|\) is \(O(1)\) with width; CKA(init, final) drops; new features form, especially in early layers.

Frontier-model training is firmly in the rich regime — they would not work otherwise. NTK theory is therefore a tractable toy, not a model of practice; but the lazy/rich boundary is the right framework for asking "is this configuration learning features at all?"

Grokking: delayed generalization

Grokking (Power et al. 2022, OpenAI): on a small modular-arithmetic task \((a, b) \mapsto (a + b) \bmod p\), a small transformer trained with weight decay shows two distinct stages:

  1. Memorization phase: train accuracy reaches 100% quickly (within hundreds of steps); test accuracy stays near chance.
  2. Grokking transition: after a long plateau (often \(10^4\text{–}10^6\) steps), test accuracy abruptly jumps to 100%. Weight norm decays during the plateau; the moment of transition coincides with the formation of an algorithmic representation (Fourier features in \(\mathbb{Z}/p\mathbb{Z}\)).

Mechanistically (Nanda et al. 2023): the model learns a generalizing circuit that implements modular addition via discrete Fourier transform — embedding each integer as \((\cos(2\pi ka/p), \sin(2\pi ka/p))\) for a few \(k\), then using attention/MLP to compute the multiplication-addition formulas \(\cos(\alpha + \beta) = \cos\alpha\cos\beta - \sin\alpha\sin\beta\). Grokking is the slow consolidation of this circuit alongside decay of the memorization circuit, driven by weight decay's penalty on the (higher-norm) memorization solution. A compact balance describing the transition: training with weight decay \(\gamma\) finds the minimizer of an effective free energy

\[ \mathcal{F}_\gamma(\theta) \;=\; L(\theta) + \tfrac{\gamma}{2}\|\theta\|^2, \]

and the algorithmic circuit (lower \(\|\theta\|^2\) at matched \(L\)) overtakes the memorization circuit when \(\gamma\)-accumulated weight-norm decay outpaces the memorization basin's depth. The empirical "grok time" \(t^*\) scales roughly as \(1/\gamma\) — directly testable, and verified across Power et al. and Nanda et al. ablations.

Connection to SLT: LLC traces (Hoogland et al. 2024) through grokking show the LLC rising sharply at the grokking transition — the model is acquiring new effective degrees of freedom corresponding to the algorithmic circuit. The picture: a Bayesian phase transition between two singular regions, observable in real training.

Grokking and SLT phase transitions are most likely the same phenomenon. Both have the same fingerprint: two distinct singular regions in parameter space (memorization circuit, algorithmic circuit), an early region with worse generalization but easier access (the memorization basin has higher entropy, higher norm, larger \(\lambda_{\text{mem}}\)), a transition driven by an external pressure (weight decay in grokking, Bayesian sample-size growth in SLT — both penalize the higher-complexity region), and an LLC inflection at the transition matching the eq (B.3.3) crossover prediction. The most credible unified picture: SGD with weight decay implements an approximate Bayesian sampling at temperature \(\eta/B\), the long pre-grokking plateau is the time spent waiting to cross the free-energy barrier between basins, and grokking is just a name we gave to one specific empirical instance of the SLT transition. Caveat: this is a research-program-level claim, not a theorem. Chen-Hoogland 2023 toy models give the strongest direct evidence; full-scale formal proof is open.

Loss landscape geometry and edge of stability

For gradient descent with learning rate \(\eta\) on a strictly-convex quadratic with Hessian eigenvalues \(\lambda_i\), stability requires \(\eta \lambda_i < 2\) for all \(i\). The classical picture says: choose \(\eta < 2/\lambda_{\max}\), and GD converges monotonically.

Edge of stability (Cohen, Kaur, Li, Kolter, Talwalkar 2021). Empirically, neural-network training with constant \(\eta\) does not stay below the stability threshold. Instead:

  1. Initially, \(\eta\,\lambda_{\max}(H) < 2\) and the loss decreases monotonically (the "progressive sharpening" phase: \(\lambda_{\max}\) rises during training).
  2. Once \(\eta\,\lambda_{\max}(H) \approx 2\) (the edge of stability), training enters a non-monotonic regime: the loss oscillates around its mean but continues to decrease on average; the top Hessian eigenvalue hovers at exactly \(\approx 2/\eta\).

Mechanism: when GD overshoots in the top-eigenvalue direction, it lands at a point where curvature in that direction is slightly lower (the landscape has negative third derivatives along this direction); the dynamics self-regulate the sharpness. This is a fundamentally non-classical regime — gradient descent acts as an implicit curvature-controller.

Edge of stability is empirical, not a guarantee. The Cohen et al. result is a robust observation across many tasks and architectures; it is not a theorem about general nonconvex losses. There are tasks where progressive sharpening fails to onset (very simple data), regimes where momentum changes the threshold from \(2/\eta\) to a momentum-corrected constant, and pathological landscapes (sharp ridges, non-smooth losses) where the self-regulation breaks. Treating EoS as a free lunch — "just use big LR, training will sort itself out" — works empirically more often than it should but is not safe to assume. Practical implication: for any new training setup, plot \(\lambda_{\max}(H)\) explicitly; if it does not plateau at \(\approx 2/\eta\) within a budget, you are in a non-EoS regime and the dynamics may diverge or get stuck.

Learning-rate and batch-size scaling

For SGD viewed as discretization of a stochastic differential equation, the stationary distribution depends on the ratio \(\eta / B\) where \(\eta\) is learning rate and \(B\) is batch size. To first order, doubling \(\eta\) is equivalent to halving \(B\) for the temperature of the implicit sampler (Smith–Le, Mandt et al.). Empirically this linear scaling rule holds across many orders of magnitude until very large batch sizes; thereafter the bias from finite step size becomes significant. The practical takeaway: there is one effective temperature, and exploration vs exploitation in SGD is controlled by it.

Loss landscape: mode connectivity and basin geometry

Garipov–Izmailov–Podoprikhin–Vetrov–Wilson (2018): independently trained SGD solutions, despite being far apart in parameter space, are connected by smooth low-loss paths (e.g., low-degree polynomial Bezier curves). Frankle–Carbin (2019) on the "lottery ticket hypothesis": dense networks contain sparse subnetworks that, when re-initialized to their original weights, train to the same accuracy. Both findings suggest a non-convex but heavily-connected basin structure: many flat valleys connected by low passes.

Worked example: NTK on a 1-hidden-layer network

Take \(f(x; \theta) = (1/\sqrt{m})\sum_{j=1}^m a_j\,\sigma(w_j^\top x)\) with \(\sigma = \mathrm{ReLU}\), \(a_j \in \mathbb{R}\), \(w_j \in \mathbb{R}^d\), all \(a_j, w_j\) i.i.d. standard Gaussian. Width \(m\).

Gradients: \(\partial f/\partial a_j = (1/\sqrt{m})\,\sigma(w_j^\top x)\); \(\partial f/\partial w_j = (1/\sqrt{m})\,a_j\,\sigma'(w_j^\top x)\,x\). The NTK is

\[ K_{\mathrm{NTK}}(x, x') = \frac{1}{m}\sum_j \big[\sigma(w_j^\top x)\,\sigma(w_j^\top x') + a_j^2\,\sigma'(w_j^\top x)\,\sigma'(w_j^\top x')\,\langle x, x' \rangle\big]. \]

As \(m \to \infty\), by law of large numbers, this converges to

\[ K_{\mathrm{NTK}}^\infty(x, x') = \mathbb{E}_w[\sigma(w^\top x)\,\sigma(w^\top x')] + \mathbb{E}_w[\sigma'(w^\top x)\,\sigma'(w^\top x')]\,\langle x, x' \rangle. \]

For ReLU and unit-norm \(x\), the first term is the arccosine kernel of order 1, the second of order 0; both have closed forms (Cho–Saul 2009):

\[ K_{\mathrm{NTK}}^\infty(x, x') = \frac{1}{2\pi}\,\|x\|\,\|x'\|\,(\sin\theta + (\pi - \theta)\cos\theta) + \frac{1}{2\pi}(\pi - \theta)\,\langle x, x' \rangle, \]

where \(\theta = \arccos(\langle x, x' \rangle / (\|x\|\,\|x'\|))\).

The crucial fact: this kernel is fixed at initialization and does not change during training (to leading order in \(1/m\)). Therefore training is exactly kernel regression with \(K_{\mathrm{NTK}}^\infty\), and the test error has the standard kernel-regression generalization properties. Conversely, real networks at practical widths (say \(m = 1000\)) show kernel drift of order \(1/\sqrt{m} \approx 3\%\) in the lazy regime, and order-1 drift in the rich regime: NTK as a model of finite practical networks holds only under aggressive lazy-parameterization.

  • NTK linearization holds in the lazy regime: training is equivalent to kernel regression with the deterministic infinite-width kernel \(K_{\mathrm{NTK}}^\infty\).
  • Lazy vs rich is a parameterization choice (NTK vs μP, Chizat-Bach); rich-regime feature learning is where representations actually change, which is the regime that matters for safety-relevant structure.
