  • Edge of stability: training self-regulates near \(\eta\,\lambda_{\max}(H) \approx 2\) — not an instability but a robust empirical operating point.
  • Grokking (Power et al.) shows phase-transition-like delayed generalization on small algorithmic tasks; the LLC story from B.3 connects this to internal-circuit formation rather than test-set artifact.
  • Practical takeaway: NTK explains why kernel methods work as analogues for trained nets in the lazy regime, but you cannot understand feature-learning capabilities without leaving NTK.

Key definitions and results

NTK
\(K_{\mathrm{NTK}}(x, x') = \langle \nabla_\theta f(x; \theta_0),\,\nabla_\theta f(x'; \theta_0) \rangle\); deterministic in infinite-width NTK parameterization, fixed throughout training.
Lazy regime
Linearization around init is valid throughout training; representations don't change; equivalent to kernel regression.
Rich (feature-learning) regime
Parameters move \(O(1)\) in norm; representations evolve; NTK itself changes during training.
Grokking
Delayed-generalization phenomenon on small algorithmic tasks: train accuracy saturates fast, test accuracy saturates much later via formation of an algorithmic circuit.
Edge of stability
Empirical regime where \(\eta\,\lambda_{\max}(H) \approx 2\); training is non-monotonic in loss but progresses on average; \(\lambda_{\max}\) self-regulates.
Linear scaling rule
SGD stationary statistics depend on \(\eta/B\); doubling LR \(\approx\) halving batch size for moderate ranges.
Mode connectivity
Independently trained solutions are connected by smooth low-loss paths in parameter space.

Retrieval practice

Q: In what sense is NTK theory falsified by practice?
A: Frontier models exhibit substantial feature learning — early-layer representations change qualitatively during training. NTK predicts no representation change. Empirical CKA(init, final) drops to near-zero in middle layers of large transformers; this is the rich regime, not the lazy one.
Q: What pins \(\lambda_{\max}\) to \(2/\eta\) at the edge of stability?
A: When \(\lambda_{\max}\) exceeds \(2/\eta\), the dynamics in the top-eigendirection are unstable: the iterate oscillates with growing amplitude. Higher-order terms in the loss damp these oscillations only when the local \(\lambda_{\max}\) drops back to \(\approx 2/\eta\). The system self-regulates.
Q: Why does weight decay help grokking?
A: The memorization solution has higher weight norm than the algorithmic (Fourier-feature) solution. Weight decay shifts the implicit prior away from memorization, eventually making the algorithmic basin the lower-free-energy attractor.
Q: What's the muP (maximal update parameterization) prescription, in one line?
A: Scale initializations and learning rates per-tensor so that activations, gradients, and updates all stay \(O(1)\) in width — yielding hyperparameter transfer across model scales.
Q: How does grokking connect to B.3 (SLT)?
A: Grokking is a Bayesian phase transition between two singular regions (memorization circuit, algorithmic circuit). LLC inflection at the transition is the SLT signature; weight-decay sharpens it by penalizing the higher-norm region.
Q: Why doesn't NTK explain why deep learning works on real tasks?
A: Kernel learners with fixed kernels are limited by the inductive bias of the kernel. NTK kernels are smooth and isotropic; many real tasks (e.g., image classification, language modeling) require hierarchical features that only emerge with feature-learning dynamics, not under a fixed kernel.

Problems

30 min · derivationProblem 1. A 2-layer ReLU network with one input is initialized in NTK parameterization. Show that under gradient flow on a regression task, \(\|\theta_t - \theta_0\|_\infty = O(1/\sqrt{m})\) per parameter, and conclude that the linearization error in \(f\) is \(O(1/\sqrt{m})\).
Solution sketch. In NTK param, \(f = (1/\sqrt{m})\sum_j a_j\,\sigma(w_j^\top x)\); the loss \(L\) has \(\partial L/\partial a_j = (1/\sqrt{m})\,(\text{residual})\cdot\sigma(w_j^\top x)\). Each per-parameter gradient is \(O(1/\sqrt{m})\); integrating gradient flow over \(O(1)\) time gives \(|\Delta a_j| = O(1/\sqrt{m})\). The Taylor remainder in \(f\) is sum of \(m\) terms each \(O(1/m)\) (product of two \(O(1/\sqrt{m})\) changes plus the prefactor), totaling \(O(1/\sqrt{m})\). Hence the NTK approximation is valid to that order.
15 min · scenarioProblem 2. Sketch a minimal experiment to reproduce edge-of-stability behavior in a 2-layer MLP on MNIST. Specify (i) the architecture, (ii) the loss + learning rate, (iii) what to plot during training, (iv) the expected signature.
Solution sketch. (i) 2-layer MLP, widths 512, ReLU, MSE on a regression-of-labels target (or cross-entropy works too). (ii) Vanilla GD (full batch), \(\eta\) set so initial \(\eta\,\lambda_{\max}\) is well below 2 (e.g., \(\eta = 0.01\)). (iii) Log loss, top Hessian eigenvalue via Lanczos every \(k\) steps. (iv) Expected: \(\lambda_{\max}\) rises ("progressive sharpening") until \(\eta\,\lambda_{\max} \approx 2\); thereafter \(\lambda_{\max}\) plateaus at \(\approx 2/\eta\), loss oscillates but decreases on average. Vary \(\eta\) and confirm the plateau scales as \(2/\eta\).

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Cohen, Kaur, Li, Kolter, Talwalkar — Gradient Descent on Neural Networks Typically Occurs at the Edge of Stability (2021) — the empirical regime that overturned the classical stability story.

Optional depth: Saxe, McClelland, Ganguli — Deep Linear Dynamics — solvable toy with stagewise feature emergence; Jacot–Gabriel–Hongler 2018 (the NTK paper); Chizat–Bach 2019 on lazy vs rich; Power et al. 2022 (grokking original); Nanda et al. 2023 on grokking circuits; Complexity Dynamics of Grokking (2025); Training Dynamics Underlying LM Scaling Laws (2025); muP (Yang–Hu 2022) for the right parameterization for hyperparameter transfer.

Solo assignment

Train a small transformer (\(\le 1\)M params) on modular addition mod \(p\) (\(p\) prime, e.g., 113). Reproduce grokking. Produce three plots over training: (i) train and test accuracy; (ii) weight norm and weight-decay-adjusted "effective complexity"; (iii) either an LLC estimate (via SGLD), or a Fourier-decomposition probe of the embedding layer to detect circuit formation. In a 2-page writeup, identify the grokking transition and explain — referencing both SLT (B.3) and the lazy/rich framework — what is happening at that moment.

B.5 Data Attribution Influence functions, TracIn, Datamodels, and provenance of model behaviors for safety.
Prerequisites: implicit function theorem; Newton's method and Hessian inverse; B.3 (singularity of \(H\) at trained NN solutions); B.4 (training-trajectory dependence); leave-one-out cross-validation intuition.

Influence functions estimate \(I_{\mathrm{up}}(z, z_{\mathrm{test}}) = -\nabla L(z_{\mathrm{test}})^\top \hat H^{-1} \nabla L(z)\) — a first-order causal counterfactual for "what if this training point's weight had been infinitesimally perturbed." For NN safety, this lets you attribute model behaviors to their training-data provenance. Three caveats bite: the Hessian is rank-deficient at NN optima (B.3 singularity, so the textbook formula needs Tikhonov / EK-FAC regularization), influence is path-dependent (TracIn improves on this), and high-influence training points are not the same as semantically-responsible ones — the validity of an attribution-based safety case depends on whether ablating identified examples actually removes the behavior.

Pre-reading questions

  1. State the influence-function formula for upweighting a training example. Where does the Hessian inverse come from?
  2. Why do classical influence functions fail in modern neural networks, and what fixes have been proposed?
  3. What is the safety relevance of training-data attribution? Concretely, what would you do with perfect TDA against emergent misalignment?
  4. Compare TracIn, influence functions, and Datamodels on what they assume about training.
  5. How does data attribution interact with the singular structure of NN solutions (B.3)?

Core exposition

The central counterfactual question

Training data attribution (TDA) asks: for a model \(\hat\theta\) trained on dataset \(\mathcal{D} = \{z_1, \ldots, z_n\}\), and for a test point \(z_{\mathrm{test}}\), what would the model's loss at \(z_{\mathrm{test}}\) be if training point \(z_i\) had been (i) absent, (ii) reweighted by \(\varepsilon\), or (iii) replaced by \(z_i'\)? The exact answer requires retraining; the goal of TDA is to produce a useful approximation without retraining.

First-order influence vs causal counterfactuals. An influence function answers "\(\partial L(z_*)/\partial\varepsilon\) at \(\varepsilon = 0\)" — the linearization of the LOO operator. It is a first-order Taylor approximation, accurate only when the perturbation \(\varepsilon\) is small and the implicit-function expansion is valid. A causal counterfactual is "what would \(L(z_*)\) actually be if \(z\) were removed and the model retrained from the same initialization?" — a finite, nonlinear change. The two coincide only to leading order. Datamodels approximate the causal counterfactual via brute-force retraining sampling; IF approximates the first-order coefficient via Hessian inversion. Frame your safety case around which one you actually need: "this training example caused 80% of the behavior" is a causal claim and Datamodels is the right tool; "this example moves the loss by \(\varepsilon\)" is a first-order claim and IF (with reservations) does it cheaply.

Influence functions (Cook 1977; Koh–Liang 2017)

Consider the empirical risk minimizer \(\hat\theta(\varepsilon) = \arg\min_\theta (1/n)\sum_i L(z_i, \theta) + \varepsilon L(z, \theta)\). The first-order Taylor expansion in \(\varepsilon\) at \(\varepsilon = 0\), derived via the implicit function theorem applied to the first-order optimality condition, gives

\[ \left.\frac{d\hat\theta}{d\varepsilon}\right|_{\varepsilon=0} = -H_{\hat\theta}^{-1}\,\nabla_\theta L(z, \hat\theta), \]

where \(H_{\hat\theta} = (1/n)\sum_i \nabla^2_\theta L(z_i, \hat\theta)\) is the empirical Hessian. The induced change in test loss is

\[ \begin{aligned} I_{\mathrm{up}}(z, z_{\mathrm{test}}) &= \left.\frac{dL(z_{\mathrm{test}}, \hat\theta(\varepsilon))}{d\varepsilon}\right|_{\varepsilon=0} \\ &= \nabla_\theta L(z_{\mathrm{test}}, \hat\theta)^\top \cdot \frac{d\hat\theta}{d\varepsilon} \\ &= -\nabla_\theta L(z_{\mathrm{test}}, \hat\theta)^\top\,H_{\hat\theta}^{-1}\,\nabla_\theta L(z, \hat\theta). \end{aligned} \]

This is the influence function. Interpretation: a small upweighting of \(z\) moves \(\hat\theta\) in direction \(-H^{-1}\nabla L(z, \hat\theta)\) (a Newton step); the dot product with \(\nabla L(z_{\mathrm{test}}, \hat\theta)\) gives the first-order change in test loss. The leave-one-out (LOO) prediction is approximately \(-(1/n)\,I_{\mathrm{up}}(z, z_{\mathrm{test}})\).

For removal of an entire training point, integrating to second order or solving the perturbation exactly leads to "Newton-step" attribution: \(\hat\theta_{-i} \approx \hat\theta - (1/(n-1))\,H^{-1}\,\nabla L(z_i, \hat\theta)\).

Why classical IF fails on deep neural networks

The formula relies on three assumptions that NN training violates:

  1. Strict convexity: \(H_{\hat\theta}\) must be invertible. For deep nets, \(H\) is rank-deficient (millions of zero eigenvalues; recall B.3: singular models). Inverting requires damping \(H + \lambda I\), but the choice of \(\lambda\) is essentially arbitrary and changes results by orders of magnitude.
  2. Convergence to a critical point: the implicit-function step assumes \(\nabla L(\hat\theta) = 0\). SGD-trained networks are not at critical points — they sit on the edge of stability, with non-trivial gradient noise.
  3. Path-independence: the formula is a property of the loss function at \(\hat\theta\), not the training trajectory. But neural networks remember initialization and order of data (B.4). Two ERMs reached by different paths give different influences.

Empirically (Basu–Pope–Feizi 2021; Bae et al. 2022), classical influence functions correlate weakly with true LOO retraining for deep networks. Improvements include damping with informed \(\lambda\), EK-FAC Hessian approximations (Grosse–Bae et al. 2023), explicit unrolling, and Bayesian-posterior-based methods (BIF, 2025).

The Hessian is rank-deficient at NN optima — naive IF does not strictly apply. By B.3, a trained neural network sits in a singular region: \(H\) has zero eigendirections (permutation symmetries, ReLU scale symmetries, dead-neuron directions). The formula \(I_{\mathrm{up}} = -\nabla L(z_*)^\top H^{-1} \nabla L(z)\) is literally undefined: \(H^{-1}\) does not exist on the kernel directions. Practical implementations paper over this by Tikhonov damping \((H + \lambda I)^{-1}\), but the \(\lambda\) is a free hyperparameter that changes attribution scores by orders of magnitude. EK-FAC is a structured approximation that handles some but not all degeneracy. The honest statement: "influence function for deep networks" is shorthand for "a damped pseudo-inverse approximation whose match to true LOO retraining is empirical and modest (correlation 0.3–0.5)." Calling its output a "first-order counterfactual" overstates the rigor. BIF reframes the problem in posterior-covariance terms specifically to bypass this issue.

TracIn (Pruthi–Liu–Kale–Sundararajan 2020)

TracIn skips the Hessian by integrating along the training trajectory. For checkpoints \(\theta_{t_1}, \ldots, \theta_{t_K}\) at intermediate training steps, define

\[ \mathrm{TracIn}(z, z_{\mathrm{test}}) = \sum_k \eta_k\,\langle \nabla L(z, \theta_{t_k}),\,\nabla L(z_{\mathrm{test}}, \theta_{t_k}) \rangle. \]

This is the cumulative first-order change in \(L(z_{\mathrm{test}}, \cdot)\) as \(z\) was visited along training (assuming SGD with one example per step). Advantages: no Hessian inversion; trajectory-aware. Disadvantages: requires saved checkpoints; assumes ideal SGD; doesn't extrapolate to counterfactual training (only attributes actual training events).

What TracIn captures that IF misses: path dependence. IF is a property of the loss function evaluated at the final parameter \(\hat\theta\). It is invariant under training trajectory: any two ERMs landing at \(\hat\theta\) get the same attribution. But networks remember their path — initialization, data order, learning-rate schedule. If \(z\) was seen early (when gradients were large) it had outsized effect; if late, smaller. TracIn's \(\sum_k \eta_k\,\langle\nabla L(z, \theta_{t_k}),\nabla L(z_*, \theta_{t_k})\rangle\) literally integrates the gradient interaction along the realized trajectory, so a single \(z\) seen at one specific step contributes only at that step, weighted by the LR at that step. The cost is the converse limitation: TracIn cannot answer counterfactual "what if \(z\) had been seen twice" because there is no second \(\theta_{t_k}\) to read off — it attributes actual training events, not hypothetical ones. IF and TracIn are duals: counterfactual but trajectory-blind vs. trajectory-honest but actual-only.

Datamodels (Ilyas–Park–Engstrom–Leclerc–Madry 2022)

Datamodels regress the model output (e.g., margin on a test point) on the binary inclusion vector \(\mathbf{1}_S \in \{0,1\}^n\) of training subsets \(S\). Train many models on random subsets, fit a linear function

\[ \mathrm{margin}(z_{\mathrm{test}};\,S) \approx \tau_0 + \sum_i \tau_i\,\mathbf{1}_{i \in S}. \]

The \(\tau_i\) are the attribution scores: how much does including training example \(i\) shift the margin? Pros: agnostic to training algorithm; gives near-causal counterfactuals on subsets. Cons: requires many training runs (thousands), so only feasible for small models.

Safety relevance: provenance of misalignment

Concrete use cases:

  • Emergent misalignment provenance (Betley et al. 2025). A model fine-tuned on insecure-code completions generalizes to broadly misaligned behavior on unrelated prompts. TDA against this fine-tune asks: which fine-tuning examples most influenced the deceptive answers? Answers help diagnose why the narrow→broad jump occurs (e.g., specific examples that activate the broader "misaligned mode" latent).
  • Memorization auditing. When a model regurgitates a copyrighted or private string, TDA identifies the source training examples. This is the well-validated regime (small effects, large-magnitude attributions).
  • Capability-source tracing. When a new capability emerges with scale, TDA on different training-data slices distinguishes "knowledge from a specific subset" from "compositional capability across many subsets."
  • Pre-training data filtering. Identify training data that contributes disproportionately to undesired behaviors (toxicity, deception in CoT). The Anthropic 2024 work on influence-function scaling and the Grosse et al. EK-FAC implementation are the canonical references.

The honest assessment of TDA's safety value: it works well for memorization (a strong signal stands out), well-enough for fine-tuning effects (small datasets, identifiable contributions), and is currently weak for pre-training compositional behaviors, where the relevant "cause" of a capability is diffuse across millions of examples and no individual example has large influence.

Connection to B.3 singular structure

At singular minima, \(H\) is rank-deficient and the IF formula's \(H^{-1}\) is undefined on the kernel directions. The Bayesian Influence Function (BIF; 2025) treats this as: the relevant first-order object is the change in the posterior, not the point estimate, under a small data perturbation. The result has the form

\[ \mathrm{BIF}(z, z_{\mathrm{test}}) \approx \mathrm{Cov}_{p(\theta \mid \mathcal{D})}\big[L(z, \theta),\,L(z_{\mathrm{test}}, \theta)\big] \]

— a posterior covariance, computable via SGLD without inverting the Hessian. Equivalently, it generalizes IF to the local-learning-coefficient geometry around \(\hat\theta\). This is the singular-aware analogue of Koh–Liang.

Worked example: influence function on logistic regression (step-by-step derivation)

Binary logistic regression: \(L(z, \theta) = \log(1 + \exp(-y \cdot \theta^\top x))\) for \(z = (x, y)\) with \(y \in \{-1, +1\}\). We derive the influence function \(I_{\mathrm{up}}(z, z_{\mathrm{test}})\) from scratch and check its scaling.

  1. \[ \nabla_\theta L(z, \theta) = -\sigma(-y\,\theta^\top x)\cdot y\,x, \qquad \sigma(u) = \frac{1}{1+e^{-u}}. \]

    Direct chain-rule differentiation of \(\log(1 + e^{-y\theta^\top x})\). The factor \(\sigma(-y\theta^\top x)\) is the model's predicted probability of the wrong label — large when the model is uncertain or wrong.

  2. \[ \nabla^2_\theta L(z, \theta) = \sigma(\theta^\top x)\,(1 - \sigma(\theta^\top x))\,x x^\top. \]

    Second derivative drops the \(y\) (squared). Per-example Hessian is rank 1 in \(x\); the scalar prefactor \(p(1-p)\) is the Bernoulli variance — maximal at \(p = 1/2\) (the decision boundary), zero at confident predictions.

  3. \[ H_{\hat\theta} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^n p_i(1-p_i)\,x_i x_i^\top, \quad p_i = \sigma(\hat\theta^\top x_i). \]

    Empirical Hessian at the trained \(\hat\theta\). Full-rank whenever \(\{x_i : p_i(1-p_i) > 0\}\) span \(\mathbb{R}^d\) — i.e., enough non-saturated examples exist. Crucially full-rank because the model is convex and the optimum is unique; this is exactly what fails for neural nets (B.3 singularity).

  4. \[ \left.\frac{d\hat\theta}{d\varepsilon}\right|_{\varepsilon = 0} = -H_{\hat\theta}^{-1}\,\nabla_\theta L(z, \hat\theta) = \sigma(-y\hat\theta^\top x)\,y\,H_{\hat\theta}^{-1} x. \]

    Implicit-function theorem applied to the perturbed-ERM optimality condition \(\nabla\hat R(\hat\theta(\varepsilon)) + \varepsilon\nabla L(z, \hat\theta(\varepsilon)) = 0\). The Newton step \(H^{-1}\nabla L(z, \hat\theta)\) is the direction \(\hat\theta\) moves under upweighting \(z\) by \(\varepsilon\).

  5. \[ I_{\mathrm{up}}(z, z_{\mathrm{test}}) = \nabla_\theta L(z_{\mathrm{test}}, \hat\theta)^\top \cdot \frac{d\hat\theta}{d\varepsilon}\bigg|_{\varepsilon = 0}. \]

    Chain rule on the induced change in test loss. This is the general influence-function formula before specializing to logistic.

  6. \[ \begin{aligned} I_{\mathrm{up}}(z, z_{\mathrm{test}}) &= \big[-\sigma(-y_{\mathrm{test}}\,\hat\theta^\top x_{\mathrm{test}})\,y_{\mathrm{test}}\,x_{\mathrm{test}}\big]^\top\,\big[\sigma(-y\hat\theta^\top x)\,y\,H_{\hat\theta}^{-1} x\big] \\ &= -\sigma(-y_{\mathrm{test}}\,\hat\theta^\top x_{\mathrm{test}})\,\sigma(-y\,\hat\theta^\top x)\,\cdot\,y\,y_{\mathrm{test}}\,\cdot\,x_{\mathrm{test}}^\top H_{\hat\theta}^{-1} x. \end{aligned} \]

    Plug step 1 (gradient) and step 4 (Newton step) into step 5. The result factors as a product of three interpretable terms: uncertainty on \(z\), uncertainty on \(z_*\), and a Mahalanobis-like alignment \(x_{\mathrm{test}}^\top H^{-1} x\) — plus the label-agreement sign \(y\,y_{\mathrm{test}}\).

  7. Interpretation. \(I_{\mathrm{up}}\) is negative (training on \(z\) reduces test loss) iff (i) the model is uncertain on both \(z\) and \(z_*\) (otherwise one of the \(\sigma(-y\cdot)\) factors is near 0), (ii) labels agree, \(y\,y_{\mathrm{test}} > 0\), and (iii) inputs are aligned in the Mahalanobis sense via \(H^{-1}\). Sign-flipped on label disagreement: training on a same-input opposite-label point hurts.

    Each factor has a clear role; this is the "best case" for IF and the most-cited canonical derivation.

  8. Validation against true LOO. For well-conditioned logistic regression with \(n \gg d\), Pearson correlation of \(I_{\mathrm{up}}\) predictions against actual LOO retraining loss changes is \(>0.95\). For neural networks (with EK-FAC, careful damping), the same comparison gives correlation 0.3–0.5 — orders-of-magnitude worse, directly reflecting the breakdown of assumptions 1–3 in the main text (strict convexity, critical-point convergence, path-independence).

    The empirical gap between logistic and NN is the punchline: the formula is exact at one extreme and a loose proxy at the other, with no quantitative theory yet for the middle.

  • Influence function \(I_{\mathrm{up}}(z, z_*) = -\nabla L(z_*)^\top \hat H^{-1} \nabla L(z)\) assumes a strictly convex loss and \(\hat\theta\) being a true critical point — both fail for NNs.
  • For NNs, \(\hat H\) is rank-deficient (B.3 singularity); naive IF requires Tikhonov regularization or EK-FAC, and even then matches true leave-one-out only with 0.3–0.5 correlation.
  • Path dependence breaks the first-order story; TracIn integrates gradient alignment along the optimization trajectory, partially fixing this.
  • Datamodels are near-causal but require ~thousands of training runs; BIF (Bayesian Influence Function) is the singular-aware alternative computed via SGLD sampling.
  • Safety case: TDA underwrites provenance claims (which training data caused this behavior?). The narrow-fine-tuning → broad-misalignment phenomenon from B.2 is the canonical use case.

Key definitions and results

Influence function \(I_{\mathrm{up}}\)
\(I_{\mathrm{up}}(z, z_{\mathrm{test}}) = -\nabla L(z_{\mathrm{test}}, \hat\theta)^\top\,H_{\hat\theta}^{-1}\,\nabla L(z, \hat\theta)\); first-order change in test loss per unit upweighting of training point.
Leave-one-out approximation
Estimated change from removing \(z_i\): \(\approx (1/n)\,I_{\mathrm{up}}(z_i, z_{\mathrm{test}})\); valid to first order in \(1/n\).
EK-FAC
Eigenvalue-corrected Kronecker-factored approximate curvature; Hessian approximation used in scalable influence-function implementations.
TracIn
Trajectory-based attribution: \(\sum_k \eta_k\,\langle \nabla L(z, \theta_{t_k}),\,\nabla L(z_{\mathrm{test}}, \theta_{t_k}) \rangle\); no Hessian needed.
Datamodels
Linear regression of model output on subset-inclusion indicators; \(\sim\) thousands of training runs; near-causal.
Bayesian Influence Function (BIF)
Posterior-covariance attribution; bypasses Hessian inversion via SGLD sampling; singular-aware.
Memorization regime
TDA's most reliable regime: a small number of training examples drive a specific behavior, and influence scores stand out from the background.
Compositional regime
TDA's hardest regime: the behavior is the aggregate of millions of small contributions; no single example has identifiable influence.

Retrieval practice

Q: Derive the IF formula in one line from the implicit function theorem.
A: From \(\nabla_\theta \hat R(\hat\theta(\varepsilon), \varepsilon) = 0\) where \(\hat R = (1/n)\sum L(z_i, \theta) + \varepsilon L(z, \theta)\), differentiate in \(\varepsilon\): \(H \cdot d\hat\theta/d\varepsilon + \nabla L(z, \hat\theta) = 0\), so \(d\hat\theta/d\varepsilon = -H^{-1}\nabla L(z, \hat\theta)\). Chain rule on test loss gives the IF formula.
Q: Why is IF damping non-innocent?
A: Using \((H + \lambda I)^{-1}\) regularizes the inversion but biases attribution toward high-gradient examples; the choice of \(\lambda\) changes which training points appear "most influential" by orders of magnitude.
Q: What does TracIn fail to capture that IF formally captures?
A: TracIn measures the actual gradient interaction along the realized trajectory; it does not extrapolate to counterfactuals "what if \(z\) had been seen twice / never." IF formally answers counterfactual questions (under its assumptions).
Q: How would you use TDA to validate or falsify a hypothesis about emergent misalignment?
A: Identify a held-out misaligned behavior (e.g., specific deceptive response). Run TDA against the fine-tuning set; predict which training examples most strongly induce the behavior. Re-fine-tune without those examples; check whether the behavior diminishes proportionally to predicted influence. If yes, the TDA story is causal; if not, the behavior is compositionally produced.
Q: Why does TDA degrade as the model gets larger?
A: Hessian inversion gets worse (more singularity), capabilities are more compositional (fewer high-influence individual examples), and the cost of approximate IF scales poorly. BIF and unrolling-based methods are the current frontier.
Q: When does Datamodels beat IF?
A: Small models, expensive but tractable many-retraining setups, and when subset-level (rather than single-example) attribution is enough. Datamodels are near-causal; IF is an approximation that gets worse with deeper networks.

Problems

30 min · derivationProblem 1. Verify the IF formula on ridge regression. Setup: \(L(z, \theta) = (1/2)(y - \theta^\top x)^2 + (\gamma/2)\|\theta\|^2\); \(\hat\theta = (X^\top X + \gamma I)^{-1} X^\top y\). Compute \(I_{\mathrm{up}}((x_i, y_i), (x_{\mathrm{test}}, y_{\mathrm{test}}))\) from the formula, and verify it agrees with the exact analytic LOO formula \(\hat\theta_{-i} = \hat\theta - (1/(1-h_{ii}))\,(X^\top X + \gamma I)^{-1} x_i (y_i - x_i^\top \hat\theta)\) (Sherman–Morrison), to first order in \(1/n\).
Solution sketch. \(\nabla L(z, \hat\theta) = -(y - \hat\theta^\top x)\,x + \gamma \hat\theta\) (the \(\gamma\hat\theta\) term is from the regularizer; cancels in the LOO comparison since it's the same on both sides). Hessian \(H = (1/n) X^\top X + \gamma I\). Plug into IF formula:
\(I_{\mathrm{up}} = -r_{\mathrm{test}}\,x_{\mathrm{test}}^\top (X^\top X + n\gamma I)^{-1}(-r_i x_i / n)\) where \(r_i = y_i - x_i^\top \hat\theta\) is the residual. So per-LOO change: \((1/n)\,I_{\mathrm{up}} = (r_{\mathrm{test}}\,r_i / n^2)\,x_{\mathrm{test}}^\top H^{-1} x_i / (1 + \text{something})\) — first-order match to the Sherman–Morrison exact form. The discrepancy is \(O(1/n^2)\), controlled by \(h_{ii}\) leverage.
1–2 hr · openProblem 2. Design a scenario-analysis protocol: you have a fine-tuned LLM showing a specific misaligned behavior (e.g., refusing on a wrongly-classified safe prompt). Outline a TDA-based investigation: (i) which method (IF/TracIn/Datamodels/BIF) you would use and why; (ii) what intermediate diagnostic checks to run; (iii) what causal-validation experiment closes the loop.
Solution sketch. (i) Fine-tuning means small dataset (e.g., 10k examples) and small parameter change → TracIn or BIF; BIF if posterior tools are available, TracIn if just checkpoints. IF with EK-FAC if neither. Datamodels infeasible at LLM scale. (ii) Diagnostic: rank training examples by attribution; check whether the top-K are semantically related to the misaligned behavior (face-validity); compute the influence-score distribution and look for heavy tails (a few examples driving the behavior) vs flat distribution (compositional). (iii) Causal close: remove the top-K examples; re-finetune; measure whether the behavior diminishes in proportion to predicted influence. If proportional → causal; if no change → spurious; if larger change → other examples are correlates of a deeper feature.

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Koh, Liang — Understanding Black-box Predictions via Influence Functions (2017) — the formal foundation; pair with Grosse et al. (Anthropic 2023) for the LLM-scale practicalities.

Optional depth: Bayesian Influence Functions (2025) — singular-aware attribution; MAGIC (2025) — unrolled differentiation for nonconvex; Influence Dynamics and Stagewise Attribution (2025) — training-time evolution of attribution; Emergent Misalignment (Betley et al. 2025) — the canonical case study for safety-relevant TDA; Pruthi et al., TracIn (2020); Ilyas et al., Datamodels (2022); Anthropic 2024 on influence functions at scale.

Solo assignment

Pick a small but real model+dataset (e.g., a fine-tuned BERT-small on a sentiment task, or a small LM on a narrow instruction-tuning set). (i) Implement an influence-function attribution (with EK-FAC or simple damping). (ii) Run exact LOO retraining for a sample of training points and compare predicted vs measured influence; plot the scatter. (iii) Identify one specific test-time behavior (correct or incorrect) and report which training examples have highest predicted influence on it. In 2 pages: assess where the IF approximation succeeds, where it breaks, and what you would change methodologically to deploy TDA on a frontier model.

C. Abstractions, Representations, and Interpretability

Four units that move from running experiments competently, to reverse-engineering trained networks, to formal theories of what a "good" representation even is. The shift across units is from artifact (the network) to substrate (the process being modeled) to abstraction (what observers must share to coordinate).

C.1 Intro to ML Engineering Experimental hygiene for interp/alignment research: minimal repros, sweep economics, and the bug-vs-result decision.
Prerequisites: PyTorch fluency, basic transformer architecture (attention, residual stream, MLP), familiarity with cross-entropy loss and Adam, and exposure to at least one published scaling-law plot (Hoffmann et al. 2022 "Chinchilla" is enough).

Empirical ML is a discipline of separating signal from artifact: most "results" you see in your own loss curves are bugs, leaks, or noise, and the cost of mistaking one for the other is months. The minimal-repro discipline, seed-variance budgeting (\(\sigma^2_{\mathrm{seed}}\) vs \(\sigma^2_{\mathrm{hp}}\)), and a fixed bug-vs-result decision tree are the three habits that distinguish researchers who ship from those who chase ghosts. Adopt them before learning any specific interpretability technique — they make every downstream method actually work.

Pre-reading questions

  1. When you see a loss spike at step 14,000 of a 100k run, what is your decision tree for "kill it" vs "let it ride"?
  2. You have 8 H100-hours. Do you do one careful run or eight cheap ones? On what does the answer depend?
  3. What is the smallest possible reproduction of a transformer training bug, and why does shrinking it matter?
  4. What distinguishes a "bug that looks like a result" from a "result that looks like a bug"?
  5. Why is "scaling law deviation" a cleaner signal than absolute loss when comparing two training recipes?

Core exposition

The minimal repro discipline

The single highest-leverage habit in empirical ML is producing a minimal reproduction of every phenomenon worth understanding. A minimal repro is a self-contained script that exhibits the phenomenon with the smallest possible model, dataset, and dependency surface — ideally one file, one GPU, under five minutes. It serves three orthogonal purposes. First, it forces you to verify the phenomenon exists in isolation, separating it from confounds in your training stack (mixed precision quirks, dataloader race conditions, learning-rate-schedule edge cases). Second, it makes the phenomenon shareable; a colleague can run it and debate it without provisioning a cluster. Third, and most importantly, it forces you to name the phenomenon precisely. If you cannot shrink "the model gets worse at math after RLHF" to a 50-line script with a measurable number, you do not yet have a claim — you have an impression.

Practical heuristics: shrink width before depth (a 2-layer, \(d=128\) transformer is far cheaper to debug than a 12-layer \(d=64\)); replace your real dataset with a synthetic one whose ground truth you control; remove every component (dropout, weight decay, learning rate warmup) that is not load-bearing for the phenomenon, one at a time; and only re-add components once the phenomenon is stable without them.

Why does the minimal-repro discipline matter more than fancier infrastructure? Because most "phenomena" disappear the moment you can no longer point at the exact lines of code that produce them. Distributed training stacks, wandb dashboards, and slurm orchestrators amplify whatever signal is real — and whatever artifact is real too. A 50-line minimal repro is the only place where signal-to-artifact ratio is yours to control end to end; everything above that layer adds entropy faster than it adds capability. The single best predictor of "is this researcher going to ship a result" is "do they have one minimal repro per claim, and can they hand it to me right now."

The single most common naive-ablation failure: reporting one seed per config and treating the resulting ordering as a "trend." In small-model interp work \(\sigma^2_{\mathrm{seed}}\) routinely dominates \(\sigma^2_{\mathrm{hp}}\) — your sweep is noise. If you haven't measured the seed-variance floor for your metric, you don't yet have evidence about hyperparameters.

Sweep-vs-replicate budget allocation

Given a fixed compute budget \(B\), the canonical decision is whether to sweep a hyperparameter or to replicate a configuration with different seeds. The framing that actually predicts your regret: estimate the variance of your metric across seeds at fixed config (\(\sigma^2_{\mathrm{seed}}\)) and the variance of the metric across the hyperparameter range you'd sweep (\(\sigma^2_{\mathrm{hp}}\)). The decision criterion:

\[ \text{sweep if } \sigma^2_{\mathrm{hp}} \gg \sigma^2_{\mathrm{seed}}; \quad \text{replicate if } \sigma^2_{\mathrm{hp}} \lesssim \sigma^2_{\mathrm{seed}}. \]

If they are comparable, you cannot tell apart a hyperparameter effect from seed noise, and you must replicate. The common failure mode is sweeping with one seed per config, observing a "trend," and reporting it — when in fact the per-seed noise floor is larger than the sweep signal. As a default for small-model interp work: at least 3 seeds per config before any claim, and a planned seed budget before you start the sweep. For frontier-scale runs replication is rarely affordable, which is exactly why frontier results are so often retracted in spirit by the next paper.

Debugging: signal vs bug

Failure modes during training cluster into a few archetypes, each with a diagnostic fingerprint:

  • NaN cascade. Loss becomes NaN at step \(t\); gradient norm spikes \(\ge 2\) orders of magnitude in the steps immediately prior. Almost always numerical — overflow in fp16 softmax, division by a zero variance in a normalization, or log of a clipped-to-zero probability. Diagnostic: add NaN-guard hooks on every forward output and bisect to the first NaN-producing layer.
  • Loss spike (recovers). Loss jumps then returns to trend over hundreds of steps. Often genuine — Adam's second-moment estimator getting "stuck" after a rare-token batch, or a rare data shard. Diagnostic: log per-batch loss and check whether the spike correlates with a specific data shard. If yes, it is a data artifact; if no, it is an optimizer state event.
  • Loss spike (no recovery). Loss jumps and trains a new, higher trajectory. This is the dangerous one. Usually optimizer divergence; the second-moment estimator has been corrupted and Adam can never re-stabilize. The Palm/OPT/BLOOM training logs all show this; the fix is gradient clipping plus optimizer-state checkpointing every few hundred steps so you can roll back.
  • Scaling-law deviation. Your run sits above (worse than) the predicted loss given its FLOPs. If the gap is constant in log-loss, it is almost certainly a recipe issue (LR, batch size, tokenizer). If the gap grows, it is architectural — you have a bottleneck that compounds with scale (most often: insufficient model width relative to data, per Chinchilla).
Is it a bug or a result?

When you see something surprising, the decision tree is: (1) Can a colleague reproduce your surprising number from your minimal repro? If no, it is a bug in your harness. (2) Does the effect survive a sign-flipped control (e.g., randomize the intervention and check the effect goes to zero)? If no, it is statistical noise or a data-pipeline leak. (3) Does the effect scale predictably with model size, data, or steps? If completely flat, suspect a constant offset in your measurement (e.g., a logging bug that always adds the same value). Only after these three pass should you write "we observe that…" The discipline of writing the null-result version of your own finding first ("if this were a bug, the bug would have to be X") is the single best calibration tool I know of.

Why this decision tree works: the three checks correspond to the three ways a phenomenon can be fake — harness artifact (reproducibility), statistical fluke (sign-flipped control), and measurement offset (scaling check). A finding that survives all three has used up its plausible failure budget; what's left is either the real mechanism or a fourth-order bug subtle enough to be a paper on its own. Almost no "surprises" survive step 1, which is why the discipline saves months.

  • Without a minimal repro, you have an impression, not a claim — and impressions don't survive peer review or your own future skepticism.
  • Estimating \(\sigma^2_{\mathrm{seed}}\) is prerequisite to any sweep; equation (C.1.1) is the actual decision rule, and most published "trends" violate it.
  • Loss-curve pathologies have distinct fingerprints (NaN cascade vs recoverable spike vs non-recoverable spike vs scaling-law deviation); diagnosis is by gradient-norm trajectory, not loss alone.
  • The bug-vs-result decision tree (reproduce → sign-flipped control → scaling check) eliminates ~90% of surprises before they reach a write-up; budget for this before writing.
  • "Bug-shaped results" — e.g., dedup leaks producing a memorization curve — look identical to real results until you inspect the actual examples; reading the data is non-negotiable.
Worked example: investigating a "memorization spike"

Scenario: you are training a 1.4B-parameter model on a code dataset. At step 18k of a 100k-step run, training loss drops sharply by 0.3 nats over 50 steps, then resumes its prior slope. Eval loss is unchanged. A colleague's first read is "the model memorized a chunk of training data."

Apply the decision tree. First, the gap between train and eval loss after the spike — is it larger than before? Compute it. If yes, memorization is consistent; if no, train loss simply hit easier data. Second, log a histogram of per-example losses at step 17,999 and 18,050. A memorization event shows a left-tail of near-zero losses on specific examples; a data-shard easiness shift shows a uniform translation. Third, replay the dataloader at step 18k with a different seed for the shard-permutation: does the spike still occur at the same step (bug in step counter), at a different step (data effect), or not at all (optimizer state effect)?

Suppose the histogram shows ~200 examples with loss \(< 0.01\) and the rest unchanged. That's the memorization fingerprint. Now the real question: is this a bug? Look at those 200 examples. If they are exact duplicates of held-out evaluation prompts that slipped through deduplication, you have a serious leak — a bug-shaped result. If they are common library boilerplate (license headers, imports), it is a real but uninteresting memorization event. The same loss-curve picture, two completely different write-ups. Until you read the examples, you do not have a finding.

Key definitions and results

Minimal repro
The smallest self-contained script reproducing a phenomenon; if you cannot write one, you have an impression, not a claim.
Seed variance \(\sigma^2_{\mathrm{seed}}\)
Spread of an outcome metric across random seeds at fixed config; the noise floor below which sweep results are uninterpretable.
Chinchilla scaling law
For dense LLMs, compute-optimal allocation has tokens \(\approx 20 \times\) parameters; deviations predict whether you are data- or parameter-bottlenecked.
NaN cascade
Loss \(\to\) NaN preceded by gradient-norm spike; almost always a numerical/precision issue, not a learning-dynamics one.
Optimizer-divergence spike
Non-recoverable loss spike caused by corrupted Adam second-moment estimates; mitigated by gradient clipping and frequent optimizer-state checkpointing.
Sign-flipped control
The same intervention applied with a randomized or inverted target; effect must vanish or invert, else the original effect is artifact.
Bug-shaped result
A finding whose mechanism is "your pipeline leaks information"; indistinguishable from a real result until you inspect the data.

Retrieval practice

Q: Why does loss spike then recover in some runs, but not in others?
A: Recovery requires the optimizer's second-moment estimator to remain uncorrupted. If gradient clipping is in place and the spike is data-driven (rare batch), Adam's running statistics absorb it. If clipping fails and the gradient updates push Adam's \(v\) to extreme values, future updates become near-zero in all directions and the model can't escape.
Q: Why is "sweep with one seed per config" almost always wrong?
A: You cannot distinguish hyperparameter effects from seed noise without a noise-floor estimate. If \(\sigma^2_{\mathrm{seed}} \approx \sigma^2_{\mathrm{hp}}\), your trend is statistically indistinguishable from random walks of three independent re-runs.
Q: What's the first thing to check when your run is above the Chinchilla curve?
A: Learning rate. Most runs that miss the law are simply under-LR'd at their batch size. The second thing is whether your token count is correct (cosmetic tokenizers can lie by 10-15%).
Q: When is a 2-layer \(d=128\) model not a valid minimal repro?
A: When the phenomenon requires representational capacity it doesn't have — induction heads (need \(\ge 2\) layers, fine), grokking (small-model is canonical, fine), but multi-step reasoning chains or in-context learning of complex algorithms can disappear below a width threshold. The honest answer: replicate at scale once after you understand the small-model story.
Q: You see scaling-law deviation that grows with FLOPs. What's the leading hypothesis?
A: An architectural or recipe bottleneck whose impact compounds — typically insufficient width for the data regime, or a normalization choice that becomes unstable at scale (RMSNorm vs LayerNorm interactions with bf16 are a classic).
Q: Why is gradient-norm logging non-negotiable?
A: It is the leading indicator for nearly every training pathology. NaN cascades, optimizer divergence, and dead-neuron events all show in grad norm 10-100 steps before they show in loss.

Problems

15 min · scenario15 min · scenarioProblem 1. You're running ablations for a paper. You have 200 H100-hours total. The full ablation grid has 32 cells. Each full run costs 8 H100-hours; each minimal repro costs 0.5 hours. Design your protocol.
Solution sketch. Don't try to run the full grid (would cost 256 hours). Instead: phase 1, run minimal repros on all 32 cells (16 hours) to identify the ~6 cells with the strongest effect signal at small scale. Phase 2, run 3 seeds \(\times\) 6 cells at full scale (144 hours) for the cells that survived phase 1. Phase 3, use the remaining ~40 hours for follow-up runs on whatever phase 2 surprised you with. Critical addition: in phase 1, also include 3 seeds \(\times\) 1 cell at full scale (24 hours) before phase 2, to estimate \(\sigma^2_{\mathrm{seed}}\) and confirm the small-scale ordering predicts large-scale ordering. If small-scale doesn't predict large-scale, you don't have a minimal repro and you need to redesign.
30 min · derivation15 min · scenarioProblem 2. A teammate reports that adding a particular auxiliary loss improves downstream benchmark scores by 1.2 points. You suspect this is noise. What four things do you ask for before believing it?
Solution sketch. (1) Number of seeds and per-seed scores — 1.2 points is within typical benchmark seed variance for many LM benchmarks. (2) Sign-flipped control — does multiplying the aux loss coefficient by zero (or randomizing its targets) leave the improvement intact? If yes, the aux loss isn't doing the work; some other change is. (3) The full eval suite, not the one benchmark — if 9 of 10 benchmarks moved by \(\pm 0.5\) and one moved by \(+1.2\), this is consistent with noise, and the report is a multiple-comparisons artifact. (4) The diff. Auxiliary losses often co-introduce changes to LR schedule, gradient clipping, or batch composition that account for the effect. Demand the actual diff, not the verbal description.

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Iliad ML Engineering track — the Colabs walk through experiment workflow at the right granularity; do the attention notebook even if you've built transformers before.

Optional depth: Attention from scratch Colab — the residual-stream view used in C.2; Runpod + VS Code remote — the actual GPU workflow you'll use; Tinker docs — the 2025 post-training API for fine-tunes without managing infra.

Solo assignment

Pick a tiny phenomenon you find surprising (induction-head emergence step, grokking onset, loss-curve kink in your favorite open run). Write a one-file minimal repro under 100 lines. Then write a 500-word memo: (1) the phenomenon's operational definition (what number, measured how); (2) the three plausible bug-shaped explanations and how you ruled each out; (3) what an adversarial reviewer would attack and how you'd defend it. Submit the script and the memo together.

C.2 Mechanistic Interpretability Circuits, superposition, sparse autoencoders, and the open question of whether SAE features are real.
Prerequisites: C.1; transformer internals (Q/K/V, residual stream as additive); linear algebra (subspaces, projections, rank); basic L1/L2 regularization; familiarity with the linear representation hypothesis.

Real transformers don't have one feature per neuron — they pack \(n \gg d\) features into a \(d\)-dimensional residual stream via superposition, exploiting sparsity to tolerate near-orthogonal interference. Sparse autoencoders attempt to recover the feature directions by solving \(L_{\mathrm{SAE}}(x) = \|x - \hat{x}\|_2^2 + \lambda \|f(x)\|_1\); the IOI circuit in GPT-2 small is the canonical existence proof that algorithm-level reverse-engineering is possible. Whether SAE features are "real" ontological units of computation, or partial duals induced by the \(\ell_1\) prior, is the open question.

Pre-reading questions

  1. State the superposition hypothesis precisely. When (sparsity condition) does it predict features are not aligned with the neuron basis?
  2. Write the SAE loss. What does each hyperparameter control, and why does L1 induce sparsity rather than just shrinkage?
  3. Name the IOI circuit components and the role each plays in subject-deletion behavior.
  4. Why is "feature splitting" evidence against treating SAE features as ground-truth ontological units?
  5. What would a refutation of the "SAE features are real" claim look like, operationally?

Core exposition

From neurons to features: superposition

The naive read of a network — "each neuron computes one feature" — is wrong for nontrivial transformers, and Elhage et al. 2022 ("Toy Models of Superposition") explains why. Suppose a network with hidden dimension \(d\) wants to represent \(n\) features with sparsity \(s\) (fraction of inputs where each feature is active). The superposition sparsity condition states that packing \(n > d\) features is tolerable when

\[ s \lesssim \frac{1}{d}, \qquad \mathbb{E}[\langle v_i, v_j \rangle] = O\!\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{d}}\right) \text{ for random unit } v_i, v_j \in \mathbb{R}^d. \]

Then the network can place \(n > d\) feature directions in the residual stream as a set of (approximately) linearly independent vectors, accepting controlled interference at the price of representational capacity. A sparse feature is recoverable as long as the simultaneously-active feature count is small relative to \(\sqrt{d}\): with \(k\) features firing, the noise in any one coordinate's readout scales as \(\sqrt{k}/\sqrt{d}\), giving a Johnson-Lindenstrauss-style packing bound \(n \lesssim \exp(c d)\) for any fixed interference budget.

The operational consequence: features in real transformers are directions in the residual stream, not neurons. Individual MLP neurons are polysemantic — they fire for unrelated concepts because each neuron sits in a basis that doesn't align with the feature basis. Reverse-engineering a network therefore requires finding the feature directions, not reading them off neurons.

Four distinct levels of abstraction get conflated in interp talks: neurons are the canonical-basis dimensions of an MLP layer (often polysemantic, almost never the right unit); SAE features are recovered directions in the residual stream (candidate "concepts," each ideally monosemantic); attention heads are computational units that read from and write to the residual stream via Q/K/V/O matrices (the unit of attention pattern analysis); circuits are subgraphs of heads + MLPs + features that jointly implement a specific algorithm (e.g., IOI, induction). A claim about features is not a claim about heads; a claim about heads is not a claim about circuits.

Sparse autoencoders (SAEs)

An SAE is a one-hidden-layer autoencoder trained on residual-stream activations \(x \in \mathbb{R}^d\), with hidden dimension \(m \gg d\) (often \(m = 8d\) to \(64d\)), encoder \(f(x) = \mathrm{ReLU}(W_e x + b_e)\), decoder \(\hat{x} = W_d f(x) + b_d\), and loss

\[ L_{\mathrm{SAE}}(x) = \| x - \hat{x} \|_2^2 + \lambda \, \| f(x) \|_1. \]

The reconstruction term forces the dictionary \(W_d\) to span the activation manifold; the \(\ell_1\) penalty pushes \(f(x)\) toward sparse, ideally one-hot codes. The hope: each hidden unit \(f_i\) corresponds to a single interpretable feature, and the columns of \(W_d\) are the recovered feature directions (monosemanticity). \(\lambda\) trades reconstruction error against sparsity; too low and you recover a dense PCA-like basis, too high and you collapse to a few features that miss most of the variance.

Two prominent variants. Top-k SAE (Makhzani & Frey; Gao et al. 2024 at OpenAI) replaces \(\ell_1\) with a hard top-\(k\) constraint, giving the objective

\[ L_{\mathrm{TopK}}(x) = \| x - W_d \, \mathrm{TopK}_k\!\big(W_e x + b_e\big) \|_2^2, \qquad \mathrm{TopK}_k(z)_i = z_i \cdot \mathbb{1}\!\big[z_i \in \text{top-}k(z)\big]. \]

This removes the shrinkage bias \(\ell_1\) imposes on active features (\(\ell_1\) penalizes their magnitudes too, biasing decoder columns to compensate by growing) and tends to give cleaner reconstruction-vs-sparsity Pareto fronts. Gated SAEs (Rajamanoharan et al. 2024) separate the "is this feature active" gate from the "how much" magnitude, recovering the \(\ell_1\)-induced shrinkage at the cost of doubled parameters in the encoder.

Feature splitting plus dictionary non-identifiability is the most serious open problem for the "SAE features = ontology" claim. Train two SAEs on the same activations with different \(m\) or different \(\lambda\) and you recover different feature sets — not because one is wrong, but because the SAE objective has no unique minimizer in the over-complete regime: any rotation that preserves sparsity gives a valid alternative decomposition. Engels et al. 2024 and Bricken et al. 2024 demonstrate empirically that two SAEs trained on identical data with different seeds can disagree on \(\sim 30\%\) of features at \(m = 16d\). If the SAE basis is non-canonical, then "SAE feature X explains behavior Y" is a claim about your particular SAE run, not about the model. Calibrate ontological confidence accordingly.

At scale, Templeton et al. 2024 (Anthropic) trained SAEs on Claude 3 Sonnet's residual streams, recovering on the order of \(10^7\) features including identifiable concepts ("Golden Gate Bridge," "code with security vulnerabilities," "sycophantic praise") that could be steered to change model behavior. This is the strongest evidence yet that something feature-like lives in the activations.

Circuits: the IOI case

The IOI circuit (Wang, Variengien, Conmy, Shlegeris, Steinhardt 2022, "Interpretability in the Wild") was the first end-to-end reverse-engineering of a non-trivial algorithm in a real language model. It established the modern mech-interp playbook: (1) define a behavior precisely on a templated dataset, (2) localize via activation patching, (3) name the heads by function, (4) verify by ablation that the named circuit accounts for most of the behavior. Before Wang et al., interp had induction heads (Olsson et al. 2022) and toy models of superposition (Elhage et al. 2022) but no scaled-up algorithmic story. After IOI, dozens of follow-up circuit papers (greater-than detection, modular arithmetic, docstring completion) used the same methodology, and the field's burden of proof shifted from "show interesting heads" to "show a discoverable, intervention-validated algorithm." It also revealed the redundancy problem (Backup Name Movers) that made single-head ablations misleading and motivated path patching as the standard tool.

Beyond features, the goal is to identify circuits: subgraphs of the computation that implement a specific algorithm. The canonical worked case is Indirect Object Identification (IOI; Wang et al. 2022) in GPT-2 small. Task: given "When Mary and John went to the store, John gave a drink to ___", predict "Mary." Wang et al. identify, by causal intervention (activation patching), a circuit spanning ~26 attention heads organized into named functional groups:

  • Duplicate Token Heads (e.g., 0.1, 0.10, 3.0): in early layers, attend from the second occurrence of a name back to the first. These flag which tokens are repeated.
  • Previous Token Heads (e.g., 2.2, 4.11): attend to the token immediately preceding the current one; supply positional structure.
  • S-Inhibition Heads (e.g., 7.3, 7.9, 8.6, 8.10): write a signal into the residual stream at the END position that says "the subject token is duplicate; do not copy it." They suppress the wrong name.
  • Name Mover Heads (e.g., 9.9, 9.6, 10.0): in late layers, attend from the END position to the indirect object (Mary) and copy its embedding to the output via their OV circuits. These are the heads that actually write the answer.
  • Backup Name Movers (e.g., 9.0, 9.7, 10.10, 10.6, 11.2, 11.9): redundant copies that activate when primary name movers are ablated. Their existence means single-head ablations underestimate causal importance — a robustness property of the learned circuit, not the experimenter's tool.
  • Negative Name Movers (e.g., 10.7, 11.10): heads that decrease the logit of the correct name, partially canceling the name movers' output. They appear to implement a calibration mechanism.

The IOI circuit is the existence proof that mechanistic interpretation at the level of algorithm-implementing subgraphs is possible. It is also a sobering anchor: 26 heads for a 7-word sentence in a 12-layer model. Scaling this style of analysis to GPT-4 via case-by-case manual investigation is hopeless, which is why SAEs and automated attribution methods (Marks et al. 2024 sparse feature circuits; Anthropic's 2025 attribution graphs) are the active frontier.

Are SAE features real?

The most important open question in mech interp 2025. Three concerns:

(1) Feature splitting / non-identifiability. Train two SAEs on the same activations with different dictionary sizes or different \(\ell_1\) coefficients. You get different feature sets. A "Golden Gate Bridge" feature at \(m = 8d\) splits into "Golden Gate at night," "Golden Gate photographed," "Golden Gate mentioned in poetry," etc. at \(m = 64d\). This is not pathology — finer dictionaries can resolve finer features — but it means there is no canonical SAE and "the feature" is not a well-defined object. Engels et al. and Bricken et al. have argued this undermines SAE-as-ontology claims.

(2) Reconstruction loss vs interpretability. Lower \(L_{\mathrm{recon}}\) at fixed sparsity does not entail more interpretable features. SAEs can find sparse codes that happen to be dense in semantic content (a single feature firing for many concepts that co-occur in training data) and pass quantitative monosemanticity checks while still being interpretively useless.

(3) The "dark matter." SAEs reconstruct ~80-90% of activation variance in practice. The missing 10-20% may contain the most important computation (rare features, dense computations not in superposition). Recent crosscoder / transcoder methods (Anthropic 2024-2025) aim to fix this by reconstructing function, not just activations: a transcoder is trained to predict an MLP's output from a sparse code of its input, capturing the input \(\to\) output mapping the MLP implements rather than the activations it produces.

The honest summary: SAEs find something that often looks like features, and steering on these directions changes model behavior in semantically coherent ways. Whether this "something" is the ontology the network is "really" using, or an interpretable but partial dual basis induced by the loss, is unsettled.

You might wonder: are SAE features "real" in the same sense a head is real? Here's a sharpening. A head is a fixed projection \(W_O W_V\) in the model's weights — there's no dispute about its existence; reverse-engineering its function is what's hard. An SAE feature is a column of \(W_d\) in an auxiliary model trained post hoc with an \(\ell_1\) prior; its existence is conditional on the SAE training run. Two operational tests for "real": (1) does the feature direction survive across seeds and dictionary sizes (most don't, fully); (2) does intervention on it predict downstream behavior in a way that other parameter-matched directions (random unit vectors, PCA components) do not (often yes — this is the strongest evidence). Anthropic's 2024-2025 work argues the second test is what matters; critics counter that the first is the prerequisite for ontology claims. Both can be right — features may be a useful operational handle without being the network's "native" representation.

Worked example: superposition condition and SAE recovery

Consider a toy: \(d = 4\), true features \(n = 8\), each feature independently active with probability \(p = 0.1\). Sparsity \(s = p = 0.1\); the condition \(s < 1/d = 0.25\) holds, so superposition is feasible. Generate ground-truth feature directions \(v_1, \ldots, v_8 \in \mathbb{R}^4\) as random unit vectors; generate activations \(x = \sum_i a_i v_i\) where \(a_i\) is a half-normal with prob \(p\), zero otherwise.

Train an SAE with hidden dim \(m = 16\) and \(\lambda\) chosen so average sparsity \(\approx 0.1 \cdot 8 = 0.8\) features per example. After training, compute the maximum cosine similarity between each true \(v_i\) and each SAE decoder column. You'll typically see 7 or 8 of the true features recovered with \(\cos > 0.9\) and the remaining SAE columns being either redundant copies (two columns at \(\cos > 0.8\) with the same \(v_i\)) or dead. This matches the empirical phenomenology at scale: dictionary slightly larger than feature count gives near-perfect recovery with some dead units.

Now repeat with \(p = 0.5\) (dense regime, condition violated). Reconstruction error stays low but no decoder column has \(\cos > 0.6\) with any single \(v_i\) — the SAE recovers a rotated mixture, not the original basis. This is the most mechanically clean version of the identifiability concern: when features aren't sparse in the data, the SAE basis is not the feature basis even if both exist.

  • Superposition: when feature sparsity \(s \lesssim 1/d\), networks can represent more features than dimensions by placing them along nearly-orthogonal directions with controlled interference \(\langle v_i, v_j\rangle \sim O(1/\sqrt{d})\).
  • SAE objective \(\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{SAE}} = \|x - \hat x\|^2 + \lambda\|f(x)\|_1\) recovers features assuming sparsity; top-k and gated variants address dead features and L1-shrinkage bias.
  • IOI circuit (Wang et al. 2022): Duplicate/Previous Token heads → S-Inhibition heads → Name Movers (with Backup and Negative variants) — a discoverable, named algorithm in a 124M-param GPT-2.
  • SAE-as-ontology is contested: feature splitting (one "feature" at scale K becomes many at 8K), recon-vs-interp tradeoffs, and "dark matter" reconstruction error suggest SAE features are partly L1-shaped artifacts.
  • Practical bar: a circuit-level claim should reproduce on adversarial inputs, survive causal intervention (activation patching), and predict downstream behavior — not just look plausible on cherry-picked examples.

Key definitions and results

Linear representation hypothesis
High-level features are encoded as approximately linear directions in activation space; manipulation along these directions causally controls model behavior.
Superposition hypothesis
Networks represent more features than they have neurons by placing features along (nearly) linearly independent residual-stream directions; feasible when feature activations are sufficiently sparse (\(s \lesssim 1/d\) for the simplest analysis).
SAE
Sparse autoencoder with overcomplete dictionary minimizing \(\|x - \hat{x}\|_2^2 + \lambda \|f(x)\|_1\); columns of the decoder are candidate feature directions.
Top-k SAE
Variant that hard-thresholds \(f(x)\) to its \(k\) largest entries; removes the \(\ell_1\) shrinkage bias on active features.
Feature splitting
The phenomenon that increasing SAE dictionary size resolves a single feature into multiple sub-features; implies no canonical "true" SAE.
Name Mover head
In the IOI circuit, a late-layer attention head that copies the indirect object's token embedding to the END position via its OV circuit.
S-Inhibition head
Mid-layer head that suppresses the subject (duplicate name) token at END, enabling Name Movers to attend to the correct (non-duplicate) name.
Activation patching
Causal-intervention method: replace a component's activation with one from a different prompt and measure downstream change; the gold standard for circuit-level causal claims.
Transcoder / crosscoder
SAE variants that reconstruct function (MLP output, or cross-layer activations) rather than raw activations, aiming to capture computation, not just representation.

Retrieval practice

Q: Why does \(\ell_1\) produce sparsity instead of just small weights?
A: The \(\ell_1\) ball is non-smooth at the axes; the optimum of a smooth loss + \(\ell_1\) penalty typically lies on a low-dimensional face of the ball, i.e., with some coordinates exactly zero. \(\ell_2\) has a smooth ball and pulls all coordinates uniformly toward zero without producing exact zeros.
Q: Why are Backup Name Movers a problem for naive ablation studies?
A: Ablating Name Movers individually shows small effect because Backup Name Movers compensate. You'd conclude the heads are unimportant. Path patching and joint ablations reveal the full circuit. The lesson: redundancy in learned circuits means causal-importance methods must be designed to handle distributed implementations.
Q: You train an SAE and 30% of features are "dead" (never fire). Is this a problem?
A: Mild. Dead features waste capacity but don't corrupt the live ones. Often fixed by resampling (Anthropic, Bricken et al. 2023) — periodically reinitializing dead features to high-loss examples. If \(> 60\%\) are dead, your \(\lambda\) is too high or your dictionary too large for the data.
Q: What does steering on an SAE feature direction actually do, mechanically?
A: You add \(\alpha v\) (where \(v\) is the feature's decoder column) to the residual stream at some layer during a forward pass. Downstream computation reads this as an extra feature firing with magnitude \(\alpha\). If the feature is "real," output shifts coherently along its semantic; if it's a polysemantic artifact, the output drifts incoherently.
Q: Why doesn't reconstruction loss alone certify a "good" SAE?
A: Reconstruction is necessary but not sufficient. PCA achieves low reconstruction loss with dense, uninterpretable components. The interpretability bar is monosemanticity (features fire for a single concept) and causal effect (steering produces semantically coherent change), neither of which is directly optimized.
Q: State the superposition sparsity condition.
A: Heuristic version: features can be packed at density \(n > d\) when expected simultaneously-active feature count is small relative to \(\sqrt{d}\); for independent features active with probability \(p\), this gives the rough condition \(p \cdot n \lesssim \sqrt{d}\), or per-feature sparsity \(s \lesssim 1/d\) for the simplest \(n \approx d/s\) regime.

Problems

30 min · derivationProblem 1. Derive (informally) why \(\ell_1\) plus reconstruction induces an interference cost between any two feature directions \(v_i, v_j\) whose inner product is non-zero. Why does this create the "Decoder Shrinkage" bias that Top-k SAEs are designed to fix?
Solution sketch. When features \(i\) and \(j\) co-occur, their contributions to \(x\) are \(a_i v_i + a_j v_j\). The optimal sparse code \(f_i, f_j\) is recovered via the encoder, but at decode \(\hat{x} = f_i W_d[:,i] + f_j W_d[:,j]\). If \(\langle v_i, v_j \rangle \ne 0\), the \(\ell_1\) penalty makes it cheaper to under-activate \(f_i, f_j\) and compensate by inflating \(\|W_d[:,i]\|\) and \(\|W_d[:,j]\|\) (no \(\ell_1\) on decoder). The activations are biased low; this is "shrinkage." Top-k removes the \(\ell_1\) on active features (only the gating is hard, not the magnitude), so active features can take their natural magnitudes and decoder columns don't need to compensate.
15 min · scenarioProblem 2. You apply activation patching on the IOI prompt: replace the activation of head 9.9 at the END token with the activation from a counterfactual prompt where Mary and John are swapped. Predict what happens to the output logit on "Mary" vs "John" and explain in terms of the circuit.
Solution sketch. Head 9.9 is a primary Name Mover. Its END-position output is approximately "(indirect object name embedding) via OV." Patching with the swapped counterfactual replaces that output with "John" embedding instead of "Mary." Predicted effect: logit on "John" rises substantially, logit on "Mary" falls. Magnitude is partial because (a) other Name Movers (9.6, 10.0) still write "Mary"; (b) Backup Name Movers may compensate. Wang et al. report \(\sim 50\%\) of the logit difference moves, consistent with one of several redundant heads being intercepted. If you also patch 9.6 and 10.0, the effect approaches full inversion.
15 min · scenarioProblem 3. Construct a minimal toy where SAE feature splitting is forced. Specify the data-generating process and the resulting SAEs at two dictionary sizes.
Solution sketch. Data: 2-feature hierarchical generative model in \(d = 10\). Feature A = "animal" (direction \(u\), prob 0.3). When A active, conditionally fire one of two sub-features: A_dog (\(u + \varepsilon_1\)) or A_cat (\(u + \varepsilon_2\)), each with prob 0.5. Train SAE with \(m = 4\): it recovers \(u\) as a single feature (animal). Train SAE with \(m = 8\): it recovers \(u + \varepsilon_1\) and \(u + \varepsilon_2\) as two features (dog, cat). Both are valid sparse codes; neither is wrong. This is the feature-splitting phenomenon: the "right" dictionary depends on the resolution you want, and there is no objective stopping point.

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Anthropic's 2025 attribution graphs — best end-to-end view of the current scalable circuit story, with concrete case studies showing where SAE-based circuit tracing succeeds and where it doesn't.

Optional depth: Open Problems in Mech Interp (2025) — frontier critique, including the SAE-as-ontology debate; A Pragmatic Vision for Interpretability — why interp's value may be diagnostic rather than ontological; Neuronpedia — actually click around a real SAE; calibration for "what features look like"; SAE training Colab — train one yourself, with the dead-feature resampling trick.

Solo assignment

Pick one prompt-completion behavior in GPT-2 small or Pythia-160M (e.g., gender pronoun resolution, simple arithmetic, a specific induction pattern). (1) Form a hypothesis about which heads implement it. (2) Run activation patching to validate or falsify. (3) Pull SAE features (via Neuronpedia or a Gemma-2-2B SAE) at the relevant layer and identify the top 3 features that fire on your prompts. (4) Write a 2-page memo: what you found, what would falsify the hypothesis, and where you suspect the analysis breaks down. The deliverable is the memo, not the notebook.

C.3 Computational Mechanics ε-machines, causal states, statistical complexity, and why transformers learn belief-state geometry.
Prerequisites: C.2; HMMs (forward algorithm, belief updates); Shannon entropy; basic probability simplex geometry; linear algebra of stochastic matrices.

Crutchfield's causal states \(S(x_{\le t}) = \{x'_{\le t} : P(X_{>t}\mid x'_{\le t}) = P(X_{>t}\mid x_{\le t})\}\) are the unique minimal sufficient statistic for prediction. The associated \(\epsilon\)-machine is the smallest unifilar HMM equivalent to the process; its entropy \(C_\mu = H[S]\) — the statistical complexity — measures pattern, with \(C_\mu \ge h_\mu\) (entropy rate) and \(C_\mu = 0\) iff the process is IID. Shai et al. 2024 showed transformers trained on HMM emissions develop residual-stream geometry isomorphic to the mixed-state presentation, suggesting causal-state recovery is what next-token learners actually do.

Pre-reading questions

  1. State Crutchfield's definition of a causal state. Why is the equivalence relation on past sequences, not on hidden states?
  2. What is statistical complexity \(C_\mu\), and how does it differ from entropy rate \(h_\mu\)?
  3. For the even process, work out the causal states and compute \(C_\mu\).
  4. What does it mean for a transformer to "learn belief state geometry," and what would falsify this claim?
  5. Why is the mixed state presentation (MSP) the relevant object, not the ε-machine itself?

Core exposition

Causal states and the ε-machine

Computational mechanics (Crutchfield & Young 1989; Shalizi & Crutchfield 2001) asks: given a stationary stochastic process producing observations \(x_0, x_1, \ldots\) drawn from some distribution \(\mu\), what is the minimal predictive representation? Define an equivalence relation on past sequences:

\[ x_{\le t} \sim_\epsilon x'_{\le t} \quad \Longleftrightarrow \quad P(X_{>t} \mid X_{\le t} = x_{\le t}) = P(X_{>t} \mid X_{\le t} = x'_{\le t}). \]

ε-machines are not HMMs in the textbook sense, despite both being state automata with stochastic emissions. An HMM is a generative model: you posit a hidden Markov chain and emission distributions, then fit parameters to data. There can be many HMMs equivalent to the same process — the latent structure is a modeling assumption, identifiable only up to relabeling and (sometimes) not even that (Locatello-style non-identifiability). An ε-machine is a derived object: its states are defined directly by the equivalence relation in equation (C.3.2) on past observations of the process. It is unique up to relabeling, minimal, and unifilar (deterministic transitions given emission). Concretely: the same data-generating process can be represented by many HMMs but exactly one ε-machine, and the ε-machine is typically smaller than the HMMs you'd write down naively.

Two pasts are equivalent iff they induce the same distribution over futures. The equivalence classes are the causal states; the function mapping pasts to their class is \(\epsilon\); the resulting automaton (causal states + transition probabilities + emission probabilities) is the ε-machine. Crutchfield's theorem: the ε-machine is the unique minimal sufficient statistic for prediction; any predictor with fewer states either loses predictive accuracy or is non-deterministic in its state transitions.

The statistical complexity \(C_\mu = H[S]\) is the Shannon entropy of the stationary distribution over causal states. This is the irreducible memory cost of optimal prediction. Crucially, \(C_\mu \ge h_\mu\) (entropy rate) with equality only for IID processes — for any process with structure, you must remember strictly more than you reveal per step.

Why \(C_\mu \ge h_\mu\)? The entropy rate \(h_\mu = \lim_n H(X_n \mid X_{<n})\) is the information revealed per emission once you have all past context. The statistical complexity \(C_\mu = H[S]\) is the information your predictor must store about the past to reach that rate. Storing strictly less than \(h_\mu\) per step is impossible because the predictor's uncertainty about \(X_n\) is itself \(h_\mu\) — so its representation must carry enough state to produce a distribution of that entropy. Storing exactly \(h_\mu\) corresponds to "I store everything I'm about to reveal," which means the process has no structure beyond what its single next emission carries — that's the IID case. Any process with memory forces strict inequality: prediction memory is bounded below by the entropy rate, but pattern adds on top (you remember which distribution to draw from, not just the distribution's entropy).

The mixed state presentation

The ε-machine is the generator's view: each causal state has a deterministic transition on each emission. But a predictor that observes the emissions doesn't know which causal state the generator is in; it maintains a belief distribution over states. As observations arrive, this belief is updated by Bayes' rule. The set of beliefs the predictor can hold (as a function of observed history) is the mixed state presentation (MSP) of the process. The MSP lives in the simplex over causal states and has its own geometry: vertices, edges, fractal structure for certain processes. The MSP is what an optimal predictor needs to track; the ε-machine is what the generator needs to run.

A key distinction: generation requires knowing the ε-machine state; prediction requires tracking the MSP belief. For some processes (the ε-machine state is observable from emissions) these coincide. For most non-trivial processes they don't, and the MSP can have many more "effective states" than the ε-machine — sometimes uncountably many, if the belief simplex is densely visited.

Transformers and belief state geometry

Shai et al. 2024 ("Transformers Represent Belief State Geometry in their Residual Stream") trained small transformers on token sequences emitted by hand-designed HMMs (mess3 process, RRXOR process). After training, they applied linear probes from the residual stream to the ground-truth belief state at each token position. Result: for processes where the MSP forms a 2D or fractal geometry inside the belief simplex, the residual stream activations recover that geometry — projecting the activations to 2D via PCA produces a configuration visually isomorphic to the true MSP, including fractal substructure for the mess3 process.

This is a striking result for several reasons. First, the geometry is not on the generator's ε-machine (which is finite and discrete) but on the predictor's MSP (which can be fractal). The transformer is doing the harder thing — tracking the belief, not the state. Second, the linear-probe recovery means the geometry is encoded approximately linearly in residual-stream directions, supporting the linear representation hypothesis from C.2 with an unusually clean ground truth. Third, the geometry is recovered even on processes where the transformer's downstream loss is near-optimal but the MSP has many "states" that could in principle be merged for the same loss; the transformer doesn't merge them, suggesting it represents the belief rather than a sufficient statistic of the belief.

The follow-up work (Piotrowski et al. 2025 on constrained belief updates; the quantum/post-quantum GHMM extensions) develops mechanism — how attention plus MLPs implement the Bayesian update — and pushes to processes that exceed classical HMM capacity. The agenda is sometimes overstated: belief-state geometry is empirically present for toy HMMs trained from scratch, but extrapolating to "frontier LLMs represent the belief state of the data-generating process of the internet" is a long inference that the field has not validated.

How strong is the Shai et al. 2024 result, really? Three caveats to keep in mind. First, the HMMs are hand-designed (mess3, RRXOR, fanizza) with known MSP geometry — this is the cleanest possible ground truth, and the linear probe accuracy is the tightest possible signal. Second, the architecture is tiny (4-layer, \(d=64\)) and trained to near-optimal loss on a process whose entropy rate is known in closed form; the "transformer learns belief geometry" claim is for the post-convergence regime, not mid-training. Third, the MSP geometry is recovered via linear probe with full regression — the probe is a learned readout, so what's being claimed is "the MSP is linearly decodable from the residual stream," which is weaker than "the residual stream is the MSP." None of this undermines the result for what it claims, but the jump from "small transformers on toy HMMs encode the MSP" to "language models encode a Bayesian posterior over a data-generating process" is several inferential steps and shouldn't be confused with the empirical content.

Worked example: the even process

Define the even process as a binary stochastic process: emit 1's and 0's such that between any two consecutive 0's, an even (positive) number of 1's appears. Equivalently: after a 0, you may emit 1's freely, but you cannot emit another 0 until you've emitted an even number of 1's since the last 0.

The causal states: knowing the past, what do you need to predict the future? Exactly one bit — whether the current count of 1's since the last 0 is even or odd. Call these states \(S_E\) (even) and \(S_O\) (odd).

  • From \(S_E\): emit 0 with prob \(p\), stay in \(S_E\); emit 1 with prob \(1 - p\), go to \(S_O\).
  • From \(S_O\): must emit 1 (prob 1), go to \(S_E\).

For \(p = 1/2\), the stationary distribution and \(C_\mu\) follow from the transition structure, computed step by step:
