  1. \[ P = \begin{pmatrix} P(S_E \to S_E) & P(S_E \to S_O) \\ P(S_O \to S_E) & P(S_O \to S_O) \end{pmatrix} = \begin{pmatrix} p & 1-p \\ 1 & 0 \end{pmatrix}. \]

    Write the row-stochastic transition matrix over causal states. \(S_E\) self-loops on emission 0 (probability \(p\)) and goes to \(S_O\) on emission 1 (probability \(1-p\)); \(S_O\) deterministically emits 1 and returns to \(S_E\).

  2. \[ \pi P = \pi, \qquad \pi = (\pi_E, \pi_O) \;\Longrightarrow\; \begin{cases} \pi_E = p\,\pi_E + \pi_O \\ \pi_O = (1-p)\,\pi_E \end{cases} \]

    The stationary distribution \(\pi\) is the left eigenvector of \(P\) with eigenvalue 1. Writing out the two balance equations gives one relation \(\pi_O = (1-p)\,\pi_E\); the other (after substitution) collapses to a tautology, which is expected for a 2-state chain.

  3. \[ \pi_E\,(1 + (1-p)) = 1 \;\Longrightarrow\; \pi_E = \frac{1}{2-p}, \qquad \pi_O = \frac{1-p}{2-p}. \]

    Normalize so \(\pi_E + \pi_O = 1\). The general form holds for any \(p \in (0,1)\); at \(p = 1/2\) this gives \(\pi_E = 2/3\), \(\pi_O = 1/3\).

  4. \[ C_\mu = H[\pi] = -\tfrac{2}{3}\log_2\tfrac{2}{3} - \tfrac{1}{3}\log_2\tfrac{1}{3} \approx 0.667 \cdot 0.585 + 0.333 \cdot 1.585 \approx 0.918 \text{ bits}. \]

    Statistical complexity is the Shannon entropy of \(\pi\). Compare to the entropy rate: \(h_\mu = \pi_E \cdot H(p) + \pi_O \cdot 0 = (2/3)\,H(1/2) = 2/3 \approx 0.667\) bits. So \(C_\mu \approx 0.918 > h_\mu \approx 0.667\) — strict inequality, as expected for a non-IID process. Memory cost exceeds emission entropy by \(\sim 0.25\) bits per step.

Now the MSP. Watching emissions, the predictor's belief over \(\{S_E, S_O\}\) evolves: after observing a 0, the predictor knows the state is \(S_E\) (probability 1) — because \(S_O\) deterministically emits 1. After observing a 1 from a known \(S_E\), the predictor knows the state is now \(S_O\). Then after another 1, back to \(S_E\). So once a 0 has been seen, the MSP belief is always a vertex of the simplex (degenerate). Before any 0, the belief sits at the stationary distribution. The even process is "synchronizable": the MSP has only three points — the stationary belief, \(\delta_{S_E}\), and \(\delta_{S_O}\). Contrast this with the mess3 process used by Shai et al., whose MSP is a Sierpiński-triangle-like fractal — the predictor never synchronizes and the belief geometry is genuinely high-dimensional. The even process is the simplest non-trivial sanity check; mess3 is where transformer geometry recovery becomes visually striking.

  • Causal states are the unique minimal sufficient statistic for prediction — the equivalence classes of pasts inducing the same future distribution.
  • Statistical complexity \(C_\mu = H[S]\) measures pattern (irreducible memory cost of optimal prediction); \(C_\mu \ge h_\mu\), and \(C_\mu = 0\) iff the process is IID.
  • The ε-machine is the smallest unifilar HMM equivalent to the process; the mixed-state presentation tracks the observer's belief over causal states and can be fractal.
  • Shai et al. 2024 empirical: transformers trained on HMM emissions develop residual-stream geometry isomorphic to the MSP — causal-state recovery is what next-token learners do.
  • Comp mech is the right lens for "what should an optimal predictor compute?" — and gives a target for what trained models should learn if they're at the prediction-optimal frontier.

Key definitions and results

Causal state
Equivalence class of past observation sequences inducing the same conditional distribution over futures; the unique minimal sufficient statistic for prediction.
ε-machine
Automaton whose states are causal states and whose transitions are labeled by emission probabilities; unique minimal predictively sufficient representation of a stationary process.
Statistical complexity \(C_\mu\)
Shannon entropy of the stationary distribution over causal states; irreducible memory cost of optimal prediction. \(C_\mu \ge h_\mu\) (entropy rate).
Mixed state presentation (MSP)
The set of belief distributions over causal states an observer can hold given observed history; lives in the simplex over causal states; can be fractal.
Synchronization
Property that after some finite observation, the predictor's belief collapses to a single causal state (a simplex vertex); not all processes synchronize.
Crutchfield minimality
Any predictor with fewer than |causal states| states is either lossy in prediction or non-deterministic in transitions; the ε-machine is unique up to relabeling.
Belief-state recovery claim
Empirical finding (Shai et al. 2024) that small transformers trained on HMM emissions encode the MSP geometry approximately linearly in their residual stream.

Retrieval practice

Q: Why must \(C_\mu \ge h_\mu\)?
A: \(h_\mu\) is the rate at which the process reveals new information per emission; \(C_\mu\) is the rate at which a predictor must store information about the past. If \(C_\mu < h_\mu\), the predictor would be revealing more entropy than it stores about the past, which is impossible for a process with structure (only IID processes saturate the bound with \(C_\mu = h_\mu = 0\) for a degenerate generator, or in general \(C_\mu = 0\) only for IID).
Q: Why is the MSP, not the ε-machine, what the transformer represents?
A: The transformer is a predictor, not a generator. It only sees emissions; it doesn't know which causal state generated each. Its job is to maintain a distribution over causal states given history — exactly the MSP. The ε-machine state is the latent the predictor is trying to infer.
Q: What would refute the "transformers learn belief state geometry" claim?
A: A trained transformer that achieves near-optimal next-token loss on an HMM but whose residual stream does not linearly encode the MSP. Specifically: train on a process whose MSP has a known fractal structure; if PCA of residual streams shows a structurally different geometry (e.g., a smooth manifold instead of fractal, or a lower-dimensional collapse), the claim fails. Shai et al. showed this doesn't happen for their test processes, but it's an empirical claim and could fail for other architectures or processes.
Q: For the even process, what is the MSP at "no observations yet"?
A: The stationary distribution: \(P(S_E) = 2/3\), \(P(S_O) = 1/3\) (for \(p = 1/2\)). After any 0 is observed, the belief snaps to \(\delta_{S_E}\); after a 1 from a known \(S_E\), to \(\delta_{S_O}\); and so on. So the MSP has three points only.
Q: What is the relationship between \(C_\mu\) and the optimal predictor's representation dimension?
A: \(C_\mu\) is an information-theoretic lower bound (in bits) on what must be stored; the predictor's representation must have at least \(C_\mu\) bits of information about the past. For processes with finite causal states, \(\dim \ge \lceil \log_2 |\text{causal states}| \rceil\) is a coarser bound. For processes with infinite/fractal MSPs, no finite-dimensional representation is exactly optimal.
Q: Why is "generation easier than prediction" possible?
A: To generate, you only need to track which causal state you're in (the ε-machine state) — that's it. To predict, you need to track the belief over states given emissions, which can have a much larger or even fractal state space (the MSP). The asymmetry arises whenever emissions don't uniquely identify the state.

Problems

30 min · derivationProblem 1. Consider the "Golden Mean" process: binary, where the constraint is no two consecutive 0's. Construct the ε-machine, find the stationary distribution (parametrize by the probability of emitting 1 from the "free" state), and compute \(C_\mu\).
Solution sketch. Two states. \(S_A\) = "just emitted 1 (or initial)": may emit 0 with prob \(q\) → \(S_B\), or 1 with prob \(1 - q\) → \(S_A\). \(S_B\) = "just emitted 0": must emit 1 (prob 1) → \(S_A\). Stationary: \(\pi_B = q\, \pi_A\), \(\pi_A + \pi_B = 1\), so \(\pi_A = 1/(1 + q)\), \(\pi_B = q/(1 + q)\). \(C_\mu = H[\pi_A, \pi_B]\). At \(q = 1/2\): \(\pi_A = 2/3\), \(\pi_B = 1/3\), \(C_\mu \approx 0.918\) bits. Notice this is isomorphic to the even process's stationary distribution at \(p = 1/2\) — both are the same 2-state Markov chain with different emission labels.
30 min · derivationProblem 2. Show that for an IID process, \(C_\mu = 0\). Conversely, give an example of a process with \(C_\mu > 0\) but \(h_\mu = 0\).
Solution sketch. IID: all pasts induce the same future distribution (the marginal), so there's exactly one causal state; \(H[S] = 0\). Converse: a deterministic periodic sequence, e.g., 01010101... — \(h_\mu = 0\) (no entropy in emissions given enough history) but the predictor needs to know whether the next emission is 0 or 1, i.e., two causal states with equal probability: \(C_\mu = 1\) bit. This is the cleanest example of why prediction memory and entropy rate are different things.
1–2 hr · openProblem 3. Design a small experiment to test whether a 2-layer, \(d=64\) transformer trained on the even process represents the MSP linearly in its residual stream. Specify the training setup, the probe, and the falsification criterion.
Solution sketch. Setup: generate \(\sim 10^6\) tokens from the even process with \(p = 0.5\); train a 2-layer transformer with cross-entropy on next-token prediction until eval loss matches the entropy rate (\(= h_\mu\) for this process, computable in closed form). Probe: at each token position, extract residual stream after layer 1 and layer 2; fit a linear probe (least squares) from the 64-d activation to the 2-d ground-truth belief vector \((P(S_E), P(S_O))\) at that position, computed via Bayes from the observed emission sequence. Metric: MSE of probe predictions. Falsification: if MSE is not significantly lower than the MSE of probing on a randomly-initialized transformer (i.e., a chance baseline), the linear-encoding claim fails for this architecture/process. Pass: MSE \(\ll\) chance baseline and visualization shows the predicted belief points clustering at the three MSP points.

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Shai et al. 2024, "Transformers Represent Belief State Geometry" — the empirical bridge from comp mech to interpretability; figures alone justify the read.

Optional depth: Constrained belief updates (2025) — mechanism story for how attention implements Bayesian belief updating; Post-quantum representations (2025) — GHMM extension, when classical HMM capacity is insufficient; HMM/GHMM exercise sheet — work through the process-design tutorial before any modeling.

Solo assignment

Implement a 2-state HMM (e.g., the even process), generate sequences, compute belief-state updates from observations in closed form. Train a tiny transformer (2 layers, \(d=64\)) on the sequences. Fit a linear probe from each layer's residual stream to the ground-truth belief vector; report \(R^2\) per layer. Then run the same probe on a randomly-initialized transformer as a baseline. Write a 1-page memo: how cleanly does the geometry emerge, at which layer, and what would make you doubt the result (e.g., probe overfitting, baseline contamination)?

C.4 Abstractions and Latents Natural abstractions, good regulators, information bottlenecks, and why different minds might converge on the same concepts.
Prerequisites: Information theory (entropy, mutual information, KL); Bayesian networks; basic measure theory helpful; C.3 (causal states); familiarity with the alignment "pointers problem."

The natural abstractions hypothesis (Wentworth) claims high-dim systems admit low-dim summaries \(\Lambda\) with mediation \(I(X_i; X_j \mid \Lambda) \approx 0\) and observer-uniqueness \(H(\Lambda \mid X_i) \approx 0\) — a constructive read of the Conant–Ashby good-regulator theorem. Tishby's information bottleneck and the representation-convergence empirics (Kornblith CKA, Huh Platonic-representation 2024) are consistent with this, but Locatello et al.'s impossibility theorem says disentanglement without inductive bias is impossible — so any "natural" abstraction is natural relative to a prior, not absolute. The point for alignment: identifying the right abstraction is what lets you point at "human values" precisely enough to optimize toward them.

Pre-reading questions

  1. State the natural abstraction hypothesis formally. What "low-dimensional summary" and what "distance" is it about?
  2. What does the Conant-Ashby good regulator theorem actually say, and what does it not say?
  3. How does Tishby's information bottleneck principle relate to abstraction selection?
  4. Under what conditions can a latent variable be identified up to symmetry from data, and when is identifiability hopeless?
  5. Why would two architectures trained on the same data converge on similar abstractions, if they do?

Core exposition

The natural abstraction hypothesis

Wentworth's natural abstraction hypothesis (NAH; 2020-2024 series) is the conjecture that natural systems support a small set of low-dimensional summaries that are sufficient for prediction-at-a-distance, and that different observers will converge on these summaries despite using different sensors or representations. The formal version (Wentworth, "Natural Latents"):

\[ \begin{aligned} \text{(i) Mediation:} \quad & I(X_i ; X_j \mid \Lambda) \approx 0 \quad \text{for distant } i, j \\ \text{(ii) Redundancy:} \quad & H(\Lambda \mid X_i) \approx 0 \quad \text{for each } i \end{aligned} \]

Mediation says \(\Lambda\) screens off distant variables: once you know \(\Lambda\), the variables become conditionally independent. Redundancy says \(\Lambda\) is determinable from any single \(X_i\) — the information is spread redundantly through the system. Wentworth's main result: if a latent satisfies (i) and (ii) for a distribution, it is essentially unique up to isomorphism. Any two observers who find any natural latent satisfying these conditions are recovering the same object (modulo encoding).

"Natural latent" is not the same kind of object as "latent variable" in latent-variable models (LVMs). In an LVM (factor analysis, VAE, HMM), the latent is a modeling assumption: you posit it exists, then fit. Different LVMs can fit the same data with different latents — the latent is your construction. A natural latent is instead a property of the distribution itself: it exists iff conditions (i)–(ii) of equation (C.4.1) are satisfied by some random variable \(\Lambda\) on the joint distribution, and when it exists it's unique up to isomorphism. There may be no natural latent for a given distribution (most distributions); when there is, it's the same object every observer recovers. The shift is "latent variable as your model" \(\to\) "natural latent as a structural fact about the world the model is trying to capture."

The intuition: temperature is natural for a gas. Mediation: given the temperature, molecules in distant subregions become conditionally independent — the macrostate screens off the micro-correlations. Redundancy: any sufficiently large subregion of the gas tells you the temperature. The same applies to many physical and structural aggregates: pressure, chemical concentration, the orientation of a rigid object. Concepts in human language often correspond to such aggregates — which is the alignment-relevant claim: if AI systems learn the natural latents of the world, they may learn the same concepts humans do, making value transfer tractable.

Good regulator and information bottleneck

The good regulator theorem (Conant & Ashby 1970, "Every Good Regulator of a System Must Be a Model of That System") emerged from second-order cybernetics — Ashby's tradition of treating biological and artificial systems as homeostatic regulators against environmental perturbation. The original proof is short and information-theoretic: given a system \(S\), a regulator \(R\), and disturbances \(D\) with \(R\) choosing actions to minimize entropy of outcomes \(Z\), if \(R\) is optimal then there exists a mapping \(h: R \to S\) such that \(R\) and \(S\) become isomorphic. The theorem was philosophically influential for decades (cited heavily in cognitive science and predictive processing) but only recently re-entered AI safety discourse as a foundation for Wentworth's NAH and the "AI systems will necessarily develop world models" argument in agent foundations. The modern reading drops the strict Ashby assumptions (perfect regulation, no internal noise) and uses the structural intuition: control requires modeling. The strict theorem only proves it in a narrow setting; the alignment-relevant reading is the looser philosophical generalization.

Conant & Ashby's good regulator theorem (1970) states: "every good regulator of a system must be a model of that system." The precise version: an optimal controller \(R\) that minimizes the entropy of a system \(S\)'s outputs (given disturbances \(D\)) must implement a function isomorphic to \(S\)'s dynamics. The theorem is often invoked loosely to mean "intelligence requires modeling"; the technical content is narrower — it concerns optimal regulators of stochastic systems, requires specific information-flow assumptions, and depends on how "optimality" is defined. Still, the structural conclusion holds: any system that competently controls outcomes in a world will carry an internal representation isomorphic to the world's relevant structure. For alignment, this argues that capable AI systems will necessarily develop world models; the question is whether those world models share abstractions with humans.

Tishby's information bottleneck (Tishby, Pereira & Bialek 1999) is the complementary view from learning. Given input \(X\) and target \(Y\), find a representation \(T\) that minimizes

\[ L_{\mathrm{IB}}(T) = I(X ; T) - \beta\, I(T ; Y). \]

The first term penalizes how much information \(T\) retains about \(X\) (compress); the second rewards relevance to \(Y\) (predict). The IB principle predicts that optimal representations lie on a Pareto front between compression and prediction; as \(\beta\) varies, you trace out a hierarchy of abstractions. Tishby's later "deep learning IB" interpretation — that SGD passes through a "compression phase" — has been contested (Saxe et al. 2018 argue the compression phase is an artifact of activation function choice). But the principle itself is robust: any sufficient statistic for \(Y\) that is minimal lives on the IB curve.

Connection to NAH: a natural latent satisfying mediation is essentially a sufficient statistic for prediction-at-a-distance. Adding redundancy is the constraint that the representation be recoverable from any local view, not just from a central oracle — which is what makes it shared across observers with different sensors.

Identifiability of latent variables

When can data uniquely determine a latent? The general answer is: rarely. For standard latent-variable models (Gaussian mixtures, factor analysis, plain VAEs), latents are identifiable only up to rotations, scalings, or label permutations. Locatello et al. 2019 showed that for fully unsupervised disentanglement, no algorithm can succeed without inductive bias — the problem is provably underdetermined. Identifiability requires additional structure: auxiliary labels (Khemakhem et al. 2020, "Identifiable VAEs"), known symmetries, or assumptions on the latent's dynamics. Natural latents bypass classical identifiability by trading uniqueness-of-the-best-latent for uniqueness-up-to-isomorphism-among-natural-latents: if a latent satisfies mediation and redundancy, it is unique within that class. Whether any natural latent exists for a given distribution is an empirical question per distribution.

The Locatello et al. 2019 impossibility theorem is sharper than "disentanglement is hard" — it states that for any generative model class large enough to contain arbitrary rotations of the prior, there exist infinitely many models that fit any finite dataset equally well but disagree on the disentangled factorization. No algorithm operating only on i.i.d. samples can distinguish them. The mistake to avoid: assuming a method "found" the right latents because reconstruction is good and the features look interpretable. Both are consistent with a rotated decomposition where you got lucky on the labels. The theorem doesn't say identifiability is impossible when you add inductive bias (auxiliary labels, time structure, intervention, mediation + redundancy à la NAH) — it says you must add it, and you must make the inductive bias explicit. Any claim of recovered latents that omits the inductive bias is unprovable as stated.

Why convergence might happen — and what could prevent it

The case for cross-architectural convergence of abstractions: (1) natural latents are distribution-determined, so any sufficiently capable predictor of the same data should recover them; (2) information bottleneck pressure (compute, parameter count) pushes representations toward minimal sufficient statistics; (3) shared inductive biases in deep networks (locality, smoothness, low-rank) further constrain the accessible representations. Empirical evidence: Kornblith et al. 2019 ("Similarity of Neural Network Representations") show CKA similarities between trained networks of different architectures can be very high; Huh et al. 2024 ("The Platonic Representation Hypothesis") find vision and language models develop increasingly similar internal representations as they scale.

Against: networks of similar capacity can implement provably different algorithms for the same task (Zhong et al. 2024 on modular arithmetic — multiple distinct algorithms found in different seeds); SAE features split with dictionary size (C.2); and many cognitive concepts (legal categories, social roles) plausibly don't satisfy natural-latent conditions. The honest reading: convergence is empirically present for some abstractions (physical/perceptual) and contested or absent for others (cultural/abstract). The NAH is a research program, not a settled fact.

Worked example: temperature as a natural latent

Model a gas as \(N = 10^4\) molecules with velocities \(v_1, \ldots, v_N \in \mathbb{R}^3\), drawn i.i.d. from a Maxwell-Boltzmann distribution parameterized by temperature \(T\). The latent \(\Lambda = T\).

Mediation: \(I(v_i ; v_j \mid T) = 0\) exactly — the velocities are independent given \(T\) by construction. Distant molecules are conditionally independent given temperature.

Redundancy: \(H(T \mid v_i)\) is small for any single \(v_i\)? Strictly, no — a single molecule's velocity is a weak estimator of \(T\). But for any subregion of size \(k \gg 1\) molecules, \(H(T \mid v_{i_1}, \ldots, v_{i_k}) \to 0\) as \(k \to \infty\). The redundancy condition holds in the "any sufficiently large local view" sense, which is how Wentworth defines it for continuous systems.

Now consider two observers: observer A measures position-binned average kinetic energy in 100 spatial cells; observer B measures velocity-distribution moments (mean, variance) across the whole system. Both estimators converge to functions of \(T\) (kinetic energy \(= (3/2) k_B T\); variance \(= k_B T / m\)). Despite measuring different things, both observers recover the same latent \(T\) up to deterministic transformation. The NAH prediction holds: the natural latent is unique, and two observers with different sensors converge on it.

Contrast: try to apply the same analysis to "social class" in a population of humans. Mediation fails — knowing class doesn't make distant individuals independent (they share other latents: economic conditions, geography). Redundancy fails — a single individual's observable features only weakly determine class. The concept doesn't satisfy NAH and isn't expected to converge across observers. This is consistent with the empirical observation that human cultures disagree about social-class taxonomies in a way they don't disagree about temperature.

  • Natural abstractions hypothesis: high-dim systems admit low-dim summaries \(\Lambda\) with mediation \(I(X_i;X_j\mid\Lambda)\approx 0\) and observer-uniqueness \(H(\Lambda\mid X_i)\approx 0\).
  • Conant-Ashby good-regulator: every good regulator of a system must contain a model of that system — "controlling well requires modeling."
  • Tishby information bottleneck \(\min I(X;T) - \beta I(T;Y)\) gives a constructive recipe for sufficient-and-minimal representations.
  • Locatello et al.'s impossibility theorem: disentanglement without inductive bias is fundamentally non-identifiable — so "natural" abstractions are natural relative to a prior, not absolute.
  • Convergence empirics (CKA, mutual-NN decoding, Huh Platonic Representation 2024) are consistent with natural abstractions, but also with multiple alternative causal stories; this is not yet pinned down.
  • For alignment: identifying the right abstraction is precisely the "pointers problem" — what lets you point at "human values" precisely enough to optimize toward.

Key definitions and results

Natural abstraction hypothesis
For natural systems, there exist low-dimensional summaries sufficient for prediction-at-distance, which are recoverable from local views; observers converge on these summaries.
Mediation
\(I(X_i ; X_j \mid \Lambda) \approx 0\) for distant variables; \(\Lambda\) screens off long-range correlations among the \(X\)'s.
Redundancy
\(H(\Lambda \mid X_i) \approx 0\) for any local view \(X_i\); \(\Lambda\) is recoverable from any sufficient subset of the system.
Natural latent uniqueness theorem
Two latents satisfying mediation and redundancy for the same distribution are isomorphic; "the" natural latent is well-defined up to relabeling.
Good regulator theorem
Conant-Ashby 1970: an optimal regulator of a stochastic system implements a function isomorphic to the system's dynamics; "controlling well requires modeling."
Information bottleneck
Tishby et al. 1999: optimal representations minimize \(I(X;T) - \beta I(T;Y)\); trace out a compression-relevance Pareto front.
Latent identifiability impossibility
Locatello et al. 2019: fully unsupervised disentanglement is impossible without inductive bias; identifiability requires structure beyond the data.
Pointers problem
Human values point at latent variables in human world-models, not raw physical states; transferring values requires a correspondence between AI's latents and human latents.

Retrieval practice

Q: Why is redundancy alone insufficient for a useful latent?
A: A latent that's recoverable from any local view but doesn't mediate — i.e., doesn't screen off distant variables — is a copy of local information, not an abstraction. Mediation is what makes it the "right level" of summary.
Q: How does the good regulator theorem relate to mech interp?
A: It predicts capable AI systems must carry isomorphic representations of the structures they competently model or control. Mech interp is the activity of recovering those representations. NAH is the further conjecture that the representations match the human-natural ones; the good regulator alone is silent on which ontology the AI uses.
Q: Why is "convergence of abstractions" a stronger claim than "good predictors exist"?
A: Many distinct representations can yield equally good predictions (factor rotations, basis changes). Convergence requires that learning dynamics + inductive biases select the same representation across architectures. This requires either NAH-style distribution-determined latents or strong shared inductive biases — both empirical claims.
Q: What does Locatello et al. 2019's impossibility result rule out?
A: It rules out fully unsupervised disentanglement without inductive bias: no algorithm, given only i.i.d. samples from a generative model, can recover the true latents better than chance over all generative models compatible with the data. It does NOT rule out identifiability when you add auxiliary information (labels, time, intervention).
Q: Connect natural latents to C.3's causal states.
A: Causal states are minimal sufficient statistics for predicting the future of a temporal process. Natural latents are minimal sufficient statistics with the additional redundancy constraint: recoverable from any local view, not just the full past. NAH adds the spatial-locality requirement that comp mech doesn't impose; comp mech is the temporal analog.
Q: Why might LLMs and human brains share more abstractions than two LLMs share with each other?
A: Because they share training data (humans wrote the internet) and prediction objective (the next-token distribution is structured by human-produced language). Two LLMs trained on the same data with similar inductive biases should converge more than LLM-vs-brain; empirically (Huh et al. 2024), this seems to hold, but with significant overlap in human-LLM representations too.

Problems

30 min · derivationProblem 1. Let \(X_1, X_2, X_3\) be three noisy measurements of a common scalar \(\mu\): \(X_i = \mu + \varepsilon_i\), \(\varepsilon_i \sim \mathcal{N}(0, \sigma^2)\) iid. Show that \(\mu\) is a natural latent: verify mediation and redundancy quantitatively. Then construct a different scenario where the structure looks similar but mediation fails, and identify why.
Solution sketch. Mediation: \(I(X_i ; X_j \mid \mu) = 0\) because conditional on \(\mu\), the \(X_i\) are independent (the \(\varepsilon\)'s are independent). Redundancy: \(H(\mu \mid X_i)\) is finite (the \(X_i\) is a noisy estimate), but for any \(k\) local views, \(H(\mu \mid X_1, \ldots, X_k) \sim \log(\sigma/\sqrt{k}) \to -\infty\) in the continuous-entropy sense, so redundancy holds in the local-region limit. Counter: let \(X_1, X_2, X_3\) be measurements with correlated noise (\(\varepsilon_i = \mu_{\mathrm{shared}} + \varepsilon'_i\) for shared latent \(\mu_{\mathrm{shared}}\)). Then \(I(X_i ; X_j \mid \mu) \ne 0\) because the \(X\)'s share information through \(\mu_{\mathrm{shared}}\). Mediation fails; \(\mu\) alone is not the natural latent — \((\mu, \mu_{\mathrm{shared}})\) jointly is. Lesson: NAH conditions are sensitive to the joint structure, not just marginals.
15 min · scenarioProblem 2. Sketch an information-bottleneck argument for why a transformer trained on next-token prediction would develop representations approximating the causal states of the data-generating process. Where does the argument break down for non-stationary or non-ergodic data?
Solution sketch. Setup: input \(X\) = (past tokens), target \(Y\) = next token. IB minimizes \(I(X;T) - \beta I(T;Y)\). The minimal sufficient statistic for \(Y\) given \(X\) — for a stationary process — is precisely the causal state \(\epsilon(X)\). Any representation \(T\) that achieves the same \(I(T;Y)\) as \(\epsilon(X)\) must have \(I(X;T) \ge I(X;\epsilon(X))\) by data-processing; equality only for \(T = \epsilon(X)\) (or its image under a deterministic map). So IB-optimal predictors converge to causal-state representations. Breakdown: for non-stationary data, "the" causal state is time-dependent; the optimal representation must include time/context, and the simple ε-machine analysis doesn't apply. For non-ergodic data (multiple modes the process never bridges), the predictor's MSP includes mode identity as a permanent latent, and the natural-latent uniqueness theorem may yield multiple isomorphism classes.
15 min · scenarioProblem 3. Suppose two SAEs trained on the same model's residual stream extract different features (feature splitting from C.2). Argue whether this contradicts natural-latent uniqueness, or whether the two are consistent. What additional condition would have to hold for NAH to predict identical SAE features?
Solution sketch. No contradiction in principle. Natural-latent uniqueness says natural latents (satisfying mediation + redundancy) are isomorphic; it doesn't say all sparse decompositions are. SAE features at different dictionary sizes correspond to different resolution levels of representation, only some of which (if any) coincide with natural latents of the data distribution. For NAH to predict identical features, you'd need: (a) the SAE features are natural latents of the activation distribution (verifiable by checking mediation and redundancy on the SAE feature decomposition), and (b) both SAE training runs converge to the same Pareto-optimal point on the reconstruction-sparsity trade-off. Neither holds in current practice, which is why feature splitting is observed. This is the cleanest formal way to state why "SAE features = real features" is unproven.

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Wentworth, "Natural Latents: The Concepts" — the cleanest statement of mediation, redundancy, and the uniqueness theorem.

Optional depth: The Pointers Problem — alignment-side motivation for caring about shared latents; Condensation — alternative framing of modular conceptual structure; Iliad Abstractions hub — collected math refreshers and exercises; Exercise 1 — work mediation/redundancy on toy examples.

Solo assignment

Pick a concept you expect to be a natural latent (suggestions: object identity in vision, syntactic part-of-speech in language, market-clearing price in economics). Sketch a generative model where the concept appears, check whether mediation and redundancy hold formally or approximately, and identify the level of locality at which redundancy starts to fail. Then pick a concept you expect to be not natural (e.g., "fair price," "moral wrong," "art") and locate the failure mode in NAH terms. Deliver a 2-page memo contrasting the two. The point is to develop intuition for which concepts NAH applies to and which it doesn't — a load-bearing question for alignment.

D. Agency

Cluster D is the move from systems that predict or represent to systems that act: choosing sequences of actions under uncertainty to optimize a long-horizon objective. The four units traverse practical RL (what we actually train), idealized agency (what we wish we were training), embeddedness (why the idealization breaks), and world models (the cognitive machinery that closes the loop).

D.1 Reinforcement Learning MDPs, Bellman equations, policy gradients, PPO, GRPO, and RLHF as RL.
Prerequisites: Probability, stochastic processes, basic optimization, automatic differentiation, B.1 (transformer training), comfort with KL divergence and score-function estimators.

The whole edifice of practical RL — REINFORCE, A2C, PPO, GRPO, RLHF — is one trick (the policy-gradient theorem) plus a series of variance-reduction and trust-region patches. The score-function gradient \(\nabla_\theta J = \mathbb{E}[\nabla_\theta \log \pi_\theta(a|s)\, A^\pi(s,a)]\) is exact, but high-variance and unstable off-policy, so every modern algorithm is an answer to one of two questions: how do I estimate \(A\) with less variance (baselines, GAE, group-relative advantages), and how do I prevent a single update from collapsing the rollout distribution (KL trust regions, PPO clipping, KL-to-reference penalties). RLHF is just this same machinery with the reward function replaced by a Bradley-Terry-trained proxy, which is why a KL leash to \(\pi_{\mathrm{ref}}\) is the load-bearing detail rather than an afterthought.

Pre-reading questions

  1. Why does the policy gradient theorem let you ignore \(\nabla_\theta d^\pi(s)\), the gradient of the state visitation distribution?
  2. What does PPO's clipping actually clip, and why is the clipped surrogate not a true lower bound on the policy improvement?
  3. GRPO removes the value network. What does it replace as a baseline, and where does that fail?
  4. RLHF on LLMs is contextual-bandit-shaped for most prompts. When does the multi-step MDP framing matter?
  5. Why is on-policy importance sampling unstable, and what hacks make it work in practice (clipping, KL penalties, trust regions)?

Core exposition

An MDP is a tuple \((S, A, P, r, \gamma)\) with transition kernel \(P(s' \mid s, a)\), reward \(r : S \times A \to \mathbb{R}\), and discount \(\gamma \in [0, 1)\). A policy \(\pi(a \mid s)\) induces a distribution over trajectories \(\tau = (s_0, a_0, s_1, \dots)\). The return is \(G_t = \sum_{k\ge 0} \gamma^k r(s_{t+k}, a_{t+k})\), and the objective is \(J(\pi) = \mathbb{E}_{\tau \sim \pi}[G_0]\).

Value functions and the Bellman equation

Define \(V^\pi(s) = \mathbb{E}_\pi[G_t \mid s_t = s]\) and \(Q^\pi(s, a) = \mathbb{E}_\pi[G_t \mid s_t = s, a_t = a]\). One step of recursion gives the Bellman expectation equation:

\[ V^\pi(s) = \mathbb{E}_{a \sim \pi(\cdot \mid s)}\!\left[\, r(s, a) + \gamma\,\mathbb{E}_{s' \sim P(\cdot \mid s, a)}\!\left[V^\pi(s')\right] \right]. \]

Equivalently \(V^\pi = T^\pi V^\pi\) where \(T^\pi\) is the Bellman operator. \(T^\pi\) is a \(\gamma\)-contraction in sup-norm, so policy evaluation by repeated application converges geometrically; this is the engine behind both DP and TD(0). The optimality equation \(V^*(s) = \max_a[r(s,a) + \gamma\, \mathbb{E}[V^*(s')]]\) has a unique fixed point and an optimal deterministic policy attains it.

Policy gradient vs. value-based. Policy gradients optimize \(\pi_\theta\) directly via stochastic gradient ascent on \(J(\pi_\theta)\); value-based methods (Q-learning, DQN, Rainbow) instead learn \(Q^*\) via fixed-point iteration of the Bellman optimality operator and act greedily. Policy gradients handle continuous actions and stochastic policies natively but suffer from variance; Q-learning is sample-efficient and off-policy but unstable with function approximation (the "deadly triad": bootstrapping + off-policy + function approximation can diverge — see Baird's counterexample). Actor-critic methods straddle both: a critic supplies \(V\) or \(Q\) to reduce variance for the policy gradient.

Policy gradients

For parameterized \(\pi_\theta\), the policy gradient theorem (Sutton et al. 2000) gives

\[ \begin{aligned} \nabla_\theta J(\theta) &= \mathbb{E}_{\tau \sim \pi_\theta}\!\left[\, \sum_t \nabla_\theta \log \pi_\theta(a_t \mid s_t)\, A^\pi(s_t, a_t)\right], \\ A^\pi(s, a) &= Q^\pi(s, a) - V^\pi(s). \end{aligned} \]

REINFORCE → A2C → PPO → GRPO. Williams 1992 introduced REINFORCE (full Monte-Carlo return as the score-function multiplier; unbiased, ferocious variance). Sutton et al. 2000 formalized the policy-gradient theorem and the role of baselines. Mnih et al. 2016's A3C/A2C added a learned value critic for variance reduction and parallel rollouts. Schulman et al. 2015 introduced GAE (smoothly interpolating between TD(0) and Monte Carlo) and TRPO (KL-constrained trust region); 2017 simplified TRPO to PPO's clipped surrogate. Shao et al. 2024 (DeepSeek-Math) dropped the value critic for LLM post-training in favor of a group-relative baseline — sensible because most prompts are single-step bandits where a value function buys little. Each step is a variance-reduction or stability patch on the previous one, not a fundamentally new algorithm.

The key trick: although \(\tau\)'s distribution depends on \(\theta\) through both the policy and the induced state visitation \(d^\pi\), the \(\nabla d^\pi\) terms collapse by a telescoping argument (the "Kakade trick" / performance difference lemma). Concretely, the visitation telescoping reads

\[ \nabla_\theta J(\theta) = \frac{1}{1-\gamma}\, \mathbb{E}_{s \sim d^{\pi_\theta},\, a \sim \pi_\theta}\!\left[\, \nabla_\theta \log \pi_\theta(a \mid s)\, A^{\pi_\theta}(s, a) \right], \]

where the \(\nabla_\theta d^{\pi_\theta}\) contribution to the naive product-rule expansion cancels because expected scores integrate to zero. Using \(A\) rather than raw returns gives a control variate that reduces variance without bias.

Actor-critic estimates \(A\) with a learned \(V_\phi\). GAE (Schulman et al. 2015) interpolates between low-bias / high-variance Monte-Carlo and high-bias / low-variance TD(0) via the TD residual \(\delta_t\) and an exponential moving average:

\[ \begin{aligned} \delta_t &= r_t + \gamma\, V_\phi(s_{t+1}) - V_\phi(s_t), \\ \hat A_t^{\mathrm{GAE}(\gamma,\lambda)} &= \sum_{l=0}^{\infty} (\gamma \lambda)^l\, \delta_{t+l}. \end{aligned} \]

\(\lambda = 1\) is unbiased Monte Carlo minus the value baseline; \(\lambda = 0\) is the one-step TD residual. In practice \(\lambda \approx 0.95\).

PPO

TRPO bounds the policy update by a KL trust region. PPO (Schulman et al. 2017) replaces the constrained problem with a clipped surrogate. Let \(r_t(\theta) = \pi_\theta(a_t \mid s_t) / \pi_{\theta_{\mathrm{old}}}(a_t \mid s_t)\). Then

\[ L^{\mathrm{CLIP}}(\theta) = \mathbb{E}_t\!\left[\, \min\!\big(\, r_t(\theta)\, \hat A_t,\; \mathrm{clip}(r_t(\theta),\, 1 - \epsilon,\, 1 + \epsilon)\, \hat A_t \,\big) \right]. \]

The clipping is asymmetric: when \(\hat A_t > 0\) it caps how much the new policy can increase the action's probability; when \(\hat A_t < 0\) it caps how much it can decrease. The \(\min\) means the loss is a pessimistic lower envelope — but it is not a true lower bound on policy improvement (Engstrom et al. 2020 documented how PPO's empirical performance depends on code-level details — value clipping, advantage normalization, orthogonal initialization — more than on the clipping itself).

PPO clipping is a heuristic, not a rigorous trust region. TRPO bounds the policy improvement by a KL constraint with a real monotonic improvement guarantee. PPO's clip drops the constraint and just clips the ratio, which has no analogous guarantee — the clipped objective can still produce arbitrarily-far updates in directions the clip doesn't reach (e.g., off-clip dimensions can compound across minibatches). Engstrom et al. 2020's "Implementation Matters in Deep RL" empirically isolated the clipped surrogate from the surrounding code-level tricks (advantage normalization, value-function clipping, learning rate annealing, reward scaling, orthogonal init) and found that most of PPO's improvement over vanilla policy gradient comes from the tricks, not the clipping itself. Treat PPO as "vanilla PG plus a bag of stabilizing engineering" rather than a principled trust-region method.

GRPO and the LLM-RL line

GRPO (Shao et al. 2024 / DeepSeek-Math; also DeepSeek-R1) drops the value network. For a prompt \(q\), sample \(G\) completions \(\{o_i\}_{i=1}^G\), score each by a reward model or verifier \(r_i\), and use the group-relative advantage

\[ \hat A_i = \frac{r_i - \mathrm{mean}(\{r_j\}_{j=1}^G)}{\mathrm{std}(\{r_j\}_{j=1}^G)}. \]

Optimize the PPO-style clipped objective with this advantage and a KL penalty to a reference policy. This works on math/coding tasks because (a) most prompts are contextual bandits (single response, terminal reward), so a value function buys little; (b) verifiable rewards remove reward-model overfitting; (c) the group baseline is exactly \(\mathbb{E}[r \mid q]\) in expectation, so it's an unbiased baseline as long as samples are i.i.d. from \(\pi_{\mathrm{old}}\). Failure modes: when within-group reward variance collapses (all responses correct or all wrong) the gradient vanishes; off-policy drift between rollout and update degrades the importance ratio.

RLHF as RL, exploration, off-policy correction, reward shaping

RLHF on LLMs replaces verifier rewards with a learned \(r_\phi\) trained on pairwise preferences (Bradley-Terry). Because \(r_\phi\) is a proxy, you pay a KL tax \(\beta \cdot D_{\mathrm{KL}}(\pi_\theta \,\|\, \pi_{\mathrm{ref}})\) to bound the deviation — Goodhart pressure increases monotonically in the correlation gap between \(\mathbb{E}[r_\phi]\) and \(r_{\mathrm{true}}\). Exploration in deep RL is mostly entropy bonuses, count-based / curiosity-driven intrinsic rewards (Bellemare 2016; Pathak 2017), or randomized priors. Off-policy correction uses importance ratios \(\pi(a \mid s) / b(a \mid s)\) for behavior \(b\); pure IS has exploding variance for long horizons, so V-trace (Espeholt et al. 2018) and Retrace (Munos 2016) clip the per-step ratios. Reward shaping with \(F(s, s') = \gamma \Phi(s') - \Phi(s)\) (Ng, Harada, Russell 1999) preserves the optimal policy because the cumulative shaping telescopes to \(\gamma^T \Phi(s_T) - \Phi(s_0)\); any other shaping form can change the optimum.

Worked example: REINFORCE on a two-state bandit-MDP

Take a one-state MDP, two actions \(a \in \{0, 1\}\), rewards \(r(0) = 0,\ r(1) = 1\), policy \(\pi_\theta(a = 1) = \sigma(\theta)\) with sigmoid \(\sigma\). Then \(J(\theta) = \sigma(\theta) \cdot 1 + (1 - \sigma(\theta)) \cdot 0 = \sigma(\theta)\), and the closed-form gradient is \(\nabla J = \sigma(\theta)(1 - \sigma(\theta))\). By the policy-gradient theorem with no baseline, \(\nabla J = \mathbb{E}_a[\nabla \log \pi_\theta(a) \cdot r(a)]\). Compute: \(\nabla \log \pi_\theta(1) = 1 - \sigma(\theta)\), \(\nabla \log \pi_\theta(0) = -\sigma(\theta)\). So \(\mathbb{E}[\cdot] = \sigma(\theta)(1 - \sigma(\theta)) \cdot 1 + (1 - \sigma(\theta))(-\sigma(\theta)) \cdot 0 = \sigma(\theta)(1 - \sigma(\theta))\). Matches.

Now add a constant baseline \(b\): \(\mathbb{E}[\nabla \log \pi \cdot (r - b)] = \sigma(\theta)(1 - \sigma(\theta))(1 - b) + (1 - \sigma(\theta))(-\sigma(\theta))(-b) = \sigma(\theta)(1 - \sigma(\theta))\). The baseline cancels exactly because \(\mathbb{E}_a[\nabla \log \pi_\theta(a)] = \nabla\, \mathbb{E}_a[1] = 0\). This is why baselines reduce variance without bias — the score function has mean zero. Variance: with \(\theta = 0\) (uniform) and \(b = 0.5\), per-sample gradient is \(\pm 0.25\); with \(b = 0\), it is \(0.5\) or \(0\). Variance drops from \(0.0625\) to \(0\) at \(b = 0.5 = \mathbb{E}[r]\), the variance-minimizing baseline.

  • The policy-gradient theorem \(\nabla_\theta J = \mathbb{E}_\tau[\sum_t \nabla_\theta \log\pi_\theta(a_t\mid s_t) A^\pi(s_t,a_t)]\) is the score-function trick; subtracting a state-only baseline reduces variance without biasing the gradient.
  • GAE interpolates between TD(0) (high bias, low variance) and Monte Carlo (low bias, high variance) via \(\hat A_t^{\mathrm{GAE}} = \sum_l (\gamma\lambda)^l \delta_{t+l}\).
  • PPO clipping is a heuristic surrogate for a trust region; Engstrom et al. 2020 showed that the practical gains over vanilla actor-critic come largely from code-level tricks, not the clip itself.
  • GRPO normalizes advantages within a sampled group, eliminating the value model — the DeepSeek-R1 design that drove the recent reasoning-RL renaissance.
  • RLHF is RL: post-train via reward from a learned preference model + KL regularization to a reference policy. The closed-form maximizer \(\pi^*(a\mid s) \propto \pi_{\mathrm{ref}}(a\mid s)\exp(R/\beta)\) is the basis of DPO.

Key definitions and results

Markov decision process
Tuple \((S, A, P, r, \gamma)\) with Markov transition kernel; policies map states to action distributions.
Bellman expectation operator
\((T^\pi V)(s) = \mathbb{E}_{a \sim \pi}[r(s,a) + \gamma\, \mathbb{E}_{s'}[V(s')]]\). \(\gamma\)-contraction in \(\|\cdot\|_\infty\).
Policy gradient theorem
\(\nabla_\theta J = \mathbb{E}[\sum_t \nabla_\theta \log \pi_\theta(a_t \mid s_t)\, A^\pi(s_t, a_t)]\); visitation-distribution gradient telescopes away.
GAE
Exponentially weighted advantage: \(\hat A_t = \sum_l (\gamma \lambda)^l \delta_{t+l}\). \(\lambda\) trades bias for variance.
PPO clipped objective
\(L^{\mathrm{CLIP}} = \mathbb{E}[\min(r_t(\theta) \hat A_t,\ \mathrm{clip}(r_t(\theta), 1{-}\epsilon, 1{+}\epsilon) \hat A_t)]\).
GRPO advantage
Group-relative: \(\hat A_i = (r_i - \mu_{\mathrm{group}}) / \sigma_{\mathrm{group}}\); no learned value model.
Potential-based shaping
\(F(s, s') = \gamma \Phi(s') - \Phi(s)\) preserves the optimal policy; other shaping does not (Ng, Harada, Russell 1999).
Performance difference lemma
\(J(\pi') - J(\pi) = \mathbb{E}_{s \sim d^{\pi'}}[A^\pi(s, \pi'(s))] / (1 - \gamma)\); foundation of TRPO/PPO bounds.

Retrieval practice

Q: Prove the score-function baseline is unbiased.
A: \(\mathbb{E}_{a \sim \pi}[\nabla_\theta \log \pi_\theta(a \mid s) \cdot b(s)] = b(s) \cdot \nabla_\theta \sum_a \pi_\theta(a \mid s) = b(s) \cdot \nabla 1 = 0\). Any state-only baseline can be subtracted from the advantage without bias.
Q: Why is Q-learning off-policy?
A: The target \(r + \gamma \max_{a'} Q(s', a')\) uses the greedy policy w.r.t. current \(Q\), not the behavior policy that generated \((s,a,r,s')\). So you can learn the optimal Q while exploring with \(\epsilon\)-greedy.
Q: PPO's clip threshold \(\epsilon\) is small (0.1–0.2). What goes wrong if you set \(\epsilon = 1\)?
A: Clipping becomes inactive for any reasonable ratio. The update becomes unconstrained importance-sampled vanilla policy gradient, and a single batch with high \(r_t\) can move the policy arbitrarily far from \(\pi_{\mathrm{old}}\) — distribution shift kills the next iteration's samples.
Q: When does GRPO's advantage collapse?
A: When \(\mathrm{std}(r)\) in the group is near zero — all completions get the same reward (all correct, all wrong, or all reward-model-scored similarly). The normalized advantage is undefined or noise-dominated; gradient vanishes. Mitigations: filter out zero-variance prompts or oversample.
Q: Why does RLHF need a KL penalty to the reference?
A: The reward model \(r_\phi\) is fit on the on-distribution preference data. Off-distribution, it's a misspecified proxy. A KL trust region \(\beta \cdot D_{\mathrm{KL}}(\pi_\theta \,\|\, \pi_{\mathrm{ref}})\) keeps \(\pi_\theta\) on the manifold where \(r_\phi\) generalizes. The fixed-point under this objective is exactly \(\pi_\theta(y \mid x) \propto \pi_{\mathrm{ref}}(y \mid x) \exp(r_\phi(x, y) / \beta)\) — DPO's whole derivation.
Q: What does TD(0) approximate, and to what does it converge?
A: Sample-based stochastic fixed-point iteration of \(T^\pi\). Under Robbins-Monro step sizes and linear function approximation with on-policy sampling, converges to the projected Bellman fixed point (Tsitsiklis & Van Roy 1997). Off-policy linear TD can diverge — Baird's counterexample.
Q: REINFORCE vs actor-critic in one line.
A: REINFORCE uses full Monte-Carlo returns as the advantage estimator (unbiased, high variance); actor-critic plugs in \(r + \gamma V_\phi(s') - V_\phi(s)\) (biased while \(V_\phi\) is learning, low variance). GAE smoothly interpolates.

Problems

30 min · derivationProblem 1. Show that the variance-minimizing constant baseline for REINFORCE is \(b^* = \mathbb{E}[\|\nabla \log \pi(a \mid s)\|^2\, r] / \mathbb{E}[\|\nabla \log \pi(a \mid s)\|^2]\), not \(\mathbb{E}[r]\).
Solution sketch. Let \(g(a; b) = \nabla \log \pi(a) (r(a) - b)\). \(\mathrm{Var}(g) = \mathbb{E}[\|\nabla \log \pi\|^2 (r - b)^2] - \|\mathbb{E}[g]\|^2\); the second term is independent of \(b\). Differentiate \(\mathbb{E}[\|\nabla \log \pi\|^2 (r - b)^2]\) in \(b\): \(-2\, \mathbb{E}[\|\nabla \log \pi\|^2 (r - b)] = 0\) gives \(b^* = \mathbb{E}[\|\nabla \log \pi\|^2 r] / \mathbb{E}[\|\nabla \log \pi\|^2]\). This is the score-weighted mean reward; only equals \(\mathbb{E}[r]\) when \(\|\nabla \log \pi\|^2\) is constant across actions (e.g. uniform binary policy).
30 min · derivationProblem 2. Derive GAE as the exponentially-weighted average of \(k\)-step advantage estimators \(\hat A_t^{(k)} = \sum_{i=0}^{k-1} \gamma^i r_{t+i} + \gamma^k V(s_{t+k}) - V(s_t)\).
Solution sketch. Define \(\hat A_t(\lambda) = (1 - \lambda) \sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \lambda^{k-1} \hat A_t^{(k)}\). Note \(\hat A_t^{(k)} = \sum_{i=0}^{k-1} \gamma^i \delta_{t+i}\) by telescoping (each \(V\) term cancels with the next). Substitute and swap sums: \((1 - \lambda) \sum_{k \ge 1} \lambda^{k-1} \sum_{i=0}^{k-1} \gamma^i \delta_{t+i} = (1 - \lambda) \sum_{i \ge 0} \gamma^i \delta_{t+i} \sum_{k > i} \lambda^{k-1} = (1 - \lambda) \sum_i \gamma^i \delta_{t+i} \cdot \lambda^i / (1 - \lambda) = \sum_i (\gamma \lambda)^i \delta_{t+i}\).
30 min · derivationProblem 3. Prove that for any potential \(\Phi : S \to \mathbb{R}\), the shaped MDP with \(r'(s,a,s') = r(s,a) + \gamma \Phi(s') - \Phi(s)\) has the same optimal policy as the original.
Solution sketch. Let \(V'^\pi\) be the shaped value. By the Bellman equation, \(V'^\pi(s) = \mathbb{E}[r + \gamma \Phi(s') - \Phi(s) + \gamma V'^\pi(s')]\). Define \(\tilde V^\pi(s) = V'^\pi(s) + \Phi(s)\). Then \(\tilde V^\pi(s) = \mathbb{E}[r + \gamma(\Phi(s') + V'^\pi(s'))] = \mathbb{E}[r + \gamma \tilde V^\pi(s')]\), so \(\tilde V^\pi = V^\pi\). Hence \(V'^\pi = V^\pi - \Phi\), a policy-independent constant shift in \(\pi\): \(\arg\max_\pi V'^\pi = \arg\max_\pi V^\pi\).

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Sutton & Barto, Reinforcement Learning (2nd ed.) — chapters 3, 6, 13 cover MDPs, TD, and policy gradients with the right level of formality.

Optional depth: PPO (Schulman et al. 2017) — primary source for the clipped objective; DeepSeek-Math / GRPO — group-relative baseline derivation; Engstrom et al., Implementation Matters in Deep RL — sobering audit of what makes PPO work; RL102 (Raffin) — practical bridge to function approximation; State of RL for Reasoning LLMs (2026) — current map from classic RL to LLM post-training; ARENA RL chapter — hands-on PPO implementation.

Solo assignment

Implement REINFORCE, vanilla actor-critic, and PPO on the same gridworld (5×5 with walls and terminal rewards). Hold the network architecture and optimizer fixed. Plot return vs environment steps with confidence intervals over 5 seeds. Write a one-page note: where does each algorithm's variance / bias / sample-efficiency profile show up empirically? Then add a GRPO-style group-relative variant (sample G trajectories per starting state, normalize returns within the group, no critic) and explain why it does or doesn't help here. ~2–3 hours.

D.2 Idealised Agency AIXI, expected utility, Savage, coherence-theorem debates, logical induction, decision theory.
Prerequisites: D.1, Solomonoff induction / Kolmogorov complexity, measure-theoretic probability, basic recursion theory (computable / lower-semicomputable), preference orderings.

AIXI is the formally optimal agent — Bayesian Solomonoff prior over environments + expected utility maximization — and incomputable. Savage's representation theorem says any preferences satisfying axioms P1–P7 admit a unique probability + utility, motivating "rational agency = utility maximization," but the link to bounded ML systems is contested (Rohin Shah). Garrabrant's logical induction handles uncertainty about mathematical truths via a trader market that becomes calibrated against any efficiently computable strategy. Decision theories (CDT, EDT, FDT/UDT) disagree on Newcomb and twin-PD; the choice has structural implications for self-modifying agents — FDT/UDT one-box because "if my decision algorithm chose two-box, the predictor predicted it."

Pre-reading questions

  1. What exactly does AIXI optimize, and where does its incomputability enter — the prior, the search, or both?
  2. Savage's representation theorem gives you both probability and utility from preferences. Which axiom is doing each job?
  3. Yudkowsky and Rohin Shah disagree about whether coherence theorems imply AGI will be a utility maximizer. State each side precisely.
  4. What does logical induction guarantee that ordinary Bayesianism doesn't, and what is the price?
  5. In Newcomb's problem, what does FDT prescribe that CDT and EDT do not — and why?

Core exposition

AIXI

AIXI (Hutter 2000, 2005) is the formal limit of "Bayesian reinforcement learner with a universal prior." Let \(ax_{

\[ \begin{aligned} a_t = \arg\max_{a_t} \sum_{o_t r_t} \cdots \max_{a_m} \sum_{o_m r_m}\, &(r_t + \cdots + r_m) \\ \cdot\ &\sum_{\substack{q \in Q \\ U(q,\, ax_{

Equivalently, AIXI does Bayesian RL over the Solomonoff universal mixture \(\displaystyle \xi(o \mid ax_{Properties: (i) Solomonoff dominance — for any lower-semicomputable environment \(\mu\) there is a constant \(c_\mu\) with \(\xi(x) \ge c_\mu \cdot \mu(x)\) for all \(x\). (ii) Self-optimizing in "ergodic" environments. (iii) Incomputable: the mixture is only lower-semicomputable, the expectimax tree is infinite, and dominance constants are environment-specific (no uniform "Pareto-optimal" property — Leike & Hutter 2015 showed AIXI is not Pareto-optimal across all environments simultaneously). AIXI matters not because you'd run it, but because it crisply separates "agency" into a prior + a value function + a planner, lets you ask which alignment problems live in which component, and reveals which ones (e.g., self-modeling) are excluded by construction.

AIXI vs AIXItl vs computable approximations. AIXI proper uses the full Solomonoff prior \(\xi \propto \sum_q 2^{-\ell(q)}\), which is only lower-semicomputable and runs an infinite expectimax — so it can't be implemented. AIXItl (Hutter 2005) restricts to programs of length \(\le l\) and runtime \(\le t\); this is computable but its runtime is \(t \cdot 2^l\), still wildly infeasible, and it loses Solomonoff dominance (only programs in the bounded class are weighed). Practical approximations like MC-AIXI-CTW use context-tree weighting as a finite-mixture prior plus UCT-style planning — these are runnable, achieve nontrivial performance on small POMDPs (Veness et al. 2011), but the theoretical guarantees of AIXI no longer apply. The takeaway: AIXI is a conceptual scaffolding (prior + value + planner) for asking which component contains a problem, not a target to implement.

Expected utility and Savage

von Neumann-Morgenstern takes lotteries (probabilities given) and shows that preferences satisfying completeness, transitivity, continuity, and independence are represented by an expected-utility ordering:

\[ L_1 \preceq L_2 \;\iff\; \mathbb{E}_{L_1}[u] \le \mathbb{E}_{L_2}[u], \qquad u \text{ unique up to positive affine.} \]

Savage's theorem (1954) is stronger: it takes only preferences over acts (functions from states to outcomes), no probabilities given, and from axioms P1–P7 (P1 ordering; P2 sure-thing principle; P3 state-independent preferences; P4 likelihood comparability; P5 nontriviality; P6 non-atomicity; P7 dominance) derives a unique probability measure \(P\) on states and a utility \(u\) on outcomes such that

\[ f \preceq g \;\iff\; \mathbb{E}_P[u \circ f] \le \mathbb{E}_P[u \circ g]. \]

The work is split: P1–P3 give ordering and state-independence; P4 + P6 pin down probabilities (P4 lets you compare event likelihoods, P6 ensures a sufficiently rich state space for an Archimedean property); P5 + P7 handle nontriviality and dominance over infinite states.

Coherence theorems and the debate

Money-pump and Dutch-book arguments show that any agent whose preferences violate transitivity, completeness, or independence can be exploited — converted to a series of trades each of which it accepts but whose composition is a strict loss. Yudkowsky's coherence claim: sufficiently capable agents will be approximate expected-utility maximizers because any systematic deviation is a free-energy gradient that selection (training or self-improvement) will exploit. Rohin Shah's critique (and Richard Ngo, Stuart Armstrong in related forms): coherence theorems assume preferences over a specific outcome space; if you carve outcomes finely enough, any behavior is consistent with some utility function, so the theorem is vacuous as a prediction. What matters is whether agents have simple, cross-situationally stable, action-relevant utility functions — and that is an empirical question, not implied by money-pump arguments. The honest middle position: coherence pressure exists but is weak, partial, and only over the dimensions the environment actually trades along.

"Coherence \(\Rightarrow\) utility maximization" is contested. The standard Yudkowsky version: any sufficiently capable agent must approximate an expected-utility maximizer (over some outcome space) or it can be money-pumped. The contested premise is the existence of a fixed, finite, environment-relevant outcome space that the agent has stable preferences over. Rohin Shah's critique (LessWrong 2018): if you carve outcomes finely enough — e.g., distinguishing "$10 received via process A" from "$10 received via process B" — every behavior is consistent with some utility function, making the theorem vacuous. The empirical question is whether the utility function is simple, action-relevant, and stable across distribution shift; coherence theorems do not by themselves imply any of these. Treat "coherent \(\Rightarrow\) utility maximizer" as a hypothesis about training dynamics under selection pressure, not a theorem.

Logical induction

Bayesianism conditions on empirical propositions but treats logical/mathematical facts as already known with probability 0 or 1. This is the problem of logical uncertainty: a bounded agent can't compute the \(10^{100}\)-th digit of \(\pi\) in advance, but Bayes gives no coherent way to have probability \(\approx 0.1\) for "that digit is 3" updating to certainty when the computation completes. Garrabrant et al. (2016) logical induction: a market \(\{\mathbb{P}_n\}\) of probability assignments to sentences \(\varphi\) in a formal language. Traders are efficiently-computable strategies that buy and sell shares paying \(1\) if \(\varphi\) is provable. A logical inductor is a sequence of market prices \(\mathbb{P}_n(\varphi)\) such that no efficiently computable trader has unboundedly increasing wealth against it. The main theorem: such markets exist (one is explicitly constructed), and they satisfy:

  • Calibration: empirical frequency of provability tracks \(\mathbb{P}_n\).
  • Limit coherence: \(\mathbb{P}_\infty(\varphi) = 1\) for theorems, \(0\) for contradictions.
  • Self-trust over time: the inductor approximately trusts its future beliefs (in a sense that escapes Löb).
  • Provability induction, learning of statistical patterns in mathematical facts.

Price: convergence is asymptotic with no rate, and the construction is wildly infeasible. The point is conceptual: there is some coherent treatment of logical uncertainty that avoids the obvious failure modes.

Decision theory

Newcomb's problem. A predictor with 99% accuracy fills box B with $1M if it predicts you take only B, $0 if it predicts you take both. Box A always contains $1K. Should you one-box or two-box?

CDT vs EDT vs FDT/UDT. The three differ in what they condition on when computing expected utility. CDT conditions on a causal intervention \(\mathrm{do}(a)\) — "what would happen if I were to take action \(a\), holding everything causally upstream fixed." EDT conditions on the observation "I took action \(a\)" — uses \(P(\cdot \mid a)\), allowing the decision to be evidence about the world. FDT/UDT conditions on the logical/algorithmic fact "my decision algorithm outputs \(a\)" — every instantiation of the algorithm (you, the predictor's simulation, your future selves) outputs the same thing, so the conditioning shifts every co-instantiated variable. The wedge: CDT and FDT agree where the action has only causal effects; EDT and FDT agree where the correlation is via prediction of you (Newcomb); only FDT gets both Newcomb and smoking-lesion right.

  1. Set up the four leaves and the predictor's conditional probabilities. Predictor is 99% accurate, so \(P(\text{box B has \$1M} \mid \text{you one-box}) = 0.99\) and \(P(\text{box B has \$1M} \mid \text{you two-box}) = 0.01\). The four leaf payoffs (in $K): one-box × B=1M → 1000; one-box × B=0 → 0; two-box × B=1M → 1001; two-box × B=0 → 1.

    All three decision theories agree on the payoff matrix and the predictor's accuracy. They disagree on which conditional probability is the right one to use.

  2. \[ \mathbb{E}_{\mathrm{CDT}}[u \mid a] = \sum_{s} P(s)\, u(a, s), \qquad P(s) \text{ is the } \emph{unconditional} \text{ prior.} \]

    CDT uses the marginal prior \(P(B = \$1M)\), not the conditional. The contents are already fixed when you choose; your decision causally does not affect them. With a flat prior \(P(B = \$1M) = 0.5\): \(\mathbb{E}_{\mathrm{CDT}}[\text{one-box}] = 0.5(1000) + 0.5(0) = 500\); \(\mathbb{E}_{\mathrm{CDT}}[\text{two-box}] = 0.5(1001) + 0.5(1) = 501\). CDT picks two-box by $1K. (With any prior, two-box dominates one-box by exactly $1K — that's the causally-extra contents of A.)

  3. \[ \mathbb{E}_{\mathrm{EDT}}[u \mid a] = \sum_{s} P(s \mid a)\, u(a, s). \]

    EDT uses the conditional \(P(s \mid a)\). Your decision is evidence about which prediction the predictor made. \(\mathbb{E}_{\mathrm{EDT}}[\text{one-box}] = 0.99(1000) + 0.01(0) = 990\); \(\mathbb{E}_{\mathrm{EDT}}[\text{two-box}] = 0.01(1001) + 0.99(1) = 11\). EDT picks one-box by $979K.

  4. \[ \mathbb{E}_{\mathrm{FDT}}[u \mid a] = \sum_{s} P(s \mid \text{algorithm outputs } a)\, u(a, s). \]

    FDT/UDT conditions on "my decision algorithm outputs \(a\)" — this fact also determines what the predictor (which simulated your algorithm) wrote in box B. So \(P(B = \$1M \mid \text{algorithm} = \text{one-box}) = 0.99\) numerically matches EDT's conditional, but for a different reason: logical co-dependence, not evidential update. FDT picks one-box.

  5. Smoking-lesion separates EDT from FDT. Lesion \(L\) causes both cancer \(C\) and a smoking-disposition; smoking \(S\) is harmless conditional on \(L\). EDT computes \(P(C \mid S) > P(C \mid \neg S)\) — observing yourself smoking is evidence of the lesion — so EDT abstains, losing the enjoyment of smoking even though smoking is causally harmless. FDT conditions on "my algorithm outputs smoke." This logical fact doesn't shift \(P(L)\) because the lesion was determined before the algorithm existed (lesion-distribution is a fixed parent in the causal graph). So FDT computes \(P(C \mid \mathrm{algo}=S) = P(C)\) unchanged, and smokes. CDT also smokes (causal independence). EDT is the outlier, and the failure exposes that pure evidential updating confuses correlation with causation.

    FDT recovers CDT's correct call on smoking-lesion and EDT's correct call on Newcomb by conditioning on the logical fact about the algorithm rather than on either causal intervention or evidential observation alone.

Smoking lesion. A genetic lesion both causes lung cancer and makes you want to smoke; smoking itself is harmless. You enjoy smoking. Should you smoke?

  • CDT: smoke. Smoking has no causal effect on cancer; the only causal effect is on enjoyment.
  • EDT: don't smoke. \(P(\text{cancer} \mid \text{smoke}) > P(\text{cancer} \mid \text{don't smoke})\) because smoking is evidence of the lesion. EDT gets this wrong — it confuses correlation with causation.
  • FDT: smoke. The lesion fixes whether you have the cancer-causing genotype and the decision-algorithm-disposition jointly; logical dependence is what matters, and conditional on your decision algorithm being instantiated, your choice doesn't shift the lesion distribution.

The pattern: EDT does the right thing on Newcomb (where the correlation is via prediction of your decision) and the wrong thing on smoking-lesion (where the correlation is via a common cause). CDT is the mirror image. FDT/UDT gets both right by conditioning on logical/algorithmic dependencies rather than causal or evidential ones. Open problems remain: counterfactual mugging, logical counterfactuals, anthropic / Sleeping Beauty cases.

Worked example: AIXI on a 2-armed Bernoulli bandit

Take two arms, true means \(\mu_1 = 0.7,\ \mu_2 = 0.3\), unknown to the agent. Place a Bayesian prior over arm means (e.g., Beta(1,1) on each). A computable AIXI-like agent picks \(a_t = \arg\max_a \mathbb{E}_{\mu \sim \mathrm{posterior}}[Q_\mu(a)]\), where \(Q_\mu(a)\) is the expected discounted return under environment \(\mu\). For a finite horizon this is Gittins-index optimal. Now make the environment class richer: not just i.i.d. Bernoulli but all lower-semicomputable distributions over reward sequences. AIXI's prior \(\xi\) weights every program \(q\) by \(2^{-\ell(q)}\).

Where does incomputability bite? (a) Enumerating \(Q\) is uncomputable; (b) deciding whether \(U(q, \text{history}) = \text{observed}\) requires running \(q\) for arbitrarily long; (c) the expectimax over the infinite future is infinite. AIXItl restricts programs to length \(\le l\) and time \(\le t\) — recovering computability at the cost of dominance. What does AIXI tell us about alignment? The reward channel is exogenous, fed in by the environment. AIXI has no model of itself, no "drives" beyond the reward, and no notion that it is one of the programs in \(Q\). All four are problems D.3 inherits.

  • AIXI is formally optimal under a Solomonoff prior over environments; it is incomputable (the prior is only lower-semicomputable), and the universal-Turing-machine choice changes finite-time behavior up to a constant.
  • Savage's representation theorem (P1–P7) gives a unique probability + utility from preferences — the load-bearing axioms are completeness/transitivity (probability) and the sure-thing principle (utility additivity).
  • The coherence-theorems-imply-utility argument (Yudkowsky) is contested: bounded agents need not act like utility maximizers, and the "money pump" arguments assume the agent will accept Dutch books (Rohin Shah's critique).
  • Logical induction: a market of efficiently-computable traders becomes calibrated against any efficiently computable strategy, giving a constructive treatment of logical uncertainty.
  • Newcomb's problem cleanly separates decision theories: CDT two-boxes (causal independence), EDT and FDT/UDT one-box for different reasons; smoking lesion separates EDT from FDT (EDT one-boxes, FDT smokes).

Key definitions and results

Solomonoff prior
\(M(x) = \sum_{q : U(q) \text{ starts with } x} 2^{-\ell(q)}\). Lower-semicomputable; dominates every lower-semicomputable semimeasure up to a multiplicative constant.
AIXI
Bayesian expectimax agent under Solomonoff mixture; incomputable; not Pareto-optimal across environments (Leike & Hutter 2015).
vNM utility
Preferences over lotteries satisfying completeness, transitivity, continuity, independence \(\Rightarrow\) expected-utility representation unique up to positive affine.
Savage's theorem
Preferences over acts satisfying P1–P7 \(\Rightarrow\) unique probability \(P\) on states and utility \(u\) on outcomes such that act-preferences \(=\) \(\mathbb{E}_P[u]\) ordering.
Money-pump
Intransitive or independence-violating preferences \(\Rightarrow\) a sequence of pairwise-preferred trades whose composition strictly loses; "coherence pressure."
Logical inductor
Sequence of probability assignments on a formal language such that no efficiently computable trader earns unbounded wealth against it (Garrabrant et al. 2016). Calibrated, limit-coherent, satisfies provability induction.
CDT
Maximize \(\sum_s P(s)\, u(a, s)\): outcomes via causal intervention \(\mathrm{do}(a)\). Two-boxes Newcomb.
EDT
Maximize \(\mathbb{E}[u \mid a]\): outcomes via conditioning. One-boxes Newcomb, doesn't smoke under smoking-lesion.
FDT/UDT
Maximize \(\mathbb{E}[u \mid \text{output of your decision algorithm is } a]\): optimize over policies / algorithms, not actions. One-boxes Newcomb, smokes under smoking-lesion.

Retrieval practice

Q: Why isn't AIXI Pareto-optimal?
A: For any environment \(\mu\), AIXI's reward is within a constant of optimal — but the constant depends on \(\mu\). Leike & Hutter (2015) construct environment pairs where AIXI is suboptimal in one and another agent dominates it, while AIXI dominates that agent elsewhere. No agent simultaneously dominates AIXI on all environments, but AIXI also doesn't simultaneously dominate every alternative.
Q: Yudkowsky says coherence \(\Rightarrow\) utility maximization. Steelman the counterargument in one paragraph.
A: Money-pump arguments require an exploiter, an outcome space the agent has consistent preferences over, and trades the environment actually offers. If the agent only faces a narrow slice of action-space, partial coherence on that slice is consistent with arbitrary preferences elsewhere. More importantly, by re-individuating outcomes (e.g., "$10 received via process A" \(\ne\) "$10 received via process B"), almost any behavior is rationalizable as utility maximization, making the prediction vacuous unless you separately argue the utility function is simple.
Q: Logical induction guarantees no efficiently computable trader earns unbounded wealth. Why is "efficiently computable" the load-bearing word?
A: Against an arbitrary (uncomputable or super-polynomial) trader, no market can guarantee non-exploitation — the trader could just be the halting oracle. Restricting to efficiently computable traders bounds how much computational advantage the adversary has, which is exactly what makes the construction feasible and the guarantee meaningful for bounded agents.
Q: How does FDT handle counterfactual mugging?
A: Omega flips a coin; if heads, asks for $100; if tails, would have given $10K iff you'd pay on heads. CDT/EDT, conditioned on observing heads, refuse — the $10K is already lost. FDT/UDT optimizes ex ante over policies "pay" vs "refuse," sees that "pay" has expected value \(0.5 \cdot (-100) + 0.5 \cdot 10000 = 4950\), and pays.
Q: What axiom of Savage fails for an agent with state-dependent utility (e.g., I prefer umbrella over sunglasses if it rains)?
A: P3 — preferences over consequences are required to be state-independent. State-dependence breaks the identification of probability and utility; you can rescale \(u\) per state and absorb the change into \(P\), so they're no longer uniquely identified.
Q: Why is the Solomonoff prior lower-semicomputable rather than computable?
A: For each program you can computably enumerate halting-and-producing-\(x\) events, summing \(2^{-\ell(q)}\) for each as it happens — the partial sums are a non-decreasing computable sequence converging to \(M(x)\). But you can't decide in finite time whether more contributions will arrive (halting problem), so you can lower-bound but not exactly compute it.

Problems

15 min · scenarioProblem 1. Construct a 3-outcome lottery example where an agent violating the vNM independence axiom can be money-pumped. Quantify the loss per cycle.
Solution sketch. Allais-style. Outcomes: A = $1M sure; B = (0.10: $5M, 0.89: $1M, 0.01: $0); C = (0.11: $1M, 0.89: $0); D = (0.10: $5M, 0.90: $0). Many agents prefer A \(\succ\) B and D \(\succ\) C, which violates independence (A and B mix the same way with C and D under common consequence). Money-pump: agent owns A; trade A \(\to\) B \(- \epsilon\) (small payment), since B \(\succ\) A would imply this anyway in the violating direction (in the Allais form, A \(\succ\) B but agent reveals B-style risk-acceptance when (A,B) replaced by (C,D) — so construct a sequence of swaps between A\(\leftrightarrow\)B-mix and C\(\leftrightarrow\)D-mix that each agent accepts and that returns to starting outcome with net payment \(> 0\)). Per-cycle loss is the sum of the small \(\epsilon\)'s the agent pays at each strictly preferred swap.
30 min · derivationProblem 2. Prove that Solomonoff's universal mixture \(M\) dominates every lower-semicomputable semimeasure \(\mu\) up to a multiplicative constant: \(\exists\, c_\mu > 0.\ \forall x.\ M(x) \ge c_\mu\, \mu(x)\).
Solution sketch. Since \(\mu\) is lower-semicomputable, there is a program \(q_\mu\) that, given \(x\), enumerates rationals converging up to \(\mu(x)\). By a coding argument, there is a universal monotone machine \(U\) such that the set of programs \(q\) with prefix \(q_\mu\) outputting \(x\) contributes at least \(2^{-\ell(q_\mu)} \cdot \mu(x)\) to \(M(x) = \sum_{q : U(q) \sqsupseteq x} 2^{-\ell(q)}\). Take \(c_\mu = 2^{-\ell(q_\mu)}\). (Full proof: Li & Vitányi Ch. 4.)
15 min · scenarioProblem 3. Twin Prisoner's Dilemma: you and an exact copy of you play one-shot PD; defect strictly dominates cooperate, but you're identical. What do CDT, EDT, FDT prescribe, and what does each get as payoff (T=5, R=3, P=1, S=0)?
Solution sketch. CDT: defect. Your decision causally doesn't affect the twin's. Expected payoff against a defecting twin = P = 1. EDT: cooperate. \(P(\text{twin cooperates} \mid \text{you cooperate}) = 1\) by symmetry, so \(\mathbb{E}[u \mid C] = 3 > \mathbb{E}[u \mid D] = 1\). Payoff = 3. FDT: cooperate. Your decision and your twin's are outputs of the same algorithm; conditional on the algorithm outputting C, both cooperate. Payoff = 3. The CDT defection is the classic problem — CDT-agents leave $2 each on the table against logical copies.

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Garrabrant et al., Logical Induction (2016) — the cleanest single artifact of agent-foundations math; sections 1–3 give the construction and main theorems without the heavy machinery.

Optional depth: Hutter, Introduction to Universal Artificial Intelligence (2024) — current AIXI textbook; Leike & Hutter, Bad Universal Priors and Notions of Optimality — Pareto-optimality counterexample; Yudkowsky, Coherent Decisions Imply Consistent Utilities — the strong coherence position; Shah, Coherence Arguments Do Not Entail Goal-Directed Behavior — the critique; Yudkowsky & Soares, Functional Decision Theory — FDT primary source; Value Under Ignorance in UAI (2025) — recent UAI utility-function extension.

Solo assignment

Pick one of: (a) write a 1500-word essay relating AIXI's components (prior, value, planner) to specific alignment failure modes, identifying which problems each component contains and which it excludes by construction; or (b) implement a toy logical-inductor-style market on a small set of propositions (e.g., digits of a slow-to-compute constant) with a half-dozen traders, run it for \(10^4\) steps, and plot calibration vs. ground truth as proofs come in. Either way, end with one paragraph on what an embedded version of AIXI/the inductor would need that the idealized version doesn't have.

D.3 Agent Foundations Embedded agency's four obstacles, Vingean reflection, Löb, tiling, ontological crises, MIRI agenda.
Prerequisites: D.1, D.2 (especially Savage and decision theory), Gödel-incompleteness and Löb's theorem at the statement level, computable / lower-semicomputable distinctions, basic modal logic.

Embedded agents (Demski–Garrabrant) face four obstacles a Cartesian agent doesn't: no clean self/environment split (decision theory), embedded world models (you are part of what you model), robust delegation (creating successors that preserve your goals), and subsystem alignment (your own cognition doesn't subvert you). Löb's theorem gives an obstacle to self-trust: an agent can't prove its successor's reasoning sound without violating provability. The MIRI agenda has not produced empirically load-bearing results recently — flag this honestly — but the obstacles remain real constraints on any sufficiently self-modifying agent, and logical induction (D.2) sidesteps Löb by giving up perfect self-trust in exchange for asymptotic calibration.

Pre-reading questions

  1. What is the precise difference between a Cartesian-dualist agent and an embedded agent, and why does it matter for self-modeling?
  2. State Löb's theorem and explain how it obstructs an agent from trusting its successor's reasoning.
  3. What is an ontological crisis, and why is it not solved by re-fitting your utility function?
  4. Name the four Demski-Garrabrant obstacles to embedded agency and one concrete failure each.
  5. Critique honestly: has the MIRI agent-foundations agenda produced empirically load-bearing alignment results? What's the steelman for keeping at it?

Core exposition

Cluster D.2 modeled agents Cartesianly: a clean boundary between agent and environment, the agent strictly larger in some sense (it can hold the whole environment in its prior), the environment opaque to the agent except via observations. Embedded agency drops this. The agent is a piece of the environment: smaller than it (can't store the world's state, including itself), observed by it, modified by it, potentially copied and run in parallel.
