Prerequisites: D-cluster mech interp (circuits, SAEs, activation patching), basic probability / measure of low-probability events, formal verification basics (Hoare logic level), E.2 backdoors for the threat-model context.

Frame interpretability claims on a falsifiability hierarchy: (i) adversarial behavior prediction, (ii) counterfactual edits, (iii) heuristic-argument-supported safety property, (iv) compact proof — increasing strength of evidence. No frontier-model interp work today reaches (iii) or (iv). ARC's heuristic-arguments program is the current attempt to close the gap by producing formally-correct-but-heuristic arguments about ML systems. Mechanistic anomaly detection — detect when the model is doing the "right" thing for the "wrong" mechanistic reason — reduces in worst case to backdoor detection, linking E.3 directly to E.2's cryptographic limits.

Pre-reading questions

  1. What would a "worst-case interpretability guarantee" actually say about a frontier model, and why don't we have any?
  2. What is the ARC heuristic-arguments program — formally and intuitively — and what does it want to replace?
  3. What is mechanistic anomaly detection trying to catch that behavioral evaluation cannot, and what does it assume about the model's mechanisms?
  4. What evidence would falsify the claim "we understand this circuit," in a sense strong enough to bet capability decisions on?
  5. Why do current faithfulness metrics fail to scale, and what's the structural reason — not just a "we need better metrics" reason?

Core exposition

The gap: heuristic interp vs. formal guarantees

Current mechanistic interpretability produces existence claims: "this circuit, on this distribution, mediates this behavior to within this measured faithfulness score." Three weaknesses make these claims insufficient for safety-relevant guarantees: (i) faithfulness is measured on a sampled input distribution, not worst-case inputs — the circuit could behave differently on a measure-zero adversarial set; (ii) the explanation is in human-natural-language terms whose mapping to the underlying computation is itself unverified; (iii) the explanation typically describes one path; in practice the model uses superposed and redundant pathways, so ablating "the" circuit changes behavior less than the explanation predicts.

A worst-case guarantee would say: "for all inputs \(x\) in some specified set \(\mathcal{X}\), the model's behavior on \(x\) satisfies property \(P\), with proof \(\pi\) we can verify." For frontier models we have none. The path from current evidence to such a guarantee is the open research problem.

Compact proofs and their limits

The cleanest existing attempt at proof-level interp: "Compact Proofs of Model Performance via Mechanistic Interpretability" (Gross et al., 2024) constructs verifiable proofs of accuracy lower bounds on small transformers trained on max-of-K tasks. The proof is compact — its length is sub-exponential in model dimension — and machine-checkable. The technique decomposes the model into circuits, bounds each circuit's contribution to logits, and aggregates.

The result is real but limited. (a) It scales as worst-case polynomial in \(n\) but the constants are tied to circuit decomposition quality; for frontier models the circuit decomposition itself is uncertain. (b) The proofs are about clean tasks with combinatorial structure; they do not yet exist for natural-language behaviors. (c) Compactness drops as the explanation becomes more faithful — there is a measured Pareto frontier where pushing faithfulness costs proof length exponentially, suggesting that for some behaviors no compact proof exists. This is the structural bottleneck: not "current proofs are bad" but "the right tradeoff curve may not include the regime we need."

ARC's heuristic-arguments program

ARC (Christiano et al.) proposes a strictly weaker but more applicable target. A heuristic argument is a structured object that produces estimates of probabilities about a system, treating some unknowns as if they were independent or uniformly distributed when there is no reason to believe a correlation exists. Heuristic arguments are not proofs — they can be wrong — but they have formal structure: they specify which independence assumptions are made, they can be checked for internal consistency, and they support a calculus of estimates.

The motivating example is the "no-coincidence principle." If a polynomial of low degree happens to vanish at \(n\) integer points, the heuristic argument that "low-degree polynomials don't have many roots" gives the right qualitative answer even though it's a heuristic. Translated to ML: if a model's output on an adversarial distribution happens to look harmless, a heuristic argument should be able to either explain why (and we trust it conditional on the explanation), or fail to explain it (and we treat the harmlessness as suspicious — a "coincidence" we should not bet on).

The program's bet: most safety-relevant claims do not require proofs, they require heuristic arguments structurally explainable enough that we can audit which assumptions they rest on. The program's open problem: formalizing "heuristic argument" generally enough to apply, and tightly enough to be checkable. The 2025 "Computational No-Coincidence Principle" line is the cleanest current formalization attempt; it remains research-stage, with no frontier-model application.

Mechanistic anomaly detection (MAD)

A complementary direction. The threat model: a model that does the right thing on the training and evaluation distributions but for the wrong mechanistic reason — e.g., a deceptively aligned model that behaves well because it's modeling the evaluation rather than because it has the intended values. Behavioral evaluation cannot catch this by construction (the behavior is correct). Mechanistic anomaly detection asks: can we detect that the mechanism producing the behavior on a deployment input is different from the mechanism that produced the behavior on the training inputs?

The formal setup: define a mechanism representation \(\mu(x)\) — e.g., the trace of activations or the sub-network responsible for the output — and a notion of distance \(d(\mu(x), \mu(x'))\). Flag \(x\) if \(\mu(x)\) is far from \(\{\mu(x_i)\}\) for training inputs \(x_i\) in a way that correlates with the prediction's reliability. Status: this works in toy settings (e.g., distinguishing trojan-triggered from clean inputs given knowledge of the trojan structure) and breaks at scale when the mechanism representation is itself learned and superposition makes \(\mu\) non-canonical.

The assumption MAD requires is that "wrong mechanism" leaves a detectable trace in the activation distribution. Goldwasser et al.'s construction is a counterexample in the limit: a cryptographic backdoor produces the right behavior on the natural distribution via the same circuits as a clean model, with the deviation triggered by a measure-zero input set the auditor cannot construct. MAD against worst-case adversaries reduces to detecting cryptographic backdoors, which we know is impossible under LWE. MAD against average-case "the model accidentally learned the wrong reason" is more tractable but its current status is empirical demos, not guarantees.

What would falsify "we understand this model"

A useful inversion: instead of asking what understanding means, ask what would falsify it. Candidate criteria, increasingly strict: (i) the explanation predicts behavior on a held-out adversarial set we constructed without using the explanation, and gets the predictions right at a calibrated rate. (ii) The explanation supports counterfactual edits: surgical modifications to the weights based on the explanation produce predicted changes in behavior, on adversarial inputs, with bounded side effects. (iii) The explanation admits a heuristic argument for a safety-relevant property that survives best-effort adversarial criticism, and we have priced in the heuristic-argument error rate. (iv) The explanation is part of a compact proof of a bounded behavioral property over a specified input class.

For frontier models we are between (i) and (ii) in narrow domains, and nowhere near (iii) or (iv). The honest status: there is no frontier model for which a safety-relevant behavioral property has been established with worst-case-style interpretability evidence at any strength comparable to (iii) or (iv). This is not a polemical claim — it is the field's own self-assessment in ARC's writings and AISI's safety-case sketches.

Worked example: heuristic argument for a max-of-K head

Setup: a one-layer attention model trained to output the max of \(K\) input numbers in \(\{0, 1, \ldots, V-1\}\). Gross et al. (2024) prove a compact accuracy bound. Here is the heuristic-argument version: model the attention pattern as "approximately uniform over the top-\(m\) values for some small \(m\)." This is a heuristic: it treats sub-leading attention weights as if they were independent noise. Under this heuristic, the output is correct with probability roughly \(1 - \exp(-c \cdot m)\) for some constant \(c\) tied to the value head's signal-to-noise ratio. The heuristic-argument calculus gives an estimate; the formal proof gives a bound. Comparing: the heuristic estimate matches the bound within a constant factor in this case, suggesting the heuristic argument is faithful for this circuit. Now apply the heuristic-argument framework to a larger model where no compact proof exists. The argument's outputs are estimates we can check internally for consistency; if a behaviorally-checkable prediction of the heuristic argument fails, that's evidence the argument has the wrong independence assumption, and we update which mechanism we think is operating. The heuristic argument is the formal substitute for "I think this is what's happening" — same epistemic role, much more auditable.

  • The falsifiability hierarchy: (i) adversarial behavior prediction, (ii) counterfactual edits, (iii) heuristic-argument-supported safety property, (iv) compact proof — increasing strength of evidence.
  • No frontier-model interp work today reaches (iii) or (iv). Current circuits/SAEs/probes sit between (i) and (ii). Closing the gap is the central open program of mech interp.
  • ARC's heuristic-arguments program: produce formally-correct-but-heuristic arguments about ML systems, with explicit independence assumptions that can be empirically checked.
  • Mechanistic anomaly detection: detect when the model is doing the "right" thing for the "wrong" mechanistic reason. In worst case (cryptographic backdoor from E.2), MAD reduces to backdoor detection and is provably defeated.
  • The framing matters: "we understand the model" is not a binary — it's a falsifiability level, and the right safety case is honest about which level its evidence reaches.

Key definitions and results

Worst-case interp guarantee
A statement of the form "for all inputs \(x \in \mathcal{X}\), the model's behavior on \(x\) satisfies \(P\), with verifiable proof \(\pi\)" — currently nonexistent for frontier models.
Compact proof (Gross et al. 2024)
A machine-checkable proof of a model behavioral property whose length is sub-exponential in model parameters; demonstrated for small max-of-K transformers, not yet for natural-language models.
Heuristic argument (ARC)
A structured object producing probability estimates about a system under explicit independence assumptions; weaker than a proof but auditable for internal consistency.
No-coincidence principle
The methodological claim that systematic non-random behavior must have a heuristic explanation; absence of such explanation is itself evidence the behavior is not what it appears.
Mechanistic anomaly detection (MAD)
Detecting that a model produced a correct output via a mechanism different from the mechanism active on training inputs; effective against accidental "wrong reasons," provably defeated by cryptographic backdoors.
Faithfulness metric
Quantification of how much an interpretation explains the model's behavior; current metrics fail under ablation choices, superposition, and worst-case inputs.
Surprise accounting
ARC-adjacent framework where an interpretation reduces our surprise at observing the model's behavior; "we understand" means cumulative surprise is bounded.
Falsifiability gradient
The hierarchy (i) adversarial prediction, (ii) counterfactual edits, (iii) heuristic-argument-supported safety property, (iv) compact proof — currently we sit between (i) and (ii) for frontier models in narrow domains.

Retrieval practice

Q: Why isn't average-case faithfulness sufficient for a safety guarantee?
A: Adversaries pick inputs from measure-zero or low-probability regions. Average-case faithfulness over the training distribution gives no bound on behavior in those regions. The Goldwasser et al. construction is the limit case: average behavior is indistinguishable from clean, but adversarial inputs produce arbitrary outputs.
Q: How does the ARC heuristic-arguments program differ from "trying harder to write proofs"?
A: It targets a different epistemic object. Proofs are conclusions valid under stated axioms. Heuristic arguments are estimates valid under stated independence assumptions, where the independence assumptions are explicitly false but useful. The program bets that for most safety-relevant claims, heuristic arguments are the strongest object you can hope for, and they're worth formalizing.
Q: Why does MAD reduce to backdoor detection in the worst case?
A: A cryptographic backdoor produces correct behavior on the natural distribution via the same circuits as a clean model; the adversarial behavior is triggered by a measure-zero input set producing activations indistinguishable from natural ones until the trigger fires. Detecting that the mechanism is "wrong" requires detecting the embedded signature-verification circuit, which is computationally infeasible under LWE/iO.
Q: What does it mean for an interpretation to fail (i) but pass (ii) in the falsifiability hierarchy?
A: (i) is adversarial behavior prediction — predicting the model's output on inputs designed to break the explanation. (ii) is counterfactual editing — making weight surgeries based on the explanation and getting predicted behavior changes. Passing (ii) without (i) is possible: an explanation could correctly identify a causal pathway you can edit, while still failing to predict outputs on inputs that route around that pathway through other circuits.
Q: What's the structural reason no compact proof exists for many behaviors?
A: Faithfulness and compactness trade off. A circuit explanation that captures all paths the model uses to produce a behavior, including superposed and redundant ones, grows in length with the number of relevant paths. For behaviors mediated by many superposed circuits, the explanation must either lose faithfulness or lose compactness; there is no third option.
Q: What's the status of the no-coincidence principle as a formal claim?
A: The 2025 "Computational No-Coincidence Principle" is a candidate formalization in a restricted setting (algorithmic problems with computable structure); it has not been applied to neural networks beyond toy examples. As a methodological principle it is widely used informally; as a theorem about frontier models it is open.
Q: If a circuit explanation passes adversarial prediction at 90% calibration, what does that license you to claim?
A: That the explanation is a useful predictive model in the tested adversarial regime. It does not license claims about untested regimes, worst-case inputs outside the test distribution, or cryptographic-adversary-constructed inputs. It is evidence of partial understanding, not a safety guarantee.

Problems

1–2 hr · openProblem 1 (scenario). A research team claims they have "understood" the refusal circuit in a frontier model: a specified attention head plus MLP route refusal-vs-comply decisions, and ablating them flips refusal behavior on 95% of held-out harmful prompts. Evaluate this claim against the falsifiability hierarchy (i)–(iv). What additional experiments would move the claim up the hierarchy, and what experiments would be needed to make a safety case turn on this understanding?
Solution sketch. The claim sits at (i) — they have adversarial-prediction-like evidence on held-out prompts, though "held out" is sampling-based, not worst-case. To move toward (ii): construct adversarial prompts using a method not based on the explanation (e.g., jailbreaks discovered by gradient methods against the full model) and check whether the identified circuit predicts which jailbreaks succeed; then check counterfactual edits — patch the circuit's outputs to "refuse" state on jailbreak inputs and see if refusal is restored without degrading capability. To approach (iii): produce a heuristic argument that the circuit is the only pathway for refusal, with explicit independence assumptions, and audit them. Failure mode: superposition almost certainly means other pathways exist; if ablating the circuit produces 95% behavior change, the missing 5% is either noise or a backup pathway. A safety case would require ruling out the backup pathway, either by demonstrating it's bounded in capability or by extending the explanation to cover it. Currently no frontier-model refusal claim survives a (iii)-level evaluation.
1–2 hr · openProblem 2 (proof-sketch). Construct a heuristic argument that a transformer of depth \(L\) and width \(d\) trained on next-token prediction over a finite vocabulary \(V\) has bounded mutual information between an early-layer activation \(h_\ell\) and a late-layer output token \(y\), given a specified intermediate token \(z\). Specify the independence assumptions and where they are likely false. What would a successful or failed empirical check of this argument tell you?
Solution sketch. Assumption: conditional on \(z\), the residual stream past layer \(\ell\) is approximately uniformly mixed across \(d\) dimensions, so any signal from \(h_\ell\) not captured by \(z\) is suppressed by a factor of \(1/d\) per layer. Argument: \[ I(h_\ell; y \mid z) \le C \cdot \left(\frac{1}{d}\right)^{L-\ell} \cdot \log |V| \] for some constant \(C\). The independence assumption — that residual-stream mixing is uniform — is false in the presence of feature superposition and skip-pathway circuits, both of which preserve specific directions through many layers. The argument is likely a substantial overestimate of suppression in worst case (real residual streams have low-rank structure surviving many layers). A successful empirical check (measuring \(I(h_\ell; y \mid z)\) via mutual-info estimators on real models) at the predicted scale would suggest the uniform-mixing assumption is approximately right on the tested distribution and license careful claims about information flow. A failed check would localize which residual directions violate uniform mixing and identify skip-pathway circuits that need explicit modeling — i.e., the failure is itself diagnostic, which is the point of having explicit independence assumptions.

Going deeper (optional)

If you read one thing: Formal Verification, Heuristic Explanations, and Surprise (ARC) — clearest framing of why proofs are too strong, demos too weak, and where heuristic arguments sit.

Optional depth: Compact Proofs of Model Performance via Mechanistic Interpretability (Gross et al. 2024) — the cleanest existing proof-level interp result and its limits; A Computational No-Coincidence Principle (2025) — current ARC formalization attempt; Transformer Circuit Faithfulness Metrics Are Not Robust — why current faithfulness measures fail and the structural reason; Formal Mechanistic Interpretability (2026) — circuit discovery with provable guarantees in restricted settings.

Solo assignment

Pick one mech-interp paper claiming to "understand" a behavior of a real (not toy) model. Locate the claim precisely in the falsifiability hierarchy (i)–(iv). Identify the strongest experiment in the paper, the strongest experiment not in the paper that would move the claim up the hierarchy, and write a 1-page heuristic argument — with explicit independence assumptions — for the safety-relevant property the paper is implicitly arguing for. Be honest about which assumptions you don't believe.

Quick reference card

The load-bearing formulas, distilled. Each card links back to the unit where it lives in context. Use this for last-mile recall before a meeting / interview / paper-reading; do not use it as a substitute for working through the units.

A.3 · RLHF / Bradley-Terry

\[ P(\tau_1 \succ \tau_0) = \sigma(R(\tau_1) - R(\tau_0)) \]
\[ \pi^*(a\mid s) \propto \pi_{\mathrm{ref}}(a\mid s)\,\exp(R_\theta(s,a)/\beta) \]

Reward identifiable only up to additive constants on connected components of the comparison graph. Partial observability of reward state biases learned reward with optimization pressure — the structural cause of sycophancy.

→ A.3

B.1 · Solomonoff dominance

\[ M(x) \ge 2^{-K(\mu)}\,\mu(x) \quad \text{for any computable }\mu \]

The universal mixture dominates every computable semimeasure up to a multiplicative constant depending only on the program length \(K(\mu)\). Optimal but incomputable — the platonic upper bound on prediction.

→ B.1

B.3 · Watanabe free-energy

\[ F_n = nL_n + \lambda \log n - (m-1)\log\log n + O_p(1) \]

The RLCT \(\lambda\) is the singular analogue of \(d/2\); \(\lambda \le d/2\) with equality iff regular. Posteriors concentrate around low-\(\lambda\), not just low-loss. LLC = finite-\(n\) estimate of \(\lambda\), traces stagewise development.

→ B.3

B.4 · NTK + edge of stability

\[ f(x;\theta_t) \approx f(x;\theta_0) + \nabla_\theta f(x;\theta_0)^\top (\theta_t - \theta_0) \]
\[ \eta\,\lambda_{\max}(H) \approx 2 \quad \text{(self-regulated)} \]

Lazy regime = kernel regression under fixed NTK. Rich regime = features change. Grokking and SLT phase transitions are likely the same phenomenon.

→ B.4

B.5 · Influence functions

\[ I_{\mathrm{up}}(z, z_{\mathrm{test}}) = -\nabla L(z_{\mathrm{test}})^\top\,\hat H^{-1}\,\nabla L(z) \]

Assumes strictly convex loss + critical \(\hat\theta\). Both fail for NNs: \(\hat H\) is rank-deficient (B.3), needs Tikhonov / EK-FAC; path-dependence broken — TracIn integrates along trajectory.

→ B.5

C.2 · Superposition + SAEs

\[ L_{\mathrm{SAE}}(x) = \| x - \hat x \|_2^2 + \lambda \, \| f(x) \|_1 \]

Features fit when \(s \lesssim 1/d\); \(\langle v_i, v_j\rangle \sim O(1/\sqrt d)\). IOI circuit: Duplicate/Previous Token \(\to\) S-Inhibition \(\to\) Name Movers (+ Backup, Negative). SAE features are partly L1-shaped artifacts.

→ C.2

C.3 · Causal states / \(\epsilon\)-machine

\[ C_\mu = H[S] \ge h_\mu \quad (C_\mu = 0 \iff \text{IID}) \]

Causal states = equivalence classes of pasts inducing same future distribution; unique minimal sufficient statistic. Transformers trained on HMM emissions learn the mixed-state-presentation geometry (Shai 2024).

→ C.3

C.4 · Natural abstractions

\[ I(X_i ; X_j \mid \Lambda) \approx 0,\quad H(\Lambda \mid X_i) \approx 0 \]

Mediation + observer-uniqueness. Connects to Conant-Ashby good-regulator + Tishby IB. Locatello impossibility: no disentanglement without inductive bias — abstractions are natural relative to a prior, not absolute.

→ C.4

D.1 · Policy gradient / PPO

\[ \nabla_\theta J = \mathbb{E}_\tau\!\left[\sum_t \nabla_\theta \log \pi_\theta(a_t\mid s_t)\,A^\pi(s_t,a_t)\right] \]
\[ \hat A_t^{\mathrm{GAE}} = \sum_{l \ge 0} (\gamma\lambda)^l \delta_{t+l} \]

PPO clipping = heuristic surrogate for trust region. GRPO = group-relative baseline, no value model. DPO closes RLHF in one step via \(\pi^* \propto \pi_{\mathrm{ref}}\exp(R/\beta)\).

→ D.1

D.2 · AIXI + decision theory

\[ a_t = \arg\max_{a_t}\!\!\sum_q\!\! 2^{-\ell(q)}\!\!\!\sum_o\!\!\big[U(q, ax_{

Solomonoff prior over environments, incomputable. Savage P1-P7 \(\Rightarrow\) unique probability + utility. CDT two-boxes Newcomb; EDT and FDT/UDT one-box (FDT via decision-algorithm logical correlation). Smoking lesion separates EDT from FDT.

→ D.2

D.3 · Löb's theorem

\[ T \vdash (\Box_T \varphi \to \varphi) \;\Longrightarrow\; T \vdash \varphi \]

An agent in theory \(T\) can't prove its successor's \(T\)-reasoning sound without trivializing \(T\). Escape: probabilistic trust (logical induction) sidesteps because Löb is about provability, not probability.

→ D.3

D.4 · Simulation lemma

\[ \|V_P^\pi - V_{\hat P}^\pi\|_\infty \le \frac{\gamma\,R_{\max}\,\epsilon}{(1-\gamma)^2} \]

Quadratic-in-horizon error compounding. Othello-GPT linear probes recover board state; causal interventions distinguish "uses representation" from "merely encodes." MuZero: composite loss without observation reconstruction.

→ D.4

E.1 · Debate guarantee

\[ \Pr[\text{honest wins}] \ge 1 - d\,\varepsilon \quad \text{(depth }d,\ \text{leaf reliability }1-\varepsilon\text{)} \]

\(\mathsf{DEBATE}_{\text{poly-judge}}^{\text{poly-depth}} \supseteq \mathsf{PSPACE}\), parallel to \(\mathsf{IP} = \mathsf{PSPACE}\). Obfuscated-arguments objection remains structurally open.

→ E.1

E.2 · Cryptographic backdoors

\[ h_{sk} \approx_c h \quad \text{under LWE (black-box) / iO (white-box)} \]

Goldwasser-Kim-Vaikuntanathan-Zamir 2022: detectability lower bound = LWE-breaking cost. Sleeper agents (Hubinger 2024): empirical version survives SFT/RLHF/adversarial training. CoT steganography: outcome rewards \(\Rightarrow\) hidden encoding.

→ E.2

E.3 · Falsifiability hierarchy

\[ \text{(i) adv. behavior} \to \text{(ii) edits} \to \text{(iii) heuristic arg} \to \text{(iv) proof} \]

No frontier model today reaches (iii) or (iv). ARC heuristic-arguments program attempts to close the gap. MAD reduces to backdoor detection in worst case — links E.3 to E.2's cryptographic limits.

→ E.3

Glossary

Every term defined across all 20 units, auto-aggregated into one searchable index. Use this when you need to look up a term mid-discussion or while reading external material.

Cross-cluster synthesis

The strongest prompts here force a specific testable position that spans clusters — they're not just "tie it together" essays. Honest curation: #2, #3, #5, #7 are the highest-leverage; spend an hour on one of those and you'll have something. #1 is useful for fellowship/application writing; #6 risks devolving into "impossibility-theorem shrug"; #8 is a generic capstone. Pick by what you'd actually defend.

1. The full training story for a frontier reasoning model

A.1A.2A.3D.1D.4

Pick a real frontier model class (e.g., a long-horizon reasoning assistant trained with RLHF + RLAIF + verifier-feedback RL). Write the training story: intended behavior; pretraining data and filtering; post-training stack (SFT, preference model, RL algorithm with the actual loss); deployment safeguards; the assumed inductive-bias argument for OOD generalization. Then identify the two assumptions you'd attack first as a red-teamer, citing specific failure mechanisms from A.3 (reward-model misspecification, partial observability of reward state) and D.1 (off-policy correction, reward hacking via exploitation of the value head).

2. Reconcile mech interp with SLT

B.3C.2C.4

Mech interp says: models compute via discrete circuits we can name. SLT says: the loss landscape is a stratified singular variety where what gets learned is determined by RLCT λ and basin geometry. These should be one story, not two. Write a 1–2 page essay: do circuits correspond to RLCT phase transitions during training? What would experimental evidence look like (cite developmental interpretability program from B.3 and IOI-style circuit discovery from C.2)? Where do the framings disagree about what counts as understanding?

3. Why the obfuscated-arguments objection is the same problem as worst-case interp

E.1E.3C.2

The obfuscated-arguments objection to debate (E.1) says: there exist arguments whose flaws are themselves too complex to verify. Worst-case interp (E.3) says: even with full model access, there exist representations we can't faithfully decode. Argue whether these are instances of one underlying hardness result. What complexity-theoretic statement (PSPACE? UP? cryptographic indistinguishability?) would unify them? What would falsify the unification?

4. Embedded agency, two ways

D.3C.3C.4

Agent foundations (D.3) treats embedded agency as a foundational problem: an agent inside its own world model. Comp mech (C.3) and natural-abstractions (C.4) suggest a constructive route: agents that learn the causal-state structure of their environment, including themselves. Is C.3/C.4 the empirical answer to D.3, or do the MIRI obstacles (Löb, tiling, robust delegation) survive even given perfect causal-state recovery? Take a position.

5. Sleeper agents vs data attribution: who wins?

E.2B.5A.2

Hubinger et al.'s sleeper-agents result (E.2) says training-time backdoors survive safety training. Influence functions and Datamodels (B.5) claim to attribute behaviors to training examples. If a sleeper agent is in your model, can B.5 methods find the trigger-inducing examples? Sketch the experiment, predict the result, and identify the assumption (linearity, single-trigger sparsity, etc.) on which the prediction depends. Then connect to A.2 pretraining/posttraining interventions: which intervention does B.5 most upgrade?

6. The "alignment target" choice as a social-choice problem

A.1A.3D.2

A.1 lists alignment targets (intent, constitution, CEV, corrigibility, societal alignment). A.3 connects RLHF to social choice (Conitzer et al.; axioms-for-alignment work). D.2 introduces coherence theorems and the AIXI ideal. Pick one alignment target. Show explicitly how it maps onto a social-choice rule (or argue it can't). Use Arrow / Gibbard-Satterthwaite style impossibility to identify which combination of properties any such target must give up.

7. What world models do that representations don't

B.2C.4D.4

Othello-GPT (D.4) and the Platonic-representation hypothesis (B.2) both claim LLMs build structured internal state. C.4 frames natural abstractions as low-dimensional sufficient statistics. Are these claims the same? If yes, predict an experiment where a transformer trained on raw HMM emissions develops representations isomorphic to the HMM's belief states — and state what the é-machine view of C.3 says should happen if the HMM is non-unifilar.

8. Final integrative essay (capstone)

all clusters

Write a 3–5 page essay: "What is the strongest current case that scalable alignment is achievable, and what is the strongest case that it is not?" Cite at least one result from each cluster (0–E). The point is not to pick a side; the point is to articulate which empirical findings would update you from one position toward the other. End with three predictions you'd be willing to bet on at >60% confidence about results that would appear in 2026–2028 papers.
